THE RED PAGE.
Heinrich Heine.
THE admirable selection from Heine’s pub-
lished works issued by Walter Scott is
indeed a monument to the memory of the
great little German Jew with the Gallic
temperament. Stigand’s two ponderous volumes
are now moss-grown and worm-eaten. Dr. Evans’
book sees too little of Heine ; his vision is obscured
by Anglican spectacles. At this late day we have
grown accustomed to regard Heine as a personality
altogether literary. We have lost sight of the
man’s wonderful political significance. The man
whom it was found necessary to banish could
have been no _ mean foe to the German
autocracy of his day and generation; and
Heine’s political significance was accentuated
by the fact that the French Government
was proud and pleased to have him on its books
as an honoured pensioner of the State. Heine was
himself keenly appreciative of his renown as an
apostle of freedom- He called himself, not once
or twice, a knight of the Holy Ghost. He was a
staunch battler for liberty of thought, and the
expression of freethought, the sort of man
that would be just as distasteful to a Maori-
land autocracy of to-day as he was to the German
bureaucracy of a dead century.
Between the years 1837 and 1840 Heine is
believed to have written some four volumes of
memoirs, fragments of which have been given to
the world through the medium of Dr. T. W.
Evans’ book, entitled “ Memoir of the Youth of
Heinrich Heine.” Heine, however, has made
admission that lie was obliged to destroy nearly
half of his autobiographical material in deference
to family considerations. But, admitting this to
be the case, there must still be a considerable
existing portion unpublished, probably stored
away in the Austrian archives, because to Austria
(Stigand, Heine’s biographer, asserts) Heine’s
wife sold them. Walter Scott’s careful selection
from the poet’s works throws considerable addi-
tional light on Heine’s youth, but tells little that
was not already well known concerning his early
manhood.
Heinrich Heine was born on the 13th of Decem-
ber, 1799—although a passage [in the “Baths of
Lucca,” might lead people who don’t understand
the Heine humour to suppose that he began life
with the 19th century. Francesca, the delightful
ballerina, asks abruptly, “How old are you?”
Heine replies, “ Signora, I was born on the morn-
ing of New Year’s Day, 1800.” “Ah,” said the
Marchese, I have always told you that you were
one of the first men of the century.”
He spent his full-blooded youth amid the
stirring scenes of the Napoleonic campaigns. Of
these, he has left us vivid pictures in the “Reise-
bilder.” In the year 1825, as a very young man,
Heine abjured the faith of his fathers in order to
obtain those social advantages denied him as a
Jew. He, embraced Lutheranism, and gave as a
characteristic reason that he did so in order to
prevent Monsieur de Rothschild treating him with
familiarity. The salient points in the meteoric
career of this brilliant German Jew are summarised
in his University residence, his quixotic marriage
with Mathilde Mirat (the beautiful and ignorant
grisette who knew no ABC save that of calcu-
lating courtezanship) his acceptance of a pension
from the French Government, his paralytic attack,
his “ mattress-grave,” and his tragic death in 1856.
Heine was a favourite of the fairies. To him
each little fay brought some gem of intellectual
endowment. But a place at the christening was
denied to the most malicious of the elfin sister-
hood. In revenge she threw a pinch of bitterness
or incompleteness into the gift of each of the more
generous. Heine was a poet, physically beautiful,
with all the graces and gifts that endear a man to
women, and he who might have wedded princesses
was scorned and spurned by a Dutch doll. Hand-
some as Apollo, he lingered for years
with features distorted and frame emaciated.
Lame, blind and paralytic, he trod the
marge of the grave with a hero’s tread. The
stings of poverty and sickness dogged the heels of
his life. His humor was of a good-natured
type, but it was acidulated with the cynical anti-
thesis which borrowed its smart from an adverse
experience. His wit ethereal was degraded by a
material alloy—an alloy of misanthropy and blas-
phemy against all human kind. What of ideal
there was of him lapsed into the indifference of
roturierism ; his pathos was only the obverse of
ribaldry; his humor was the chuckle from a
death-mask. But blame him, nobody can. He
was the victim of the Fates. Heine was a Pagan.
He had, like many of us, not even the cheating
illusion of a wronged God’s forgiveness. His
verse breathes forth the Hedonism that Grant
Allen made religion of—that verse which exhales
a charm, subtle as the perfume of the violet,
instinct with seductive potency, evanescent and
intangible. This German Jew with the Gallic
temperament united the romantic tenderness of
the Teuton, the religious sentiment of the Hebrew,
and the cynical philosophy of the Frenchman.
During youth, the romantic element of a complex
temperament prevailed. Then he figured as a poet
of the school of Novalis, Schiller and Fouque.
Then he wrote some of his most beautiful and
idealistic poems.
As a soug-writet, Heine has never been equalled
by any poet for tenderness of sentiment and deli-
cacy of touch. As a lyric poet, Heine stands
unique, alone. He 'reclothed the beautiful
legends of undine and lorelei. To the old
Teutonic mythology he gave a vivid reality and
an ideal beauty. VVitb a swarm of golden fancies
his love-lays teem as a hive of bees in summer-
time. Thus Leland Englishes a gem :
A pine-tree’s standing lonely,
In the North on a mountain’s brow, <
Nodding, with whitest cover,
Wrapped up by the ice and snow.
He’s dreaming of a palm-tree .
V Which, far in the morning land,
Lonely and silent sorrows
’Mid burning rocks and sand.
There are a hundred similar lyrics in the “ Reise-
bilder” and the “ Buch der Lieder.” Heine was
a fount Caatalian. inexhaustible. But not always
was lie the poet of pure sensuousness, as witness :
How wearily time Crawls along,
That hideous snail that hastens not—
While I, without the power to move,
Am ever fixed to one dull spot.
Upon my dreary chamber wall
No gleam of sunshine can I trace ;
I know that only for the grave
Shall I exchange this hopeless place
Perhaps already I am dead,
And these perhaps are phantoms vain
These motley phantasies that pass
At night through my disordered brain.
Perhaps with ancient heathen shapes,
Old faded gods, this brain is full
Who, for their most unholy rites,
Have chosen a dead poet’s skull.
And charming, frightful orgies hold, —
The mad-cap phantoms ! —all the night,
That in the morning this dead hand
About their revelries may write.
But Heine is at root the poet of dreamy tender-
ness. The skull and cross-bones intrude not
often. Thus could he sing in a sweeter mood: —
I love a fair flower, but I know not its name ;
Oh, sorrow and smart!
I look in each flower-cup—my luck is the same :
For I seek for a heart.
The flowers breathe their perfume—in evening’s red shine,
The nightingale trills,
I seek for a heart which is gentle as mine,
Which as tenderly thrills.
The nightingale sings, and I know what she says
In her beautiful song,
We both are love-weary and lorn in our lays,
And oh ! Sorrow is loner !
Daley, among all Austral poets, has Heinesque
characteristics. At least he has that splendid gift
of clean-cut expression. Our German, among
many other things, wrote this little gem:—
The graceful water-lily
Looks dreamily up from the lake,
And the moon looketb lovingly on her
For light love keeps fond hearts awake.
Then she bows her small head to the water
Ashamed those bright glances to meet,
And sees the poor, pale lily lovers
All lying in love at her feet.
Heine’s invocation to the Devil is not the least
effective of the lighter verse he has written.
I called the Devil, and—he came.
In blank amaze his form I scanned ;
He is not ugly, is not lame,
But a refined, accomplished man.
One in the very prime of life ;
At home in every Cabinet strife,
Who, as diplomatist, can tell
Church and state news extremely well.
He is somewhat pale, and no wonder either,
Since he studies Sanscrit and Hegel together.
His favorite poet is still Fouqui.
Of criticism he makes no mention ;
Since all such matters unworthy attention,
He leaves to his grandmother, HecatA
He praised my legal efforts, and said
That he also, when younger, some law' had read,
Remarking that friendship like mine would be
An acquisition, and bowed to me.
Then I asked him if we had not met before
At the Spanish Minister’s soiree?
And as I scanned his face once more,
I found I had known him for many a day.
Besides Charles G. Leland the best-known of
Heine’s English translators are Sir Theodore
Martin, Kate Freiligrath Kroeker, J. E. Wallis,
James Thomson, Alma Strettell, Francis Hueffer,
George MacDonald, A. Rogers, Ernest Radford,
W. H. Furness, Emma Lazarus, John Todhunter,
Edgar A. Bowring, Richard Garnett, Franklin
Johnson, Heinrich Herz, J. Snodgrass, A. Mac-
millan, and Julian Fane. Perhaps, however,
Heine is better known to English readers from
Matthew Arnold’s In Memoriam verses, entitled
“Heine’s Grave,” than by anything written by
himself. But the latest selection published by
Walter Scott should do > much towards banishing
the reproach of neglect ‘by us of this fine classic
and choice wit. Yet, notwithstanding the general
ignorance of Heine’s great body of writing, mots
and aphorisms of his have passed into the mintage
of all languages. Of Napoleon he said—
He was one who was haunted by the spectre of free-
dom as by the ghost of a murdered mother.
Of Lafayette—
He has fashioned for himself a better column than that
of the Place de Vendome, and a better statue than one of
metal or marble. Where is there any marble as pure as
his heart ? Where is there any metal so strong as the
truth of old Lafayette ?—Defender of the People against
the cunning of the Great!
Of Napoleon again—
Napoleon is dead, and modern Philistinism breathes
freely, as if released from a brilliant nightmare. Over
his tomb springs up the era of Industrialism—an era
which admires quite other" heroes ; as for example the
virtuous Lafayette or James 'VVatt.
He could deal a trenchant blow at his own deity,
Democracy—
The goddess of Liberty seldom possesses a chemise, and
is, on that account, very bitter against all who wear clean
linen.
Heine’s eloquence was of the dithyrambic type.
Thus he writes of the Bible—
What a book ! vast and wide as the world, rooted in
the abysses of Creation, and towering up beyond the blue
secrets cjf heaven ; sunrise and sunset, promise and fulfil-
ment, birth and death, the whole drama of Humanity, all
are in this book. It is the book of books— Biblion ! '
Always a sensualist, religion was to him an indul-
gence—
In my youth I was often overpowered by the infinite
sweetness, the mysterious and blessed influence, the
delirious death ecstasy of Catholicism.
But Heine could never be serious. Just before
his death on his mattress-grave, he is reported to
have said—
A religious reaction has set in upon me for some time.
God knows whether the morphine or the poultices have
anything to do with it. It is so. I believe again in a
personal God. To this we come when we are sick, sick
unto death and broken down. Do not make a crime of
it. If the German people accept the King of Prussia in
their need, why should I not accept a personal God ? My
friend, hear a great truth. When health is used up,
money used up also, and sound human senses used up,
Christianity begins.
And his last words, when asked if he prayed for
the forgiveness of God—
Eh, God will forgive me ! that’s His business.
Of his wife Mathilde, w-fio perfunctorily hursed
bim through the years of his paralysis, he could
say—
Of such are the angels, they do not discount their
future ; they have always ready money.
Of this same wife, however, a somewhat different
and indifferent story is told. She had a parrot
called “ Cocotte.” One day Heine believed that
his spouse had deserted him; but was hugely
reassured by hearing “Cocotte” squawking some-
where in the house. Mathilde might desert the
dying poet—the living parrot, never! He re-
marked with inimitable mockery:
Had I not wife and parrot, I would (God forgive me the
sin), like a Roman, put an end to my misery.
Again,—
It just comes out of the marrow of life, the doctors
may console me as they like ; I have nothing to expect
but a miserable malady, probably full of vicissitudes. The
last circumstance has its advantages. When one sud-
denly wakes up deaf, one forgets for a time that one has
already been blind ; and what is the use of it ? Just none.
At the most this passion-story can serve as an advertise-
ment for the complete edition of my works for the benefit
of Campe and my wife.
Besides the “ Reisebilder, ” or travel-picture-
book, and the “ Buch der Lieder” (“Book of
Songs ”), Heine wrote hundreds of newspaper
articles on philosophy, literature and art. In the
Revue des Deux Mondes he printed “Contributions
to the History of Religion and Poetry in Ger-
many”—a most noteworthy -volume. Heine died
quietly in the grey morning of the 17th February,
1856, and lies buried in the cemetery of Mont-
marte, his grave bearing only a head-piece,
inscribed with the two words, “Henri Heine.”
Matthew Arnold, in his noble dirge to the
memory of the great German Hebrew (“Heine’s
Grave”) writes: —
“ Henri Heine ” —’tis here !
The black tombstone, the name
Carved there —no more! and th° smooth,
Swarded alleys, the limes
Touch’d with yellow by hot
Summer, but under them still
In September’s bright afternoon
Shadow, and verdure, and cool!
Trim Montmarte ! the faint
Murmur of Paris outside : *
Crisp everlasting flowers.
Yellow and black, on the graves.
Half-blind, palsied, in pain,
Hither to come, from the streets'
Uproar, surely not loath
Wast thou, Heine !—to lie
Quiet! To ask for closed
Shutters, and darken’d room,
And cool drinks, and an eased
Posture, and opium, no more !
Hither to come, and to sleep
Under jibe wings of Renown.
F.J.B.
"The Compleat Spooner."
“ Monowai ” gives the Book of Spooner, from
Genesis to Revelations:
1. The Man Spooner : Dean of New College, Oxford. A
timid little albino of 65. Supposed to know something
about Herodotus.
2. The Rise of the Spoonerism : Every generation of
Oxford barbarians requires its butt. Spooner succeeded
Jowett. Jowett reported to have been much amused at
a recital of Spoonerism!. “ Fifteen years ago,” he piped,
“ they used all these about me.”
3. The Archetypal Spoonerism : Sp. gave out the
hymn, “Conquering kings their titles take,” as “Tin-
quering tongs their kitles kake,” and then nervously
altered to “ Kinqering kongs.”
4. From this alleged incident sprang the Spoonerism,
which may be divided into—
A. The Spoonerism of Action.
B. The Spoonerism of Speech. (Spoonerismus Vul-
garis.)
(a) Entering the robm in a violent hurry, the Rev. Sp.
threw his paste-board cap at the back of the Are, and
spat in his arm-chair. Better, still, he once put the
cat under his pillow and kicked his watch downstairs.
In undergraduate days Sp. found boiling his gold watch
and timing himself with an egg.
At a railway-station farewell the Rev. Sp. gave two-
pence to his wife, and kissed the porter.
(b) “ Here beginneth,” gave out Sp., in the grand new
college chapel, with Sir Joshua’s windows staining his
featureless face with unwonted colour, “ Here beginneth
the Apostle of the Epistle Paul.”
“ We' all know what it is, my friends,” he said, “to
have a half-warmed fish [sc., half-formed wish] in our
bosoms.”
Found wandering about at Greenwich in vacation look-
ing for the “ Dull Man ” inn, where, he told a sailor, he
had been asked to dine. “No sich pub, sir,” said the
sailor. “Dear me,” said Sp., “it must have been the
‘Green Man Inn’ at Dulwich, and I thought it was the
‘ Dull Man Inn’ at Greenwich.”
“ I must have walked to-day,” he said, “ ten miles as
the fly crows.” (Shamelessly appropriated by a recent
London Punch).
Asked, at dinner, what sweet he would have—“ I
think,” jocularly said the Rev. Sp., “ I will have some of
that stink puff.” (Sc.: pink stuff).
“Sorrow may endure for a joy,” he. once gave out for
text, “ but night cometh in the morning 1 ”
“ Excuse me,” he said once in chapel to a trespassing
undergrad., “ but I think you are occuplacing my pie.”
The Jowlerism is a more recondite form of humour than
the Spoonerism, and less apocryphal, Dr. Jowett (alias
Jowler) having a genuine fund .of, bitter wit.
The daughters of a well-known Australian Q.C. visited
Oxford, armed with an introduction to Jowett. They
called. He did not return the call for a month,
and then, the night before they departed, caught them
roasting chestnuts round the hearth. “ I’m sorry,” he
piped, stumbling into the half-lighted room,, “ not to have
come before. I have been exceptionally busy ... or
else 1 forgot . . . I think it must have been the latter ! ”
“ Very good thing,” he once squeaked, “ so many young
men from this college going to be missionaries. It ivill
teach them how much good there is in other people’s
religions.”
The nervous undergraduate yarn. Five-mile walk with
Jowett. “Fine day, sir.” No answer. (Two miles
walked.) “ Balliol seems likely to do well on the river
this year.” No answer. (Two more miles.) “Oxford
looks its prettiest in the autumn.” No answer. (Balliol
reached.) “ Well-er-er, Johnson, you have made three
exceedingly common-place remarks. Good evening.”
From Victor Hugo—
In the domain of art there can be no light without heat.
Civilization exhales art as a flower its perfume.
The higher the plane of thought the more does the
matter lose itself in mist.
J*
Rumored in London that Oscar Wilde is the
undiscovered author of the much-discussed
‘‘ Englishwoman’s Love Letters,” one of the two
most financially successful of last year’s publica-
tions. The probabilities are that the rumor is well
founded. The style of the letters is sufficiently
degenerate to pass as brilliant with the multitude,
and Wilde’s reputation for brilliant writing rests
entirely upon an undoubted faculty for unhealthy
literary distortion whose designed effect is to
apparel innocent ideas with an atmosphere of
ingenious evil.
ACHIEVEMENT.
Who says we fail? We prosper beyond dreams,
As architect of ruin we have no peer.
We thought to Are but farmsteads ; we have lit
A flame less transient in the hearts of men.
We are ill at building, yet have we at least
Destroyed to better purpose than we knew,
We have raised up heroes where we found but hinds.
We have ravaged well, our rapine is not vain.
Redder from our red hoofprints the wild rose
Of Freedom shall afresh hereafter spring,
And in our own despite are we the sires
Of Liberty, as night begets the day.
Sufficient claim to memory this I deem,
Title enow, were other passport none.
The Speaker.
WILLIAM WATSON.

THE RESULT OP
A TORPID LIVER.
Another Maitland Proof.
Th* Case of
Mr. W. COLEMAN.
(BY our reporter.)
To convey to the reader an impression of
the real importance of the subject which is
dealt with t elow in any other way than to
despatch a special reporter to the residence
of Mr. William Coleman, of Pitnacree road,
East Maitland, would be an impossi ility,
and when we get our information direct
from the author reliance can be placed on
its genuineness. Mr. Coleman began by
saying :
“I am a brickmaker, and when I com-
menced to suffer many years ago I thought
that the injurious elements connected with
my work were responsible for my health
breaking down. Such a strange feeing
came over me that I wondered what was
the matter. For a long time I felt out of
sorts, 1 eing languid and tired before I star-
ted work in the mornings, and with no am-
bition or interest in regard to anything I
had to do. In the hot weather I had no
energy at all, feeling so drowsy and de-
pr< ssed that 1 only wanted to lie down
quietly away from everybody. An aching
pain used to come to my brow in the early
part of the day, and stay there till I fell
asleep When I woke up again the same
distressing aches renewed their activity, and
in this way I was kept in a constant state
of misery.”
“ Were those the only discomfarts you
had to endure ? ” asked the writer.
“ If they had been the only ones I would
have considered myself very fortunate.
Worse luck, I had all sorts of agonies to
put up with, and I noticed that they were
always much worse after I had been eating.
My appetite was by no means good, and, of
course, I ate ut very sparingly; yet, if I had
overburdened my stomach with food, the
symptoms of indigestion could not have been
more severe. There was a t earing down
pain in my chest, as if something heavy and
indigesti le had stuck there, and as I drew
my breath I felt s if a weight had been
placed upon me.® I was troubled with 1 etch-
ing of wind, and also cramping pains in the
stomach, which used to swell out owing to
the quantity of gas which seemed to rise up
from my food. At times my legs and loins
ached so acutely that it was too much like
hard work to go for a walk, while an intense
pain in the back also induced me to stay in
doors, as it became quite unbearable if I
exerted myself in any way. If anything
occasioned me to stoop or bend my head I
instantly turned giddy, and sometimes my
sight was so bad that when I went out I was
somewhat bewildered by the clouded ap-
pearance of everything in front of me. Pal-
pitation of the heart was another matter
which brought anxiety to my mind, especi-
ally as I did not know how it was caused,
and I would have been much happier with-
out the sickly taste which was in my mouth
every morning. My nights became more
restless than ever, as I not only experienc d
greater difficulty in falling asleep, but what
little sleep I did get was made unrefreshing
by the presence of nasty dreams. Thus, I
was uneasy and troubled both night and
day, and in these circumstances it will not
surprise you to hear that I became strangely
affected with nervousness. My brain dwelt
upon th* most unpleasant subjects I could
think o • feme dreadful thing was going
to happen, according to my imaginations,
and no matter what was said or done I
never felt cheerful. The gradual escape of
my vitality weighed heavily upon my mind.
A coated tongue and the color of my skin
were constant reminders of the disease
which was making a wreck of me, for you
must know that I lost a good deal of weight,
besides getting very weak.”
“ You must have been suffering some con-
•iderable time ? ” r
“ Mj complaint hung on to mo for fully
two years, so that you will not think it was
extraordinary for me to feelasifall the bright-
ness had vanished from my life. Often I
stayed away from work because I was too
ill and feeble to do it, and how I would
have fared but for Clements Tonic I do
not like to imagine. Although my suffer-
ings were very great I did not go to a doc-
tor, and I think that I have reason to be
glad that I did not, as Clements Tonic was
evidently just what I required. fc The ex-
pense of getting medical advice was saved,
and so was I, for Clements Tonic made a
new man of me, and I heartily advise any-
body suffering from the same complaint as
mine to keep pounds in their packets by
using the remedy which cured me. The
first benefit I derived from the use of
Clements Tonic was the recovery of my
appetite. I surprised everybody with the
way I could eat when I had taken a couple
of Lotties of that medicine, and by-and-bye
I experienced wonderful relief from pain.
It is easy to account for that, as it all lay ii
the fact that my digesti.ve organs were bei,
beneficially operated upon by Clement.-
Tonic. My mind became cheerful again
when I discovered that I was on the right
track at last. • There was no uneasiness in
my chest after meals, neither was there any
pain or flatulence in the stomach. I was
more restful in my bed, and it was some-
thing that I greatly appreciated to be able
to sleep peacefully till morning. My head-
aches were soon ended by Clements Tonic,
and the pains in my 1 ack and shoulders were
also stopped. These were not the only
blessings which flowed from the bottles con-
taining Clements Tonic, as palpitation and
giddiness disappeared as well The ability
to regulate and strengthen my system in
every conceivable way was undeniably
possessed by the medicine I was taking, and
no words of mine would ever do full justice
to Clements Tonic for buil ing up my ner-
vous system in the splendid manner that it
did. Instead of feeling drowsy and half
dead I now felt bright and full of animation
and vitality. The wretched aches that hung
about my loins and lim s so long were en-
tirely swept away, and I must give Clements
Tonic credit for bestowing upon me such
powers of endurance that I could work
hard all day without feeling tired or dis-
tressed. '1 he flesh which had wasted away
from my body was recovered, and the altera-
tion in my complexion showed that my liver
affectious had been removed, and that
Clements Tonic had also purified my blood.
In fact, my health was sound in every de-
tail, and I think I would he doing a great
wrong to the general public if I withheld
these statements from them.”
“ Iu that case it will be necessary to pub-
lish them.”
“ I am quite willing that you should pub-
lish them in any way which will be most
serviceable.”
STATUTORY DECLARATION.
I, William Coleman, of Pitnacree-road, East
Maitland, in the State of New South Wales, Com-
monwealth of Australia, do solemnly and sin-
cerely declare that I have carefully read the
annexed consisting of four folios
and con i ively numbered from one to four, and
thit it contains and is a true and faithful account of
my illness and cure by Clements Tonic, and also con-
tains my full permission to publish in any way
my statements—which I give voluntarily, without
receiving any payment; and I make this solemn de-
claration conscientiously believing the same to be
true, and by virtue of the provisions of the Oaths
Act, 1900.
Subscribed and declared at East Maitland, this
twelfth day of November, one thousand nine hun-
dred and one.
PATRICK BOURKE, J.P.

“Sufferer from
Indigestion ”
*» Anything I ate caused me great
pain and distress ”
We often find that deranged digestion i 3
no more and no less than an indication of
low vitality. Enrich the blood with the
properties which renew the vital spark of
life, and dig stion will respond to the treat-
ment and become in a normal state again.
These facts speak for themselves :
“ 121, Great George’s Street,
“ Wigan, Lancashire, England,
“ December 31st, 1900.
“Dear Sirs,—For the past four years I
have been a great sufferer from indigestion,
and during that time have tried many
different remedies, but failed to find any-
thing that suited my condition. I suffered
from a poof appetite, and anything I ate,
no matter how simple, would not digest,
and caused me great pain and distress.
My condition got worse, and I became
seriously ill. My doctor advised me to take
Sour Scott’s Emulsion, and the same evening
e did so I read a testimonial in a
Manchester paper from a graduated male
nurse, stating his experience with Scott’s
Emulsion, and the many cases in which he
w. MANTLE.
(From a photograph.)
had found it had given good results. This
determined me to give the preparation a
trial, and before I had taken the contents of
one bottle I was so well satisfied with the
results that I made up my mind to give it a
fair test. I have found so much benefit
from the continued use of Scott’s Emulsion
that I now have a new relish for food, and
no longer feel any distress after my meals.
X am perfectly free from all the distressed
feelings I experienced before taking the
Emulsion, and can eat anything without
the least sign of deranging my digestion.—
Yours very truly, (Signed) W. Mantle.
There are properties in Scott’s Emulsion
which have a direct action upon the
digestive system. Hypophosphites of lime
and soda, for instance, stimulate the
appetite and assist digestion in many ways,
while cod-liver oil has a beneficial effect
upon the entire alimentary canal. The
most important work of Scott’s Emulsion
in the system, however, is in restoring
vitality, and giving that resistive force to
life which represents vigour and health. We
cannot say that Scott’s Emulsion will cure
a chronic case of dyspepsia, but we do know
that it is beneficial to the entire digestive
system, as is shown clearly in the case
which we have placed before you.
The consumptive, and all other anaemic
persons, find Scott’s Emulsion of great
advantage in giving them strength to over-
come the wasting conditions of the system.
When the blood is depleted, the whole
organism is reduced to a state of low vitality,
and there is no remedy that can be employed
that will be as effectual as Scott’s Emulsion.
Babies and children who do not thrive,
derive health and strength from its use.
It is a mistake to think that Scott’s
Emulsion is like other forms of cod-liver
oil. Scott’s Emulsion is palatable, and does
not dei ange digestion. If you will be care-
ful to purchase only the genuine preparation
and make a trial of it, you will soon learn
its superiority.
You can obtain a sample of Scott’s Emulsion
by sending fourpence to Scott & Bowne, Ltd.,
483 Kent street, Sydney, and mentioning this
paper. All chemists sell Scott’s Emulsion.

When the Wool Comes In I
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
’Tis a lazy life and languid round the railway-
stations when
No wool’s piled on the platforms or the sidings
lone and gray;
When the porter lies half-sleeping while the
“boss’s” rusty pen
Scratches dreamy notes to No One, in a tired-out,
sleepy way.
But, behold ! a change swings over when the first
wool-team comes in,
With its heaped-up bales roped tightly and its
dusty man of sin.
Men waken from their dreams
When the creaking bullock-teams
Signify to all and sundry that the Wool is
coming in !
O, the William-cans are loaded at the hostel ’cross
the road,
And the good old “Damns” and “Blankies”
soar across the smiling sky.
Things recover from their torpor and man’s
planetoid abode
Turns a pleasing face and bosom to the one-time
carping eye,
When the teams from Way-to-Blazes bring their
loading safely in—
O, the porter-men make merry and gird on a
cheerful grin;
For the Wool means lots of Beer,
And the earth is full of cheer
For the jaded railway loafers when the bales are
coming in.
The land is full of curses and they fall on it like
rain—
The cheerful objurgations of the Bringer of the
Wool;
And the skies are streaked with language as he
hurries back again
To fetch another burden, while we oram the goods-
sheds full.
O, the Wool, jolly Wool! When it comes a-orowd-
ing in
Our life is worth the living, and a pleasant breath
of Sin
Stirs the lazy, stagnant air,
And we break the back of Care,
And hang out Grief to dry, the time the Wool is
rolling in.
A.
GRANT HERVEY.
Dobbins on the Tip.
There’s five-and-forty navvies in the cutting on
the hill,
There’s five-and-forty navvies' in the gang;
You can hear the clay-clods falling in the dobbins
as they fill;
In the gravel you can hear the shovels clang.
For we’re working late and early, and the ganger
on the top
Has his eyes upon the sweating crowd below ;
If you’re spotted as a talker, your name will soon
be Walker!
For the shirkers from the workers lave to go.
Then it's lend your hand and lift her /
That's the way, my hoys, to shift her !
Heave it lively / or the boss will rear and rip :
“ Run that dobbin out 1 ” he’s bawling,
And to-day he'll stand no crawling,
For they're calling out for dobbins on the tip !
When the Summer sun is beating with a cruel
burning heat
On the “ heading ’’—still, that you may endure ;
For the roving breezes fan you, and the air is fresh
and sweet:
In the cutting it is fetid and impure.
You choke and gasp and stifle, and your breath
comes fiery hot—
And you long with fervent longing for the breeze,
That is ever, ever blowing, and far above you
flowing—
You hear it singing past you in the trees.
Hear the shovels clang and clatter I
See the stones roll doion the batter /
Dodge them as they hop and jump and skip !
Watch your chance and stand from under ;
Lose no time and make no blunder,
For they're calling out for dobbins on the tip I
When the dewdrops gleam and glisten in the early
rose-lit dawn,
And the Day-God sheds his first faint smile on
earth,
Little fairy flowerets laugh along the rock-strewn
lawn;
The bush birds sing their morning hymn of mirth.
The navvy boils his billy, and bolts his sodden
grub,
Then hastens to the chasm in the hill—
To his dreary, dismal toiling while his fevered
blood is boiling—
Oh, a glutton in a cutting gets his fill!
While on top—the ganger—hear him !
You curse him, though you fear him—
It's pay-day has our manhcod on the hip ;
There's your ticket if you grumble ;
So the rock you break and crumble,
And send it rolling outward to the tip l
The long day nears its ending, and westward sinks
the sun ;
The fitful, waning shadows come and go ;
We w«>ch the final dobbin jolting on the outward
WhmTwe ’re waiting for the welcome word “ Yo-
ho! ”
And when Life’s long day is over, and the last
“ Yo-ho ! ” is said,
And the navvy has for ever downed his tools—_
As he goes across the River, will his weary spirit
shiver, _
As he plunges in its cold and silent pools :
“ Dust to dust, and ash to ashes ! ”
Close the eyes and smooth the lashes —
See old Charon waiting yonder with his ship /
When the navvy goes down under,
Oh, I wonder ! oh, I wonder !
Will he hear them call for dobbins on the tip l
Q.

Notice to Subscribers in Victoria, S. Australia,
Westralia and Maoriland.
In consequence of the imposition of One Halfpenny
postage on Interprovincial Neivspapers, the annual sub-
scription outside N.S. Wales (except Queensland) is now
■EI, with 2s. 2d. added for postage.
No newspaper can be sent unless the subscription is
prepaid. There will be no exception to this rule.
To Subscribers in Queensland.
Owing to the imposition of\\d. postage, the subscription
to Tiie ' Bulletin to Queensland will be £1 6s. 6d. per
annum, payable in advance.
To ease off in some degree the burden of the unreasonable
postage —l id. on a Bulletin of ordinary weight—now
exacted by the Queensland Post Office, a special Thynne-
paper edition of The Bulletin is now sent to Queensland.

By sending direct to The Bulletin Office Twenty
Shillings sterling—with postage added where required—
which you may pay by Post Office Order, Postal Note,
One Pound Note, or stamps of any Australian province,
you can have The Bulletin posted regularly to you every
week for a year.

to be addressed to William Maoleod, managing director.

Special Notice.
The whole contents of this paper are copyright by the
publishers. The right to reproduce or translate any
article, tale, sketch, poem, paragraph, illustration, or
any other part of the contents of this number, is hereby
expressly reserved by the publishers.
The Editor will carefully read and, where practicable,
acknowledge in the “ Correspondence” column all contri-
butions submitted, whether in the form of Political,
Social, or other Articles, Verse, Short Tales or Sketches
(those dealing with Australian subjects, and not exceed-
ing two columns in length, or, say, 8000 words, are speci-
ally acceptable), Paragraphs•, Letters, or Newspaper
clippings. All communications will be regarded as
strictly confidential.
The Bulletin will return all unsuitable MS. when the
address is specified and stamps are enclosed. But we
desire those sending MS. to distinctly understand that we
are not responsible for its preservation or transmission.
To Amateurs in lilacJc and White.
Anyone supplying to The Bulletin original humorous
or other topical drawings in line or wash, of sufficient
merit and interest and adapted for direct reproduction, or
any interesting photograph illustrative of a current event,
will be paid .for same on acceptance. Any picture con-
taining a good idea, but unfitted for direct reproduction
. — i.e., any picture which has to be re-drawn —will be paid
for as a suggestion only. The conditions under which
such artistic contributions will be received are those
applying to MS.

SATURDAY. APRIL 26, 1902.
South Australia and its Jenkins.
Early next month South Australia goes to
the poll to consider how one of the most lost
States in Australasia may best be saved.
Premier Jenkins and Tory leader Darling
are the loudest applicants for the billet of
saviour. There is a stupendous contract for
the man who gets the appointment, and if
the appointed person really does the work
his salary should be raised to £60,000 a year,
with a pension upon retirement. He would
be worth the money. S.A. politics have for
some time been drifting back to the old,
indefinite condition from which Kingston
extricated them. Before Kingston’s day
one leader was so much like another, as a
rule, that it hardly mattered who was in
office. There were few party distinctions,
few big political battle-cries, and generally
little public interest in politics. Spectral
Ministries fled across the stage like ghosts—
Kingston’s made the 40th in 36 years. His
Cabinet existed for 6j years —an unprece-
dented event in S. A. history—and then the
procession of shadows and dreams resumed,
and in 2| years Holder, Solomon and Jen-
kins have successively stampeded athwart the
political field of view. InKiNGSTON’stimeS. A.
had a strong, definite democratic policy ; it
had none for a long time before him, and it has
had none since. Kingston supplied it with
a State Land Bank, he increased the land and
income tax, laid a special graduated tax on
large estates, imposed an absentee-tax, and
otherwise did his best to break up the
ruinous land monopoly ; reduced the loan
profligacy to the lowest limit it has reached
in S.A. for many years, and almost made
ends meet—in six years his total shortage
was £94,000. Now that the big man is gone
the very small men are scuffling again on the
steps of the empty Treasury.
The man who sets to work to save a
country needs to consider first of all how
far, and in what respects, the country is lost.
Here are the principal features as regards
the lostness of S.A. :
It is a country seemingly almost devoid of minerals, or
rather one whose scanty mineral resources have been
almost worked out. Even Tasmania, a p ace which is a
mere speck on the map by comparison, produces much
more than three times its output.
Its want of coal makes the manufacturing outlook very
unpromising.
It lives very largely on agriculture, but it is the driest
of all the States, and the most barren. It has just
scraped together another 54 bushel harvest; it is always
granting seed-wheat to its distressed farmers ; there is a
movement on for a large exodus of its impoverished Ger-
man agriculturists to Queensland, and the area under
cultivation this year is a trifle less than it was in 1884 —18
years ago.
A larger proportion of its farmers are tenants, and
kept down by the rent incubus, than is the case in any
other State, though this evil has diminished somewhat in
recent years.
At June, 1901, it had, in proportion to population, the
heaviest debt in this world, and though Queensland may
have got ahead of it since then it still holds a good second
place. It owes £69 16s. 4d. per head—practically £2BO
for a family of four or £419 for a family of six. Think of
a family like the Schippans owing £419 ! It owes over
£6,000,000 (i 16 16s. 4d. per head, or over £67 for a
family of four) which is represented by no assets what-
ever, and this item alone amounts to more than the total
debt in the great majority of European countries.
It has had ten deficits in 19 years, and has added the
bulk of them (not quite £1,000,000) to its permanent
debt.
Its railways depend for their profit mainly on the
traffic with Broken Hill. Broken Hill is now in a bad
way, and so S.A. is in a bad way. When Broken Hill is
finished and worn out, unless some special Providence
comes to S.A.’s assistance, it will be in a much worse way.
The value of its wool exports in 1900 was not much
more than half, and of its wheat and flour exports about
one-third the figures for 1884.
Its land revenue, which was £604,745 twenty years ago,
is now £151,888 and is still falling.
It had, a few years ago, four fire insurance companies
which are dead; two life assurance offices which are
dead ; and four banks, three of which are dead. It once
owned the second largest fire office in the Commonwealth
States, and among its dead banks is the great institution
which was named after the State and which dated
practically from the State’s first beginnings. These cir-
cumstances not only mean a loss to the State through the
profits of these businesses going abroad ; they are also
signs of decadence.
* * * *
A big man is wanted to undertake the
saving of a country in this condition. The
position of'Queensland and Tasmania is -bad
enough, yet a mere Jenkins, if he had
courage and good intentions, might save
them. They have resources which S.A.
doesn’t possess, and only the inconceivable
mismanagement and cowardice of their
rulers have brought them to their present
pass. But somebody bigger than a Jenkins
or a Darling, even if they had the best in-
tentions instead of the worst, is required to
drag S.A. out of its troubles. It hasn’t been
doing any very violent financial drunks of
late years—in fact, it is now, barring Vic-
toria, the smallest borrower in proportion to
population in the Commonwealth. But it
can’t stand even that addition to its burdens.
It shattered its mind by an appalling
razzle nearly 20 years ago—for the single
year 1881 its loan expenditure ran to over
£6 per inhabitant, and it didn’t begin to
sober up for ten years after that; and it never
had the constitution of some of its neighbors,
so it became a debilitated old person at a
very early age. The gold and silver finds
which pulled some of the other States
through at times of crisis didn’t happen its
way. Not only a great part of its railway
earnings, but about half its commerce, de-
pend on that temporary institution, Broken
Hill. It has done remarkably well in
manufactures, considering its disadvantages,
but still its industries only employ some
16,000 hands, and precarious Broken Hill is
one of their supports also. There have been
a remarkable number of rich men in S.A.
considering its lack of resources, but they
made their wealth in the days when there
were land booms and copper booms and wool
booms and loan booms. Now there is noth-
ing to do except to grow low-priced wool and
produce on the driest and least productive
soil in Australasia, and run manufacturing
industries without a local coal supply, and
to be the commercial outlet of Broken Hill,
which will some day become a rabbit-haunted
wilderness again.
• * • *
Darling, the boss Tory of S.A., has a
theory that State Governments should be
abolished, and the Federal Parliament made
sole ruler of Australia. That might save a
little money, but it wouldn’t make S.A. any
less barren, less devoid of minerals and the
sinews of manufacturing industry, or less
indebted. Jenkins doesn’t appear to have
even that amount of theory. And both
Darling and Jenkins agree that the
community should trust to the great
non - existent resources of its dusty
land, and treat all hostile comments with
contempt, as the work of a public
enemy. That is what all Australia has
been doing for a long while, and it is
what every country which went broke did till
the breakage arrived. S.A., with no debt,
or with a debt of, perhaps, £lO per inhabi-
tant, might be as solid as New South Wales
or Victoria, with a debt of £5O per head, the
affairs of the three States being equally well
administered. When it staggers under a
heavier burden than either of them, its con-
dition is lamentable. It can’t be saved by
any big heroic schemes. No immense
financier and vast political bounder of the
Rhodes type would do it any good, for there
is nothing to annex or to steak What is re-
quired as its ruler is an enlightened and pro-
gressive miser, with a capacity for cheese-
paring which goes even beyond genius. It
should abolish all borrowing; one of the
poorest countries going, with, in proportion
to population, the second biggest debt on
earth, can t afford to get into any further
trouble. It should cut down its Governor’s
salary to £5OO a year, and, it being impossi-
ble to get one at the money, let some retired
Judge wield the rubber-stamp of office with
the above amount as an addition to his pen-
sion. It should abolish its Legislative
Council, which costs a free railway-pass
and £2OO annually per member, also some
expense for gas, upholstery, and reporting.
It should try even now to recover its
Adelaide Tramway rights from Snow, the
syndicate man ; an investment which pro-
mises to yield even £lO,OOO a year over and
above interest on its cost is one which the
country can’t afford to lose. It should found
a State life assurance office and a State fire
insurance department in order to keep in
the country as much as possible of the
profits which now go abroad under these two
headings ; and cease hankering after an
Outer Harbor for the present; and set aside
£500,000 a year out of revenue for public
works, even if it is necessary, in order to
raise the money, to further increase the in-
come tax, and double the absentee tax,
and levy more taxation on that absentee
corporation, the S.A. Co., and make the
totalisator a Government monopoly, and
run a State lottery, and charge a
license fee on all shopkeepers, and establish
a State drink monopoly, and increase the
minimum fine for being drunk to £l, and do
other energetic things. It should do all that
is possible to create new local industries, and
cease sending to the Federal Parliament
foolish persons like Glynn, who clamor for
the destruction of the customs revenue in
order that French and German manu-
facturers may thrive at this country’s
expense. Then in a dozen or twenty years,
with the growth of population and no
corresponding increase of debt, the burden
which is now nearly £7O a head might be
only £5O, and later on only £3O, and with
this diminution S.A. might become a reason-
ably prosperous place.
* * * *
But as Jenkins Iscariot is only a show-
man with a gift of showman’s patter, and
the other loud applicant for the Premier’s
position is merely a dull, stodgy, utterly
unprogressive representative of the old
Tory crowd whose boom policy was
largely responsible for all S.A.’s troubles,
the State had better have nothing to do with
either of them. There remains the Labor
party, which most closely represents the old
Kingston policy, and the Kingston policy
represented the nearest approach to sober,
sane, and solvent administration South
Australia has known for very many years.
Whether the Labor party without Kingston
would, be a success is a matter for specula-
tion, but the country knows that the party
with which Darling is associated is not a
success ; it knows that Jenkins Iscariot is
a failure; and to continue ringing the
changes on two failures is mere foolishness.
The country declared in the main for the
Kingston policy, and it has never gone back
on that decision. Jenkins has abandoned
the Kingston programme in every impor-
tant detail, and it is time for the country to
abandon Jenkins. There is a legend about
the inadvisability of changing horses while
crossing a stream, but when the horse is
practically dead and remains all the time in
the middle of the stream without getting any
further he isn’t crossing the stream within
the meaning of the Act. Moreover, the
only time this country can change is while
crossing a stream; it is Australia’s mis-
fortune to be doing that all the year round.
Yet even the most promising party in
present S.A. politics is a sorrowful dis-
appointment, so far as some features of its
programme are concerned. At a time when
S.A.’s present expenditure is far ahead of
the revenue, it proposes to add to the
outgoings £lOO,OfJO or more per annum
for old age pensions, and to increase
the wages paid on Government con-
tracts, and it doesn’t make the slightest
suggestion as to how it proposes to raise the
money. No party has a right to talk of old
age pensions or a minimum wage on Govern-
ment contracts in S.A. just now, unless it
has a plan for raising £500,000 a year more
income, or is prepared to take the responsi-
bility of advocating the repudiation of the
public liabilities.
* * • •
Still the Labor party, which proposes to
pay old age pensions without providing the
money, is far more attractive than the other
parties, which went murdering Boers without
providing the money, and the best thing
S.A. can do is to give the Labor party its
solid vote, and wait to see what will happen.
Its financial policy is at least no worse than
that of the hopeless Darling and the hope-
less Jenkins Iscariot ; and the rest of its
policy is immeasurably better. In fact, it is
the only S.A. party which has a policy at
the present time.
PLAIN ENGLISH.
The Stricken West.
All the West of N.S.W. is naked and vermin-
infested. The rabbits are worse than scab in
sheep or locusts amongst green vines—but, even if
the rabbits were exterminated, the ghastly naked-
ness of the land would only become more
assertively plain. It is the bald, bleached skull of
a desert which now extends from the slopes of the
main divide to the coastal lands of the West. We
have worn it down so by the attrition of our system
of occupation. We have “rung,” burned, lopped,
thrashed down all sorts of eucalypti and casuarina,
myall, and mulga, boree and kooba. Sheep have
eaten the salt and cotton-bush off the surface, and
the rabbits have gnawed the crowns of their roots
out of the ground. Blue Mitchell and kangaroo
grasses are extinct over thousands of square miles,
and the wild-oat, carrot and barley-grass will
spring no more. What is to be done with such a
land ? Kill off the rabbits ? Certainly. Turn
on the water? Yes, every cubic foot avail-
able. But, what then? The squatters’ sheep
and axes _ have destroyed all the native
growths which would afford sustenance to prob-
able stock, and no permanent good can be done
till they are by some means restored. It is a big
question this—bigger, so far as the real desolate
West is concerned, than that of water-supply. A
vast area of N.S.W. territory is of no more use to
the squatter now than is a cut-out forest to a
timber-getter, and no use will be got out of it till
a work akin to that of re-forestation is undertaken
in a practical way. Nobody in Australia has
really thought about such a work as yet, thougli a
good many people in the Western States of
America have gone far beyond thinking of the
value of our saltbush. They have imported hun-
dreds of bushels of seed ; they have thousands of
acres of flourishing saltbush plantations ; whereas,
in Australia, we have not a 100-acre patch, and
are not likely to under present circumstances.
Yet there is no insuperable difficulty about the
matter. If the Australian doesn’t know enough
to cultivate his own native vegetable, who should
know?
The Latest O’Sullivanism.
The N.S.W. O’Sullivan has appropriately inter-
rupted a discussion on a “Folly Point ” tramway
to write to Earl Rosebery, one of the trustees
under the will of the late Cecil Rhodes, asking
him to give a matter of £IOO,OOO or so, out of the
dead millionaire’s fishily-acquired wealth, for
raising a statue of “ Australia Facing the Dawn ”
on Pinchgut Island, Sydney Harbor (the O’Sul-
livan avoids mentioning Pinchgut, by the way).
This, he points out, would be an appropriate
means of glorifying Australian Federation (and
the O’Sullivan), and incidentally
would be a graceful recognition of the valuable service
rendered to the British Empire at a severe crisis in its
history by Australasian troops in South Africa.
Unfortunately, the emphatic answer that such ?
request deserves won’t come from Rosebery.
The O’Sullivan transparently prigged his “ Aus
tralia Facing the Dawn ” idea from Bartholdi’s
statue of “Liberty Facing the World” in New
York harbor, and sorrily degraded it in the
prigging. The erection of the Liberty Statue by
one free nation, on the shores of the land of
another which it had assisted to Freedom, was a
noble and poetic idea. To raise, on the “ Pinch-
fut” rock, soaked in the blood and tears of
unger-tortured convicts, a memorial to record
how Australia assisted to strangle the liberty of
another free people—and to raise it out of the
dishonestly-acquired, blood-stained wealth of a
Rhodes —that is a damnable plagiarism of a great
thought. It is not likely that Rosebery (who
is a son-in-law of London Stock Exchange) will
point all this out to Mr. O’Sullivan, for Rose-
bery’s is an essentially politic mind ; but it can
hardly help occurring to him.
Reproductive Works which Produce
Nothing.
Two curious extracts from last week’s papers
£4,000,000 was borrowed on the distinct understand-
ing that the money was to be used for the purchase of
reproductive property. We were assured that it added
nothing to the real indebtedness of the country as the
interest would be paid out of returns, thereby leaving
the taxpayers unburdened. —Sydney Telegram! leading
article.
Of course it goes without saying that loan expenditure
employed on reproductive works is no burden.—Mel-
bourne Age leading article.
The Bulletin begs to file its caveat against that
last proposition, which is another of the comfort-
ing delusions of the borrowing disease. Money
borrowed for reproductive works may ruin a
country just as money borrowed for unproductive
works does. The fact that the Australian rail-
ways earned last year about £4,000,000 in the
country doesn’t prove that Australia can afford to
send every year £4,000,000 interest on railway
loans out of the country. The buildings of Syd-
ney and its suburbs and of N.S.W. generally are
reproductive works and return good rents, but if
they, as well as the railways, had all been built
with borrowed money N.S.W. would have to
send away in interest about £12,000,000 a year,
and it would go bankrupt through borrowing for
reproductive works. Of course about that time
the works would cease to be reproductive, but
that hardly meets the case. Works are repro-
ductive because the people have monev with
which to pay for the use of them, and do pay for
the use of them. The fact that they have that
money is largely owing to the floating of new loans
to replenish the stock of cash which would other-
wise disappear in payment of foreign interest. In
other words, the Government borrows money to
build reproductive works. Then it borrows more
money to keep these works reproductive. Plenty
of works that are reproductive while the State is
borrowing cease to be so when the borrowings
diminish. Victoria’s railways, which yielded as
much as £4 3s. 7d. per cent, and paid their way
in a year of great borrowing, dropped to a humble
return of £2 4s. lOd. per cent., and became a
heavy charge on the taxpayer in a year when
hardly any loans were floated, and if there had
been no loans at all they might have fallen lower.
The Dead Democrat.
In view of the visit to N.S.W. of the Premier of
Maoriland (Mr. Seddon), the council of the British Empire
League of Australia will hold a special meeting at four
o’clock this afternoon at the School of Arts, the object
being to take steps to suitably recognise the eminent
services rendered by Mr. Seddon to the Empire.—S.M.
Herald.
They gave him for the Empire’s sake
A feast with fine Eclat;
They little know it was the wake
Of Dick of Kumara.
What wreath should crown the festive glass
For him foresworn, foregone?
Rue for Remembrance, and also
Rue for oblivion.
And has he come at last to that,
And ends his record thus ?
A dead soul buried deep in fat—
A huge sarcophagus.
The worst thing possible for man,
Though he may seem to thrive,
It is to spoil his Maker’s plan—
Be dead —and look alive.
This was the man by Fortune picked
To hold a splendid sway—
Alnaschar Seddon who has kicked
His golden eggs away.
De mortuis ! He is no more,
And let it rest at that —
And throw a clod of sorrow o’er
The old Dead Democrat.


As regards such a case as the See Government s
£4,000,000 resumption loan, floated in England,
the interest on that is almost wholly a dead loss
to N.S.W., even if the rents of the resumed area
pay all the interest, or pay it twice over. The
transaction simply means that £4,000,000
worth of property formerly owned in Australia
is now virtually owned in England, and
that (say) £140,000 a year of rents that
used to be spent in Australia now go to
England, and that the drain on this country’s
resources is £130,000 a year greater than it was.
As for the £3,600,000 odd which this country
received from England it has already been frit-
tered away, and there is no more money in
N.S.W. and no more apparent prosperity than
there was before. Borrowing money abroad
for productive works is only a shade less ruin-
ous than doing the same for non-productive
works. A work is not productive, in the true sense
of the term, because the public is willing to pay
enough for the use of it to cover interest on the loan;
only if it adds enough to the wealth of the coun-
try. An opera co., or an Eiffel tower, or a three-
headed man, if imported with money raised by
foreign loan at 3| per cent., might attract enough
cash to pay 10 per cent, interest on the loan, but
as they would add nothing to the wealth of the
country the interest would be a dead loss to the
country all the same, and, despite their produc-
tiveness, they would be wholly unproductive.
The same might be said of a thumbscrew, which,
if used for the purpose intended, generally pro-
duces a great deal more than 3£ per cent, interest
on its cost.
The Plea of Poverty.
How the public expenditure increases;
1896. 1901. Increase.
Tasmania .-. £750,244 .. £865,000 .. £104,756
S. Australia.. 2,509,468 .. 2,957,954 .. 448,486
Queensland .. 3,567,947 .. 4,855,533 .. 1,287,586
Westralia .. 1,823,863 .. 3,165,244 .. 1,341,381
Victoria .. 6,540,182 .. 7,986,475 .. 1,446,293
N.S. Wales .. 9,852,034 .. 11,350,442 .. 1,498,408
The increase in the cost of government is far ahead
of any increase of population, and in no case had
public works expenditure any important bearing
on the case—that was paid out of loans as usual.
Moreover, in 1901, all the States, especially
Tasmania, had shifted a portion of their ex-
penditure on to the Federal Government, so things
are even worse than they look. Every State has
acquired the easy habit of scattering all the money
it can raise and a little more, without making any
provision for its public works expenditure. If
Queensland, Victoria and N.S.W. decided to
abolish loans and set aside £1,000,000 a year out of
revenue for railways and other works, the evidence
of the above figures is that they could do it without
any increase of revenue and without being even
quite so economical as they wereinlß96. And if the
£1,000,000 a year for new public works was made
a first charge on the revenue—if it had to be paid
whatever else happened—the evidence of the
figures is that there is nothing at all to prevent
the Treasurers making the revenue that was left
cover the country’s necessities. They all did it
before, and they could do it again. As to why
this isn’t done —that is the country’s fault. When
the country demands economy it always does it
with a view to abolishing taxes —never with a
view to abolishing loans. When Victoria’s last
local loan was floated with such difficulty,
Premier ' Peacock practically shut up the
public-works policy of the State, and the
local press and politicians concurred, quite recog-
nising that to expect the Government to build
anything without loan funds was absurd,
and that the idea of reducing ordinary expendi-
ture and squeezing £500,000 or £1,000,000 a year
for public works out of ordinary revenue was quite
impossible. And almost immediately afterwards
a large section of the same press, and many of the
same politicians, started to demonstrate that it
was quite possible to cut down the ordinary ex-
penditure by £500,000 or £1,000,000 —not to reduce
the deficit, or provide for necessary public works,
but in order to cut down taxation, which is not at
all high in any case. When the country always
sees clearly that it is quite possible to cut down
expenditure so as to abolish taxes, and yet that it
is quite impossible to cut it down so as to abolish
loans, any real reform is next to hopeless.
Public Health and Individual Rights.
Some moneyed genius with a taste for research
might make a most interesting book by compiling
an account of all the good laws that have become
obsolete through the lack of a little human sym-
pathy and consideration. The thing that is not
built on human nature will perish. In all proba-
bility the things that are built on it will perish
also, but the other kind will perish first. Maoriland
has a law to the effect that no consumptive shall
be employed in the preparation of food—and the
law is a good one, and will do much to save life.
Quite recently a man who had been all his life
employed in the biscuit and confectionery trade
was found to be suffering from consumption, and
the Dunedin Health Department ordered that he
be dismissed. The order had to be obeyed, and,
through no fault of his own, the man (married and
with several young children) found himself reduced
from the comparative comfort of £3 per week to
utter destitution. He laid his ease before the
local Council, and all the Council could do was to
recommend him to the tender mercies of the
Charitable Aid Board. This is safeguarding
the Public Health, but it is doing it in
so barbarous a way that the law will become
unpopular and repulsive, and in the end
will be practically abrogated. No one will
supply the information that will lead to consump-
tives being suddenly pauperised. The thing is an
outrage, and the law is an outrage also. While
Eroviding for the safety of the public, it should
ave safeguarded the rights of the individual
sacrificed in the public interest. If Maoriland
had sanatoria for consumptives, and the
machinery for making the suddenly-dismissed
consumptive a pensioner until he was cured, it
could logically insist on him leaving work and
taking up his quarters in a healthy resort
till the cure was effected. A man’s first duty
is to his family—at least, nothing will pre-
vent him regarding matters in that light; his
duty to the public comes next, and where the
public insists on being considered first, it ought to
make provision accordingly. Meanwhile, the
man mentioned cannot be employed at the only
trade he knows, in Maoriland. He will drift
about trying to get some sort of occupation, and
spreading the germs of his disease, and the family
will suffer hardships that will probably develop
any latent tendencies it may have towards tuber-
culosis. For the safety of the community, the
suggested addition to the law is as necessary as
the law itself.

The Pearl-Pishing 1 Monopoly.
A letter from “ Sandy MThairson ” in the
North :
Your Sydney daily newspapers have been much exer-
cised during the last 10 or 12 days at the probable alleged
departure of the pearl-fishers from Thursday Island to
Merouke in Dutch New Guinea. Our Brisbane patriots,
Robert Philp and his brothers-in-law, James Olark and
his brothers-in-law, also Borns Philp and Co. of your
city, are getting terribly afraid of the Commonwealth.
Well they may be. According to Hon. John Douglas, of
Thursday Island, pearl-fishing employs 2032 men, and of
those 2032 only 65 are Europeans. There are 247 boats
licensed as pearl-fishing luggers, and no boat is allowed
to go on the ground to fish unless licensed. Early this
year there appeared a Government advt. in the Torres
Straits Pilot. That advt. announced that no more
licenses would be issued to shelling luggers as the grounds
were getting denuded of shell by the excessive working.
The yield of shell in Torres Straits falls and increases
according as the weather during the year is favorable for
working or the reverse. Of the 247 licensed shelling-
boats nearly 200 are owned or controlled by the syndi-
cates of which Burns Philp and Co., Premier Robert
Philp and his brothers-in-law, and James Clark and his
brothers-in-law are the leading and shining lights.
M'llwraitii and M‘Eacharn are the agents in London for
these enterprising monopolists, and get their commission.
Having this information before them the Australian
public need feel no surprise that, when the yield of
pearl-shell for 1901 was a trifle under that of 1900 (owing
to the unfavorable weather), Robert Philp and co. (the
Queensland Government) decided to issue no more
licenses for luggers to go upon the shelling ground. By
this adroit manoeuvre they created a monopoly for them-
selves in licenses. They have already driven out the
white working-man by surrounding him and his boat
with ten or twelve boats worked by the colored aliens,
and working on his patch of shell. Australian pearl-shelling
should give employment to between 2000 and 3000 men
of our own race and blood. The industry extends
from Cairns, in Queensland, to Roebourne, in Wes-
tralia. It produces a material worth nearly £300,000 per
annum, without the pearls. That part of it which is
worked from Thursday Island is in waters which are not
only in the area of the proclaimed Commonwealth, but
are between two shores, both of which belong to
the Commonwealth. The shelling-ground is between
British New Guinea on the north, and Australia on the
South. Where do the Dutch come in ? But what patriots
we have to be sure in Burns, Philp and Co., Robert
Philp and his brothers-in-law, etc. What patriots 1
Because they cannot fill up their shelling boats with the
scum of Manila, Singapore, Hongkong and Japan they
threaten to turn Dutchmen ! If the Commonwealth
Government is composed of statesmen, they will pass over
the letters of the Queensland Govt., ignore the opinions
of MTlwraitii, M'Eacharn and Co., and appoint a
Commission of their own Parliament to thoroughly
inquire into this, which is really a great Australian
industry. That Commission will, no doubt, find out why
it is that this industry, which 12 years ago gave employ-
ment to white divers, now gives employment to none.
That poor little paper, the Evening News, says : “ White
men cannot be expected to dive ! ” Indeed, why not ?
Because pearling monopolists do not want them to come
on the shelling ground, and will never have them until
they are made to have them. When next the election
for Senators in Australia is to the fore, let those who love
their country see to it that this pearl-shelling monopoly
is duly exposed, and that the would-be Senators are
asked—“ Why are white men kept out of the pearlshell
industry ? "
Victoria’s Alleged Parliamentary Reform
Movement.
Victoria would do well to look with a suspicious
eye on that great public demonstration in favor of
a reduction of its State Legislature until it knows
for certain who is running it, and is positive that
the round, red countenance of old Duncan Gillies
is not rising like a full moon behind the curtain.
At present it seems quite possible that the whole
proceeding is a large, stale fish drawn across the
scent in the Tory interest. The agitation arose
first among country property-holders (the same
gang of thieves which swindled the State so
shamefully over the irrigation business), and the
property-holding class appears to have been
largely in it all along. What is demanded
is a reduction in the dimensions of both
Houses —a bald reduction which will save a
little money, but do nothing else. With an
Assembly of 46 members and a Council of 23 there
is nothing to prevent the Council being as stupid,
as hostile to all reform, and as hopeless a snag in
the way of progress as it is now, when it is a dead
idiot of 48 members obstructing an Assembly of
95. At the last general election the people
demanded a popularly-elected Convention to
draft a complete new State Constitution
as the Federal Constitution was drafted.
It was offered them by the party in power,
and they demanded it in the only way
open to them—by returning that party with a
strong majority. They didn’t get their Conven-
tion because the Peacock party “ turned dog,”
but that makes no diffe ence in the moral aspect
of the case. They may demand it again at next
election with such energy that they will get it ;
and there is always the chance that the Conven-
tion might propose the abolition of the 48 Rich
Landlords, and insist on having only one
House of State Legislature, after the fashion of
Ontario, New Brunswick, Manitoba, British
Columbia, Prince Edward Island, and the
North West Territories in Canada. So it is a wise
proceeding now to draw the defunct whale of
strategy across the scent of progress by working
up a bogus public demand for a reduction of mem-
bership which will leave the House of Landlords
in the same impregnable position as before. It is
easy to do such things—2ooo people crying out at
public meetings, especially if the same man
attends several meetings, can make more row and
draw more attention than 50,000 people at the polls.
The dispossessed M’s.L.C. would lose nothing
material —not even their salaries, for they haven’t
any, nor their political ambitions, for the House
of Landlords is no road to the fulfilment of any
great political ambitions. The 23 Landlords could
preserve the interests of the property-holding class
quite as well as the 48, and prevent the imposition
of a really useful land-tax, and save the big estates,
and do all the other sins. And when once this
bogus reform was granted the Upper House would
meet any demands for real improvement by
saying, indignantly, “We have just reformed
ourselves; what more do you want?” Even
if the House of Landlords doesn’t want
to reduce its numbers it gains something by the
present agitation. Last election the fight was
between the party whioh wanted to construct a
new State Constitution by a popular Convention
and the party which didn’t. The former won ;it
took a good deal of political manoeuvring and axe-
grinding to cheat it out of the fruits of its'vic-
tory, and there is no certainty that it might
not be even more difficult to cheat it next time.
If the reformers can be divided there will be three
parties at next election—the Convention Party,
a large section of which hopes to see the Legis-
lative Council utterly abolished; the Bogus
Economy Party, which proposes to reduce both
Houses without reforming the dreadful Council in
the least, but which may capture a lot of the re-
formers’ votes by its cry of economy; and the
Tired, or Ultra-Tory Party, which believes
in letting things remain as they are. By
dividing the reformers the Tired Party
may control the situation, especially as it
will be supported by many members who get
in on the reform-ticket but who hate to see profane
hands laid on the sacred ark of their billets.
They will always have the excuse that the Voice
of the People, by reason of the split between the
Convention Party and the Reduction Party, is
uncertain and unintelligible. And when the
billets are at stake the anxiety of legislators not to
rush into any hasty misinterpretation of the Voice
of the People amounts to a disease.
Our Alien Inclusion Act.
A Queensland sugar co. has just received a con-
signment of 23 Japanese. They were landed in
Townsville and sent on to the co.’s works. Another
batch of 53 is on the road to the co.’s plantation,
and is expected to arrive next month. The Aliens
Immigration Restriction Act seems to be almost
absolutely a dead letter, particularly in Queens-
land. A Federal Parliament return gives the
following interesting figures as to the rejection
and admission of colored people since the passing
of the Immigration Restriction Act:
Number Number
Admitted. Rejected.
N.S.W. .. 12 6
Vic 70 9
Q 274 14
S.A none 2
W.A 28 1
Tas none none
Totals .. 386 32
It seems to be a very efficacious way indeed
for promoting a piebald Australia. If the
administration of the Act is to be on these lines
it will be necessary to demand a measure at once
for the absolute exclusion of colored aliens.
Australia took the present Act “on the honor of
Mr. Barton,” on the understanding that the
educational subterfuge was intended to more
thoroughly exclude objectionable immigrants.
And now it seems to be a subterfuge to admit
them, and is being used even to over-ride the old
Chinese Exclusion Acts.

Now that the first shout of enthusiasm has died
away people begin to notice certain large possi-
bilities about the Anglo- Jap. alliance. The treaty
provides that if either Britain or Japan is at war
with one State the other may remain neutral—-it
may join in if it pleases, but it isn’t bound to do
so. But if Japan is at war with two States then
Britain is pledged to join in, and vice versa. Japan
badly wants to fight Russia —it wants the island
of Saghalien, which it always regarded as a Russian
steal, and it desires to spread Jappy influence in
Corea, and it wants other things as well. But it
has great doubts about its ability to fight Russia
single-handed. Under the new treaty, however,
if it can provoke a war with Russia and Russia’s
ally, France, then Britain is pledged to declare
war against both of them. And it would be much
easier for Japan to fight both Russia and France
with Britain’sassistancethan tofight Russia single-
handed. The little brown Jap.’s best chance to get
level with its enemy is to bring about a war
with him and some other Power while the treaty
lasts. This is where the really gratifying view of
the case comes in—for Britain. Its new brown
friend feels sure that it will have to fight Russia
one day or another, and to-day is the best day for
the purpose. Of course it is a misfortune that so
massive a Power as France is the only other one
that it seems easy to drag in; Russia and Switzer-
land would make the ideal combination, if that
could only be brought about. The Jap difficulty
is that Russia is a State with a vast capacity for
patience. It will probably pub a good deal of the
Jap’s arrogance quietly on its slate until the
Anglo-Jap alliance dies a natural death, and then
ask the Jap person what he meant by it.
It was solemnly proposed the other day by the
Sydney Morning Herald that if the Federal
capital were fixed near Bombala 80,000 acres
should be set apart as a Federal territory. Eighty
thousand acres ! 125 square miles ! Say a bit of
land eleven miles each way. The Federal
Government might be sole ground-landlord inside
that space, but the increased land values, created
by the expenditure of the people’s money, would
spread far beyond these narrow limits, and the
private landlord outside would be there to collect
the proceeds. Nothing short of 5000 square miles
should be set aside for this great national purpose,
and N.S.W., with its 310,700 square miles, can
spare that area, especially in a desolate region
where you may follow the high road for 35
miles without coming to a public-house, and for
10 miles without seeing a house at all.
Furthermore, it is essential that the Federal terri-
tory should have its own port, if there is one handy,
and be sole ground-landlord there and all around.
Australia has now the sort of chance that comes
once in a thousand years to try a splendid and
profitable experiment in land nationalisation,
and if the land-grabbers’ papers succeed in their
efforts to pen up the Federal city on a stuffy little
plot of ground for the temporary benefit of a few
local earth-owners, Australia will regret the mis-
take for all time.

Sydney D.T. has discovered “ the crying need for
retrenchment” in N.S.W. (it hasn’t suggested,
however, the retrenchment of abolishing the sub-
sidies of free postage and free special railway
trains to the morning papers), and some of the
facts and figures which it quotes in regard to the
civil service are certainly remarkable. All the
branches of the service, comparing the figures for
1897-8 with those of 1901-2, show a distinct increase
in cost, ranging from 18 per cent, in one depart-
ment to 30 per cent, in others. The increases are
due chiefly to staff additions and the raising of
staff salaries; and in all cases the percentage of
increase may be fairly called unreasonable and ex-
cessive. On an average, the cost of the civil ser-
vice has increased by nearly 25 per cent, in four
years, whilst the population has only increased 6
per cent. Yet, during the Reid regime, there
was formed, with a loud flourish of trumpets, a
Public Service Board, which was for all future
time to secure an economical and effective civil
service ! That Board does not seem to have been
a brilliant success.
