The Federal Coinage Committee has decided that
the Commonwealth should have a metrical coin-
age, the sovereign being divided into 10 florins,
the florin into 10 dimes, and the dime into 10
cents. The committee certainly hasn’t done
much. If it was proposed to adopt the dollar of
100 cents, this country would get a simple metrical
coinage which would bring it in line with Canada,
the U.S., Chili and other countries, instead of
starting out on its own and adding another to the
over-numerous coinages already existing. And a
metrical system of weights and measures to re-
place the 50 or so tangled and incomprehensible
tables now existing, is far more essential than a
metrical coinage. The ideal system would be a
uniform metrical coinage, beginning with the
sovereign and working downwards, and a uniform
metrical system of weights and measures, for the
whole British Empire, which empire now has so
many coinages and weights and measures that it
is just about crazy. Supposing 400,000,000 people
were using the same methods there would then be
a chance that the rest of the globe would join in
by degrees. Perhaps if Australia refused to send
any more contingents till this matter was attended
to some good might result.
The Freetrade Brisbane Courier, after declaring
for years that Protection means high prices and
Freetrade means low ones, and that the consumer
pays the duty, and more than the duty, thus (27th
March) gives the show away :
The great bulk of the oil consumed in Australia by the
working-man is controlled by the Standard Oil Trust.
When it became likely that kerosene would be placed on
the freelist, merchants still hastened to purchase in bond.
Such a course may seem absurd, since many of our readers
will assume that the price would be the same, if the duty
were taken off, whether the oil were in or out of bond.
Why did not buyers of large parcels take their time ?
Simply because it had been rumored that the Standard
Oil people were preparing to obtain a good slice of the
lifted duty. That is to say, the duty may be taken off
kerosene, but the price of the commodity will not neces-
sarily fall in proportion. The working-man will not bene-
fit so much as the monopolists who control the oil-market.
They will sell their oil as if the duty, or a good slice of it,
remained.
The Courier thus suddenly discovers that Pro-
tection doesn’t necessarily mean increased prices,
and that the seller sometimes pays the duty. If
he does that in a case like that of kerosene, where
there is no serious possibility of any local industry
to compete with him, how much more is he likely
to do it in the matter of (say) boots where he has
to compete with local industry? And why does
the Courier imagine a vain thing, and fancy that
it is a Freetrade paper when it is liable to
symptoms akin to the above ?
Freetrader Willis, of N.S.W., vehemently
denounced the match-industry in the House of
Reps, as one which distributed £5OOO a year in
wages and lost the Treasury £12,000 a year in
duty on imported matches. In his view it was
another of these Unprofitable Industries that the
Shoddy party is always girding at, and by the
Freetrade theory such an industry is a loss of
£7OOO a year to the country. By Willis’s own
showing, if there was no local match-industry the
country would have to pay about £50,000 a year
more for imported matches, and if the Treasury
got £12,000 out of that the country would still
have £38,000 a year more to pay to some foreign
community. Also, it would have 50 or 100 more
unemployed citizens on its hands. Fifty or 100
more unemployed and £50,000 more going out of
the country in order to provide £12,000 revenue,
are the Freetrader’s idea of economy. The foreign
manufacturers’ hireling says the Australian
buyer pays the duty and then, when the
people don’t pay it but manufacture for
themselves, he reckons the duty they don’t pay
as so much dead loss. Even wheat-growing and
cattle-rearing are Unprofitable Industries now by
this theory, for if all the wheat-fields were burned
and all the cattle killed off, Australia would have
to import its wheat and beef, and then the Trea-
sury would get the duty. By raising wheat and
cattle the country, according to all solid Foreign-
trade theories, loses two or three millions every
year now. The same applies to sugar, potatoes,
beer, wine, &e. It is a beautiful argument when
you come to inspect it closely, and should have a
special lunatic asylum built to put it in lest it gets
lost.
_
Sydney Telegraph, discussing the latest British
move towards Protection, the imposition of corn
and flour duties, prints interviews with prominent
Sydney merchants. One is allowed to say this —
It does not follow that the people in England will have
to pay more for their bread because of this duty. When
we start exporting, I take it that these charges of sd. a
cwt. on flour and 3d. on wheat will be added to our
present charges.
Another remarks—
The new duty will be a very good thing for the Home
people. Of course it will greatly assist the British millers,
as well as the home farmers, whose industry has been at
a low ebb for many years past.
Another (with a very unkind preface indeed):
“You can talk a lot of twaddle, but it all comes back
to this : A duty has to be reckoned, by shippers as an
extra charge. If the price of wheat is £1 9s. 6d. to-day,
we have got to reckon the duty as an extra charge, or
else the English buyer has got to pay £llos. 6d., or some-
thing extra to make up for the charges. It is probable
that we will have to accept at least half of the duty, and
the British consumer will pay the rest.”
The JD.T. chose its own experts and put them in
the box, and their evidence was: (1) that the
foreign producer, and not the English consumer,
will pay perhaps the whole, and certainly at least
the half, of the protective duty on corn and flour
imposed by England ; (2) that the duties will be a
very good thing for the British farmer. And that
is the whole case for a protective tariff in a nut-
shell. One doesn’t expect, however, to see it so
succinctly expressed in the Freetrade Sydney
D.T.

The Senate, in a fit of aberration, amended portion
of the Public Service Bill, and struck out the clause
that provided for a minimum-wage in the Govern-
ment service, because it would increase the ex-
penditure by £40,000 a year, which ‘ ‘ the Common-
wealth cannot afford.” If this is right, why send
contingents to South Africa? The Post-office,
which is under Drake’s control, is badly in need
of a minimum-wage, and for long it has been known
to the public as a sweating-den and criminal-maker.
Mail drivers who have been in the service from 13
to 20 years get £B4 a year. Operators, some of
whom have worked themselves up from telegraph-
boys, and have been in the department doing
nerve-wearing work for 20 and 25 years, are getting
£BO a year ! Postmasters’ wages vary from £2 a
week to £6, and in some of the city offices they are
drawing the' magnificent wage of £2 10s. a week.
Letter-carriers fare worst of all, as most of them
only get £75 a year. The department grades
officials as junior-postmasters, junior-operators,
&c. This system makes the public think that they
are not first-class hands, but such is not the case ;
it only means that they are receiving junior
salaries. Many of the juniors are married, and
have growing families to keep, to do which they
must solve some pretty stiff problems. Parliament
could have afforded to pay the minimum-wage
proposed if it had not thrown away a lot of postal
revenue by its suppression of Tattersall. Hav-
ing suppressed Tattersall, and sacrificed the
revenue, it didn’t say that it was therefore unable
to pay its own members a living wage—not much.
It decided that it couldn’t pay its employees a
living wage.
The fact that the present agitation for a reduction
in the cost of Government in Victoria was started
by a mass meeting of indignant farmers at Kya-
bram recalls the fact that the Victorian Treasury
would be some hundreds of thousands richer but
for the shameful dishonesty of the agriculturists
thereabouts. When Deakin was a Minister
in Victoria he was got at by one M‘Coll,
a canal faddist, who talked him into expending
much money in extensive irrigation works on the
River Goulburn. These works are certainly as
perfect in their way as anything can be. All the
inhabitants of the districts around were in ecstacies
and meetings were held which eulogised the noble
and patriotic action of the Government. There
was no difficulty in getting the landed proprietors
to promise to further the gigantic scheme by
having their property irrigated, and when the
works were completed, applications for water
poured in by the score, the grateful applicants
agreeing enthusiastically to pay the very reason-

4
A cleaned and brightened theatre was
Sydney Royal when it re-opened on Saturday, after
a long retirement from public life, and the audi-
ence was well-disposed and quite prepared to
speak kindly to the new actress. Still, it wasn’t
such an audience as might have been expected
for the opening performance of a new star,
especially when two theatres are empty, and
opposition is at the lowest ebb. This writer has
a. dark suspicion that the public is growing dead
tired of “As You Like It.” There are six
Shakespearian works whose names hang with
the persistence of an ancient tarred malefactor
in chains outside the playhouses of the English-
speaking world : “ Hamlet,” “ Merchant of
Venice,” “As You Like It,” “Romeo and
Juliet,” “Othello,” and “Macbeth” —the last
two hanging with a shade less persistence, it may
be, than the rest. There are a few others which
appear occasionally, and a number that are almost
as forgotten as what Caractacus had for dinner.
Even assuming that the public isn’t hungering for
“ Henry IV.” or “Henry VI.” or “Henry VIII.,”
there are a dozen good Shakespearian plays
which are almost as rare as the poems of Edward
VII., and if they are not quite up to thestandard of
the weary six, still the public might prefer to see
a second-rate play for the first time rather than a
first-rate one for the 120th. A good manager
with a good co., and a good star, and a good
theatre, who announces a season of the forgotten
plays of Shakespeare, may make a lot of money, or
he may lose it, but either way he will be a public
benefactor.
* * * •
Miss Janet Waldorf who perpetrated the
frequent Rosalind at the Royal on Saturday, is a
clever actress with a good figure and a bright
capable face —also a rather hard voice. She is not
an overwhelming star, but she supplies a pleasing
version of the lady with the doublet and hose, and
the audience accepts her in a kindly spirit, with-
out absolutely losing its head in the struggle.
This agreeable visitor is supported by a company
which answers the same description as herself. It
maintains a fair level of excellence, and treats
Shakespeare with the respect due to his age. H.
H. Vincent, back after a long and regrettable
absence, gives a strong rendering of Jaques the
philosopher, and delivers the famous soliloquy
about the disadvantages of being old in a highly
creditable fashion. Norval M‘Gregor is a bright,
boyish-looking Orlando, and Dale Revenall is a
brawny Charles, and between them they produce
one of the most realistic wrestling-matches ever
seen on the Australian stage. As a rule Charles
knows something of wrestling while Orlando
knows about enough of it to struggle with
a caterpillar, and Charles toils visibly to do
himself credit, and yet let Orlando win, and
to conceal his deep longing to take up the
incompetent Orlando and break the orchestra or
the duke with him. In the present case, however,
Orlando contrives to look as if he were keeping
Charles fully employed and is not a person to
be thrown at any duke with impunity. Of the two
dukes, Hamilton Stewart makes the one look
wicked on the throne in long, red clothes, while
Austin Melford makes the other look benevolent
in the forest in long, brown boots. Miss Gleeson
is a clever Audrey ; Miss Ruth Mackay is only
moderate as Celia; Edmund Gwenn is a high-class
Touchstone, and J. B. Atholwood, who is a young
man with a great capacity for old man characters,
does the desolate part of old Adam most com-
mendably. Adam was the original Dan’l Jaikes
of dramatic literature, bestowing on his young
master in the hour of calamity the penny he had
saved up against a rainy day. But Adam is a
profoundly serious old man, and if he had met
Bodger later on in the forest he would probably
have failed to extract any humor out of the in-
cident. Besides, in the indefinite land where
Shakespeare’s dukes hang out, Bodger would be
impossible.
• • • *
The present Shakespearian season lasts
only a fortnight, and then the Comic Opera Co.
returns to Sydney to play the “Runaway Girl.”
** * *
Once we grasp the fact with both ears (and
provided our minds are free from inter-State jea-
lousy) that Amy Castles does not come out here
as a “finished singer,” we shall appreciate
her singing. Amy’s voice to-day, after less
than three years’ European cultivation, shows
what study has done for her in an art which, like
chess, can never be wholly learned, because its
possibilities are endless. Thursday of last week
the re-captured bird was let loose in Sydney
Town Hall, where she limed the souls of over
three thousand willing listeners with her sweet,
bright voice.
* * • •
At the Castles’ first concert, the star’s
contributions were Ophelia’s mad scene, and in
place of advertised “Jerusalem,” the music of
which had been forgotten, she warbled David’s
“ O Dieu Brahma ! ” and her share of the duet,
“ Parigi o cara,” from “ Traviata for encores —
“Home, Sweet Home,” and an Irish folk-song of
eerie, crooning melody. The gem of the second
concert was her (Coenen’s) “Spring,” and the
Waltz Song from “Romeo and Juliet” was not
two scintillations behind. In the duets with
Dani, the well-trained tenor does not dominate
the girl—their voices dovetail, and the audience
accepts her with him naturally; any weakness in
her would have been pathetically shown up by his
strength. A quality of the great artist she has—
she sings pianissimo, a whisper that may be heard
at the farthest end of the hall; yet her fortissimo
is not a shout. The public, which has insisted
on starring Dani, is as mad as ever on its only
Carlo’s high C. Miss Una Bourne, an ascetic-
looking young pianiste of great nerve, plays well
and satisfies those who .can -stand the piano as a
solo instrument. Miss Nora M‘Kay, a Queens-
land violiniste, has some genius, and if she could
only avoid making a double chin with the south
end of her fiddle, fastidious persons would be
grateful. Miss Dalrymple and Mr. Baritone
Needham are always well received and encored,
and when Amy and Carlo are on the programme
it is no small honor to be asked to remain in the
road longer than is absolutely necessary.
** * *
The “ Span of Life,” despite the old age
that is coming upon it, hangs across its African
chasm at Sydney Lyceum with all the vigor of
youth. One reason for its continuance, besides its
span and its chasm, probably lies in the fact that
it departs here and there from the old, dry-rotted
canons of melodrama. For instance, the incrimi-
nating document looms largely in Act 1., but it
isn’t found in the coat-pocket of the innocent
hero, because the son of the lady who put
it there abstracts it, unknowing that Ma used it
merely as a trap, and being led away by his own
ungovernable passion for property. So the packet
is found in the right person’s pocket for once in a
way, and the boy who ate the poisoned grapes
clinches the matter by telling on Ma before he
dies near the footlights. The lighthouse-scene
goes as well as usual, and Miss Ross’s rather
springy Robin Redbreast walk is conspicuous
when she accepts her dowry in an off-hand
manner from the villain without counting it, and
takes it along to Baker in the lighthouse just
when a storm is rising and it is dangerous to be
out. In real life even a girl in love doesn’t take
all her worldly wealth to a wet lighthouse in bad
weather, but knows enough to take it instead to a
dry bank. The heroine of melodrama, however, is
blessed with a lack of sense which is beyond
words, and she falls in as a consequence, and then
she puts on a white frock and lets her hair down
and reposes her trust in Providence.
** * *
“Dr. Bill” was still on the stage at
Sydney Empire when this issue went to press,
though if the Cosgroves adhere to their present
intentions something new will take place by the
time it reaches the public. The Empire is an
erratic little place and likewise a law unto itself,
and it may keep up to its intentions or it
may not. “What Women Wed” is the piece
which is to succeed “Dr. Bill.” Whether it is
meant to show the kind of woman who marries,
or the kind of object a woman is liable to get
married to, is a matter regarding which the writer
offers no opinion. Either way a large subject
is opened up.
** * *
The new variety programme at Sydney
Palace on Saturday night lasted until 11.40 p.m.,
and kept the bulk of the audience until then —
strong proof of its attractiveness. On Monday a
merciful management compressed things consider-
ably, and people got away by 11. As a further
reform “ the little Dartos” might be sent home to
bed, and a fairly strict rule against encores enforced.
Perhaps the chief feature of the new programme
is the revelation of Miss Rose Sutherland’s
versatility as a dancer and a comedy singer.
Bunth and Rudd are again at the Palace, and the
unctuous humor of Bunth is now shown in two
“turns.” “The animated song-sheet ” proves to
be a fresher and more amusing novelty than
usual. Salerno still juggles wonderfully, and
does some new feats, which are quite good enough
to rank with his old ones. The public gets a lot
for its money at Sydney Palace just now : a
comic-opera in tabloid form, a compressed circus,
a juggling show, and a minstrel entertainment-
all of them good.
** * *
Kitty, the horse-riding Tiger, and “ Fred
Grey,” the Boxing Kangaroo, are the two greatest
attractions of Fitzgeralds’ new programme. Fred
boxes at first in rather a ladylike manner, but
warms up after awhile and gets his opponent’s
head in chancery several times, and lets out at him
furiously. During the intervals his weird features
are duly, fanned with a white towel, a proceeding
which he takes in a resentful spirit, desiring to
hoof the attentive one. When he shows a dis-
position to use his. large hinder feet in defiance of
rule viii., sub-section 127, he is promptly bundled
into his triumphal car and taken away in a
wondering frame of mind. Kitty goes through
her part with more spirit than the lion named
Solomon, but to less imposing effect. The six
white broncos who yawn at the audience make a
pleasing picture on the whole, and the Olympians,
Harry Cardello and Miss Katie, are cleverly
imitated by clown Duckworth ; the hurricane
hurdle act of Miss F. Bartolo brings in the usual
hoop of fire and almost-flaming lady, and the
other new items include the wonderful Cycle
Whiz by various whirling wheelers, who whiz with
great fury, and the grand equestrian spectacle,
“The Harvest Home.” They really do have a
Harvest Home in England, because they have
harvests there. Here, where the harvest consists
often of a bush-fire and an appeal to the Govern-
ment for seed wheat, the habit has never taken
root.
** * *
“ The cake-walk ” is perhaps seen at its
best in the performance of the Perman trio at
Sydney Tivoli; and seeing the cake-walk, at its
best, shows how completely eccentricity has dis-
placed gracefulness in dancing. The ebullition
of savage good spirits, not the languorous grace of
feeling, has to be expressed by the successful
dancer : there is no poetry of dancing, only a very
rugged prose nowadays. Peggy Pryde, the
vivacious, is playing a short return season at this
theatre, and shares the favor of the audience with
Callaghan and Mack, Lydia Titus and the Jack-
son family.
*# * *
Clement Wragge frantically waving the
Union Jack in Rev. Walters’ Australian Church,
Sydney, on Sunday night was resented by many
as a burlesque both on politics and religion. Mr.
Wragge lectured in the Exchange Hotel hall
during the week to a big and remunerative
audience, which came fully prepared to be inter-
ested, but soon had its enthusiasm damped by the
speaker’s puerilities. Mr. Wragge should get
his discourses severely edited by some smart man.
** * *
“Dorothy,” even in its rough revival at
Her Majesty’s (Melb.), is a welcome contrast to
the latter-day musical comedy, which loses all
coherence after 10 p.m. and lends itself to new
interpolations as it goes along. “ Dorothy ” has
suffered no changes for the better or worse all
these 15 Australian years. The present faults and
virtues of the opera are just what they were when
natty little Leonora Braham played the heroine,
and Elton used to fall down the staircase of Squire
Bantam’s ancestral hall. Composer Cellier’s
charming melodiesdeserved a more ingenious story,
likewise brighter dialogue, but the librettist (B. C.
Stephenson) can at least be credited with an
attempt to do the right thing. The show is a
harmony of music, color and character. There
were suggestions of rustic romance, top boots
and old fashions in Cellier’s work, and Stephen-
son, having to suit the ‘ book ’ to the score
borrowed his main idea from “She Stoops to
Conquer,” and gave “Dorothy” a congenial
atmosphere. The plot is not so absurd in concep-
tion as in the working-out of details. Two men
meet the same two girls in each of the three acts,
and are supposed to regard them as three different
pairs of girls, which is a difficult supposition to
swallow when one of the girls talks with Grace
Palotta’s foreign accent. Also, the bogus burglary
at the hall of the bucolic Bantam is a clumsily
constructed fraud. These things might have been
made less foolish, but the same complaint applies
to the plot of many other tuneful operas with a lot
more force.
• * * *
“ Dorothy,” as produced at Her Majesty’s
for a nine-nights’ season, is a sufficiently lively
and well-staged reminder of the bygone period
when Melbourne expected better performances,
and got Jem. Perhaps the public was spoilt by
the Firm in those times. A new, unspoilt public
is springing up to offer atonement for the hyper-
critical snortings of its progenitors, and
“Dorothy” seemed to please it on Saturday.
All the same, the performance was musically
sick and dramatically weak for the most part.
Miss Florence Young and Kenningham plus a
crude chorus, represented the vocal strength of
the show. The music of the name part is too
high for Miss Young, but she makes it go and her
brilliant top-note seizes every opportunity to
shine. Moreover she is a spirited, buxom Dorothy.
Miss Palotta is a nice Grace who can’t sing satis-
factorily ; and Miss Maud Chetwynd fails to the
same extent, or even more so. Bathurst, the
baritone, will no doubt improve on his Saturday
“ form.” He sang out of tune, as a rule, and his
difficult ballad, “ Queen of my Heart, To-Night,”
was not a work of art. Lauri, as Lurcher, is not
quite at his best, though Lauri in any case
amounts to a success.
* * * Hi
At the Princess’s, where the Broughs are
drawing very near to their absolutely final farewell,
the wild narrative of “Dr. Bill” is putting in
six nights, not because the management wanted
to perpetrate that frivolous deed, but because
“ Dr. Bill ’’ looked likely to find favor with many
erring citizens who stop away from serious
comedies. Judging from the medium size of
Saturday’s audience Brough might as well have
devoted his penultimate week to a heavier task.
“Dr. Bill,” although it wears well and remains
fairly funny for its age, is not a cyclonic absurdity.
The situations require a more impossible arrange-
ment of doors leading into next rooms than is
usual in newer farces, and the red-dressed girl
of giddy tendencies thrusting her society upon
the man who has now taken unto himself a
mother-in-law and a higher code of morality, is
rather an exhausted interloper. Nevertheless,
the kangaroo dance, which results from the arrival
of the scarlet woman, was hailed with joy on
Saturday, and the house roared at Brough and
Miss Temple as though their historic hop were a
revelation of unsuspected skill. The versatile
Robert ought to disguise himself once again in
war-paint and feathers, and give them his Red
Indian dance from “ Princess Toto ” —but no
matter. On Saturday (26th), “A Village Priest”
will be revived for two performances only ; after
which “ The Liars ” and “ Mrs. Tanqueray ” may
be expected, unless the advt. decrees otherwise.
** * *
“ Greed of Gold ” continues to hold the
ensanguined stage of Melb. Royal, and the man
who got struck dumb on a mill-wheel is grunting
and gesticulating as before. He grunts loudest
when an artless small boy wants to pay the princi-
pal villain a shilling for an attempted murder,
but he utters no grunts whatever whilst acting
as ballet-master for his sister’s bridesmaids.
Probably the dumb gentleman’s emotion at
this proud moment is too deep for grunts.
He merely waves a handkerchief towards
the ballet at the request of his mother,
or his aunt, who presently retires to her
bedroom on the ground floor, and dies a violent
death. Meanwhile the bride and bridegroom and
other people have gone upstairs to roost, and as
they don’t come to any harm the moral of the
drama is obvious. Never have your sleeping
apartment on the ground floor when danger
threatens, and there never was a melodrama in
which it didn’t threaten. Another fatal mistake
is the habit of keeping one’s cash and papers (in-
cluding the last will and testament) in a room
where somebody invariably enters from the garden
as soon as the light is turned down. Hundreds of
wealthy parents and uncles have died to slow
music in consequence of this habit.
at * * *
At Melb. Bijou the converted capitalist in
gaudy raiment is still heaping floral tributes on a
beauteous Henrietta. Also the man of many
dollars asserts his ancient right to kiss a tall,
attractive niece every evening till further notice,
and what with one girl and the other the Wrong
Mr. Wright ought to be happy. Twenty years oi
unflagging energy in the wholesale liquor
business bring a lot of consolation for that
healthy American citizen when he decides to
let himself off the chain. Probably “Wrong
Mr. Wright” was not intended as a sermon
for the young’, nevertheless it enforces tha
solemn, unpoetic truth that everything in woman-
hood comes to the millionaire who waits. Wright
is a stout, bald-headed joke of the toughest com-
mercial description, yet at a time of life when tha
average married man is a mere abandoned hulk on
the ocean of social life, Wright floats his richly-
upholstered pleasure yacht, and Roxy Barton
rushes the position of first mate. Also, he is
privileged to gaze on the awful spinster whom he
didn’t marry in the dim long ago, and the spec-
tacle of what he missed adds greatly to his
happiness.
** # *
Fagan and Byron, the latest arrivals at
Rickards’s, are a nimble Hibernian male who
bursts forth in a violet-colored suit, and a delect-
able little lady whose costumes are even more
frequent and fanciful. They sing alternately,
assisted by a wondrous gramophone which repeats
the refrain, and they dance together, more or less.
First impression of Miss Byron and her Fagan are
favorable rather than superlative. Colby and
Way, together with the stuffed figure who implores
Colby to place Way in the box, are back at the
Opera House, the other people being as they were
last week.

Marshall Hall’s 49th orchestral concert,
with Madame Wiedermann as vocalist, is to
transpire at Melb. Town Hall next Saturday
(26th) afternoon, when the admirers of those
genuine artists and musical gospellers ‘ will cer-
tainly not forget to be present. On this occasion
a symphonic overture by August Siede will be
sandwiched between pieces by Wagner and
Beethoven.

Adelaide Royal replaced “San Toy” by
“The Runaway Girl.” J. C. Williamson ran
over to see one night of each piece, and found
them going very strong. Rose Musgrove played
the maid instead of San ; Lilian Digges playing
heroine instead of San’s maid ; but the latter’s
voice is hardly strong enough for leading parts.
Similarly, Chas. Trood came down from the
Emperor of China’s throne to take juvenile lead.
The Jubilee Singers, who have been giving hymns
at the Town Hall, have now moved in, with
a (presumably sinful) programme “specially
arranged for the patrons of the Royal.” Nellie
Stewart, in “Old Drury,” follows immediately.
The Tivoli (under manager Leete) has a strong
programme, with Derenda and Breen, the Lund-
greens’ ladder, and Winton (and M’Ginty) at the
head of it ; also Worttwell and Emily Lyndale,
two English artists just off the boat.

The See-O’Sullivan Government, however, makes things
look a little better by including as revenue the rents of a
huge block of properties which it has taken over but not
paid for, and by not including as expenditure the interest
that has accrued up to date on the unpaid purchase
money— that it gaily paid out of loans. And by that
simple, crude, primitive dodge the See-O’Sullivan Govern-
ment makes things appear like this :
New South Wales (9 months to 31st March).
Revenue (Rents of
Resumed Area of Ordinary
Sydney included). Expenditure. Deficit.
1901 .. £7,840,057 .. £7,972,455 .. £132,398
1902 .. 8,081,426 .. 8,200,540 .. 119,114
Increase.. £241,369 .. £228,085
Decrease £13,284
The real deficit, if the accounts were treated on an honest
basis, is £111,629 more than it was at this date last year,
though by the Government’s disreputable dodge it is
made to look £13,214 less.

The process of paying for the resumed property is lament-
ably slow, and the See-O’Sullivan policy makes it pay to
be slow. If a property taken over yields, say, £lO,OOO a
year in net rental, and the interest on the purchase-
money payable to the previous owner from the date of
resumption till his claim is settled also runs to
£lO,OOO a year, and the Government sticks to its
present discreditable dodge of using the £lO,OOO
rent as revenue and paying the £lO,OOO interest
out of loan, it certainly suits it to be slow. If the
matter hangs fire for one year, the Government is
able to treat £lO,OOO of other people’s money as clear
revenue ; if payment is postponed for three years it gets
the benefit of £30,000. In the latter case, of course,
£30,000 instead of £lO,OOO is unjustifiably added to the
public debt, but a small thing like that isn’t calculated
to trouble the See Government much.

Mr. Mephan "Ferguson, Engineer, Ironfoundor,
&c., of Melbourne, Adelaide, and Perth, reports that the
great demand for spiral riveted pipes has necessitated the
increase of manufacturing facilities at his Footsoray
works, and he is now in a position to supply orders at
very short notice, for all sizes of pipes from six to twenty
inches in diameter, with either patent cast-iron bolted
joints, flanged joints or the ordinary spigot and faucet
lead joints. Mr. Ferguson also manufactures boilers,
pumps, fluming, patent Nelyambo earth-scoops or tank-
cleaners, rabbit exterminators, refrigerators, ice-making
machinery, bolts,nuts,forgings, andallsortsof iron-work.*

Mr. A. Gilfillan, B.Sc., O.E. (Edin.), Mining
and Metallurgical Engineer, Stock Exchange Buildings,
Melbourne, is leaving for London about April next, for
the purpose of establishing a London office, and attending
to the flotation of approved mining and other proposi-
tions. Before leaving Mr. Gilfillan will visit the various
colonies for the purpose of inspecting any options which
may be offered. Correspondence invited from mine'
owners who have properties worth handling.*

Gazette of January, 1891, compares Zetz Spa to the cele-

bladder, diabetes, dyspepsia, splenic and hepatic dis-
orders.
* * * •
Vanning Tables.—The very newest and what
gives exceedingly fair promise of superseding all other
tables is the machine known as the Wesley-Baird Vanning
Table. The table is of South Australian invention, and
has given excellent results on Broken Hill ores and ores
containing pyrites, carbonate of copper and tin. Like all
tables, the best work is done on “ fines,” and more espe-
cially when the product is properly classified. The
Central Mining and Tramway Co., of 424 Little Collins-
street, Melbourne, and 40 Hunter-s.treet, Sydney, are the
sole agents in the Eastern States. The table has a capa-
city up to 30 tons per day.*

The Austral Otis Engineering Oo.’s tender for
the supply of nearly a million arc lamps of Conradty’s
manufacture has been accepted by the City Council of
Melbourne. The same co. has also been entrusted with
the supply of the Launceston City Council’s requirements
of arc lamp carbons.*

The latest is a U.S. nickel trust, capital
£6,000,000. It proposes to control the American and
Canadian works, and also important deposits in New
Caledonia, and will then have a practical monopoly of
production.

Queensland’s own revenue and expenditure are concerned,
this year is £118,899 worse than last. Loan expenditure
(£865,201 for the nine months) is £60,808 more
than it was for the corresponding period y ea F;
Another deficit of very close on £500,000 is
practically certain, and £BOO,OOO has been talked
about, yet the imbecile Philp Ministry makes no
effort to stop the drift by imposing land and income
taxes, or by other means. It is waiting for something to
turn up, yet what it hopes or imagines can turn up passes
comprehension. The fall in revenue is solely owing to
the abolition of border duties and to the fact that postal
revenue (and the corresponding expenditure) are no
longer included in the State balance-sheet. The railway
revenue has improved, and promises to be very nearly the
largest Queensland ever collected. Dividend tax for the Dine
months is nearly £3OOO better than in the corresponding
period of last year ; totalisator tax has increased £100i;
license revenue, £2OOO ; railways (as already mentioned),
£44,000 ; and miscellaneous revenue has improved £92,000.
Barring a drop of £650 in land revenue the improvement
is an all-round one. The revenue, in fact, bids fair to be
the best Queensland has ever received from its purely
State sources of income, and the Philp Government is in
a fair way to have a £500,000 deficit despite this fact.
The policy of waiting till the biggest revenue on the
State’s record grows £500,000 bigger in order. to put
things straight is a hopeless business. There is more
likely to be a fall than a rise, and if ends can’t be made
to meet now the only remedy is more taxation.
Victoria’s revenue for the nine months to 31st
March, 1902, was £5,208,072 as compared with £5,495,998
up to same date last year, a decline of £287,926. The
purely State sources of revenue have increased by £94,523
but the fact that postal receipts no longer appear in the
State balance-sheet creates a large apparent decrease, in
the total. Still Peacock is relieved of the corresponding
postal expenditure, so his position ought to be good
enough. In the absence of any statement of expenditure,
however, this is mere conjecture.

The March revenue in Tasmania was £4424 more
than for March, 1901, so things look a little better for the
moment.
Tasmania (3 months to 31st March).
Revenue.
1901 £208,742
1902 .. .. ... 175,270
Decrease £33,472
Tasmania’s politicians still show the same old hopeless-
ness of spirit over their financial situation. Philip Fysh
now says that by the end of the five years book-keeping
period the island will have accumulated £750,000 of
deficits, which will have to be added to the permanent
debt. During the five years book-keeping period an ac-
count is k°pt for each State, and each gets back the
revenue t supplied, less its exact share of the Federal
expenditure. At the end of that time, Tasmanian poli-
ticians hope for a new arrangement by which the island
will get more than its exact share, and be put in a con-
dition of affluence at somebody else’s loss. The favorite
idea is that, after five years are up, the surplus will be
divided at so much per inhabitant, and that .is quite pos-
sible. But the event may not come off. If it does come
off, it may leave Tasmania very much where it is now,
for, with the increase of local production brought about
by the abolition of border tariff, imports and customs
revenue are bound to decrease, and a division of the sur-
plus according to population in 1908 may not give Tas-
mania any more than the present method yields in 1902.
And if the new method does give it, say, £30,000 a year
more, the interest on the £750,000 of deficits which P.
Fysh proposes adding to the public debt will abont
swallow it up and leave the deficit as large as ever. Any-
how, Fysh can’t expect any new tariff arrangement to
give Tasmania £150,000 a year more, and that is what, he
says that it wants. So Tasmania had better face its diffl
culty now, and start that State lottery.

Westralia still collects its inter-State duties, so it stands
in a different position from its neighbors. And its sur-
plus is created in a great measure by cutting down the
public works expenditure—the part of it that is paid out
of revenue, not the part that is paid out of loans.
South Australia presents a lamentable picture
—the worst of the lot in some senses :
South Australia (9 months to 31st March).
Revenue.
1901 £2,086,876
1902 1,755,019
Decrease £331,856
The disaster is worse than it looks, in this sense : Only
£5OOO of the decline in the receipts is owing to loss of
customs revenue under the Federal tariff, and £181,350
of it arises from the transfer of the post-office revenue
(and expenditure) to the Federal accounts. This means
that the State’s own sources of revenue have dried up to
the extent of £145,000 in nine months. Railway revenue
has gone down £95,000 through the depression at Broken
Hill, the place which has done so much in recent years to
keep Jenkins’ unlucky State afloat. Land-tax, income-
tax, stamps, licences, probate duties, land revenue—in
fact, almost everything has fallen unanimously. With a
bad harvest, the second largest debt per head in the
world, nearly a million of old deficits still unpaid, the
profits which it should have made out of the Adelaide
tramways—almost the only promising spec, that hascome
its way of late years—jobbed away to Snow, with no
minerals worth mentioning, and little coal, and, conse-
quently, poor manufacturing prospects, with a dismally
barren" and waterless country, and a Jenkins, the pros-
pect could hardly be worse.
» » * *
The row between the Broken Hill municipal
council and the mines over the amount of the mines rates
has been settled by wiping the slate. The cos. spent
£5320 in law and the town between £6OOO and £6500.
Each side pays its own costs ; the cos. pay no rates at all
for 1901, but as against that they don’t require repay-
ment of £3OOO which the court said they had overpaid in
1900. The law is just as uncertain as it was when the
row started, and while it remains so the lawyers may
hope for a renewal of the ructions any time. Of course
for the present the Privy Council appeal is abandoned.

The Bank of Adelaide, the lone survivor of four
South Australian banks, hasn’t quite kept up to the high
level it attained during the year ended March, 1901, but
its consistent prosperity still stands by it, and its growth
is remarkable: —
Hk Pr ° fltB ' Dividends ’ Re d se?vel°Re?ervi.
1891 ..’ £33,398 7 per cent. =£28,000 £5398 £152,385
1892 .. 31,116 7 „ „ 28,000 3116 155,501
1893.. 30,976 7 „ „ 28,000 2976 158,477
1894.. 30,763 7 „ „ 28,000 2763 161,240
1895.. 31,049 7 ~ ~ 28,000 3049 164,289
1896.. 31,225 7 ~ „ 28,000 3225 167,514
1897.. 31,047 7 „ „ 28,000 3047 170,561

1899.. 33,026 7 ~ ~ 28,000 5026 180,988
1900.. 38,413 8 „ „ 32,000 6413 187,401
1901.. 48,097 8 ~ ~ 32,000 16,097 203,498
1902.. 45,925 8 „ „ 32,000 13,925 217,423
The profits represent £7 12s. 2d. per cent, per
annum on the shareholders’ funds with which the year
commenced—a very respectable figure. The Bank of
Adelaide holds £400,328 in Government securities—an
amount rather more than equal to its capital; and its
total reserve of cash and liquid securities is £1,081,284,
equal to 50 per cent, on all liabilities to the public. These
are exceedingly solid figures, and, though both deposits
and liquid securities are about £lOO,OOO less than they
were a year ago, the extra depression which has lately
settled on S.A. is sufficient explanation of that. The
solidity of the Bank of Adelaide appears to be a great
deal better assured than that of the State in which it has
its residence.
In 1890, the Bank of Adelaide had reserves
amounting to £196,986, but a wyiting-off of £50,000 for
losses made in the time of the great slump when the
Commercial Bank of S.A. went under, brought the figures
down materially. The loss has now been more than made
good, but with a bigger debt and a worse drought, and
even more gloomy prospects, hanging over the State
than in 1886, the reserves can’t be too solid. The Bank of
Adelaide has a capital of £400,000, so its total shareholders’
funds are £617,423. There is room for a much larger
institution than this in S.A., where outside banks do a
very large proportion of the business, and S.A.’s solitary
bank should keep its dividend at not more than the
present 8 per cent., and adhere steadily to the policy of
building up the institution. The old Bank of South
Australia had nominally £1,000,000 capital and reserves,
and the Commercial of S.A. about £500,000, and in the
days of their greatness the Bank of Adelaide was almost
as large as it is now. Since the two first-named have
died there is a considerable gap to fill.

The Colonial Bank (Melbourne) has so far
recovered that it is able to resume the payment of divi-
dends on its ordinary capital. Both ordinary and
preference shares receive 5 per cent, this half-year. The
Colonial Bank hasn’t much ordinary capital left now (only
£133,642) so the dividend isn’t a very large undertaking;
still it marks a distinct improvement in affairs. The
record since the cleaning up took place has been :

The profits are £7 6s. 6d. per cent, per annum on share-

Compared with six months ago note circulation has
increased by about £12,000; bills in circulation by
£38,000 ; deposits by £lO,OOO ; reserve of cash and liquid
securities by £49,000. This latter stands at £546,779
about 19 per cent, of all liabilities to the public. This is
far from being a strong reserve—in fact, it is one of the
weakest. The cash reserve is not below, or not much
below, the average, but as regards a second line of
defence in the shape of State securities there is prac-
tically none, the entire holding of these being £11,845.
The fact that this eminently unsafe policy is
exactly the same which the Colonial and many other
banks followed before 1893 is no recommendation. The
events of 1893 were a very bad advertisement for it. The
Colonial Bank should at ieast keep its new reserve. fund
separately invested in first-class securities, so that it can
be found when wanted. The old reserve fund in ’93 was
mixed up in the general business and couldn’t be found.

The Royal Bank of Australia (Melbourne) con-
tinues its steady progress, each half-yearly report being
a trifle better than its predecessors. This is the recent
record :

This shows very good progress. The earnings are at the
rate of £8 4s. sd. per cent, per annum on the share-
holders’ funds as at the beginning of the half-year, which
is above the average rate earned nowadays by the
banking institutions of the Commonwealth. The earnings
of the last half-year are the largest since the painful
events of 1893, and the amount added to the reserves is
also the largest. Another half-year like the one just
completed will raise the reserves beyond the highest
point which they had attained previous to 1893. In
every respect the last 12 months show a solid improve-
ment in the affairs of the Royal Bank, principal items
being as follows:

The very solid holding of State securities is an especially
good feature. They may deteriorate a little in a time of
slump, but they never fall as mortgages and landed
property do. If the Royal continues its present policy of
small dividends and careful building up of reserves, there
is no reason why it shouldn’t be able some time this half-
century to restore the £150,000 capital which disappeared
in the slump. The fact that it didn’t suspend in ’93 is
good for its prestige, and it isn’t hampered by preference
shares like some of its neighbors
• * * *
The Commercial Bank of Australia holds a
special meeting on May 1 to write down the fully-paid
ordinary shares from £6 to 10s. each, which will provide
something over £1,000,000 to cover losses on the Assets
Trust. This will leave the bank with £2,117,230 of pre-
ference capital and about £lOO,OOO of ordinary capital
paid up, and against this there will be about £1,000,000
of losses still to be provided for out of earnings—on
present calculations. A good deal of this may be re-
covered through the securities of the Assets Trust
realising more than the present valuation, but, again, it
may not. Also, a good deal ought to be recovered by the
repurchase of the depreciated Assets Trust deposits at a
discount, though so far this source of profit appears to
have been a good deal neglected. Still, at the moment there
seems to be about £1,000,000 to be provided (which leaves the
Commercial Bank with about £1,200,000 of capital intact,
over and above all losses). The question which interests
the shareholders is—how long is the providing process
likely to take. Apart from any profits realised by the
sale of properties or by the repurchase of deposits, that
depends entirely on earnings, and nobody knows what
the earnings are. It is a remarkable position, bub no
shareholder has any distinct idea what his own bank is
making. Last half-year the profit was £72,460, less the
amount of the Assets Trust’s loss for the half-year, what-
ever that might be. Judging by the valuation now put
on the Trust’s assets l -which must have some relation to
their earning loss may have been £22,000, and
in that case the net earnings would be £50,000, and after
paying the preference dividend, there would be nearly
£20,000 left with which to reduce the deficit. Things
may have been better, or they may not. The ques-
tion which some indignant person should ask
on May 1 is whether the shareholders are here-
after to know each year, not only how much their
bank earns, but also the profit or loss, as the case may be,
of their Assets Trust—the amount realised each half-year
from all sources (rents, interest, profit on repurchase of
deposits, and profits on sales of property), the amount
going out in interest and management expenses, and the
amount of the deficit still to be provided for so far as the
directors know it. Every paltry building or investment
society is able to show in each balance-sheet how its
deficit has increased or diminished, and the Assets Trust
is an investment society pure and simple, and can do the
same. Now that the amount of the deficit has been
acknowledged there is not even a bad excuse for secrecy.
A curious side-issue is the proposal of the
Commercial Bank directors to change the voting power
of shareholders from the present system (one vote for
each £1 of capital) to one vote for each share—the
individual with a £lO preference share and the one with a
10s. ordinary one to have equal authority. The curious
result is that if the 256,000 ordinary shares, or even
a large majority of them, remain good, or if those which
are forfeited should be re-issued, the ordinary shareholders
will boss the bank, whilp the holders of the 211,000 prefer-
ence shares, which represent nearly 20 times the capital
held by the ordinary shareholders, will be a mere
minority. The idea isn’t based on any democratic prin-
ciple ; why one capitalist with £lOOO at stake and no
liability for future calls should have 2000 votes while
another with £lOOO at stake under similar conditions
should have only 100 is a mystery. The ordinary share-
holder, in fact, hasn’t a penny of capital in the bank.
Not only is all his capital lost but about £900,000 of the
preference capital also, and if the sensible process of at
once writing off all losses was adopted there would be no
ordinary shareholder left.

about its best year up to date on 31st December, 1901:

On Ist January, 1893, the Mutual Life Association had an
assurance fund of £947,722, whilst on 31st December,
1901, the fund stood at £1,533,693. This is eminently
respectable progress. The business for the year just
ended is the largest on the Association’s record, and the
addition to the fund the second largest. Also there has
been a slight decrease in the expense rate :
Expenses Compared with Total Income.
1893 .. £2O 11 0 per cent. 1898 .. £l9 13 2 per cent.
1894 .. 20 16 11 „ 1899 .. 18 17 10 „
1895 .. 19 10 2 „ 1900 .. 20 11 2 „
1896 .. 21 311 „ 1901 .. 19 19 3 „
1897 .. 19 19 7 „
The Mutual Life Association announced, at the beginning
of 1898, that it had'opened a London branch. Business
has certainly increased materially since then, but there
appears to be no permanent reduction in the heavy ex-
pense-rate to make things snore profitable for the policy-
holders. In fact, 1901 shows a higher expense-rate than
either 1895,1898 or 1899, and the expense-rate for 1900 was
still higher. There may be something in the British ex-
pansion policy to bring profits to the Australian policy-
holders, but it is certainly not apparent yet.
The usual and regular fall in the rate of interest-
earnings again asserts itself this year. It isn’t very much
of a fall this time, and there is still abundant margin
between the £3 10s. per cent, assumed to be earned and
the £4 Bs. 9d. per cent actually realised. Still this con-
tinuous decline, from which all offices are suffering, is
not a cheerful thing for policy-holders to contemplate.
The figures in recent years have been as follows :
Interest Earned on Average Funds.
1893 .. £5 7 7 per cent. 1898 .. £4 14 11 per cent.
1894 .. 5 310 „ 1899 .. 410 0 „
~895 ..5 0 2 „ 1900 .. 4 9 0 „
1896 .. 418 2 „ 1901 .. 4 8 9 „
1897 .. 418 7 „
The Mutual Life Association adheres steadily to
the wise practice of making provision as it goes along for
possible depreciation of its mortgages, foreclosed proper-
ties and freehold securities. It includes no dubious or
paper items among its assets. And a specially commend-
able feature is that a very small proportion of its new
accumulations are being invested in mortgages, and the
bulk is going into State and municipal securities. The
Association has always been a solid, reliable, and well-
managed institution, and it continues to live up to the
character.
* * * *
No doubt B.H. Block 10 directors will take the
earliest opportunity to justify to the shareholders their
choice of a general manager. It is putting it very mildly
to say that the appointment has surprised the field.
• * * *
The Corowa Deep Lead mine people have got
coarse gold, and are much elated. They are going to
make a big spoon or spoil a costly horn.
• • * *
Shoalhaven River (N.S.W.) alluvial deposits
have been tried on a mammoth scale by hydraulic sluicing
and by dredging. Ghastly failures have been the result,
but salvation is supposed to be at hand. A patent gold-
saving machine, which is at work, is said to be “ capable
of treating any quantity of washdirt per hour,” and is
saving 75 per cent, of the float-gold. A marvellous
machine, truly ! But more marvellous still would be the
machine which would save all the gold in Shoalhaven
drifts and show a profit.
* * • •
Minister O’Sullivan is to turn the first sod of
the Temora-Wyalong railway on 30th inst. A corres-
pondent suggests that Govt, has no serious intention of
proceeding with the line “ as the goldfield is played out.”
Wyalong may be a one-lode field, but if that lode is not a
permanent one, then there is no such thing in N.S.W.
It is quite a false idea to judge Wyalong by present
working and results. Were the mines on the main lode
worked on the scale and system that prospects warrant
no other equal area in N.S.W. would show greater results.
* » * •
Maorilander Dr. Black’s report on the Latrobe
(Tas.) shale-shows is that the stuff averages about 60 gals,
crude oil per ton —tests range from 44 to 78. He says the
mineral is more like the Russian petroleum shale than the
American, N.S.W., or Scotch shale, having less paraffin
than the latter, and being heavier. From 300 oz. of crude
oil there was recovered 21oz. benzine, 250 z. No. 1 lighting-
oil, 15joz. No. 2 lighting oil, and 105 oz. of Rubricating
oils of various qualities. The estimated costs"'of produc-
tion run from 6|d. per gal. for benzine and lighting-oil to
2s. for the specially-refined heavy lubricating oils. So ,
far little better than surface tests of the beds have been
made. ,
*’* • *
“Critic” Hess on North Mount Lyell: “Its
history is a wonderful tale of unfulfilled promises and .
directorial bundling, and what was at one time ad-
mittedly a promising mining proposition has been well-
nigh ruined by bad finance in Melbourne and London and
gross mismanagement on the property, and unless a won-
derful change takes place in the administration of the
venture in the near future the prospects of the English
shareholders are gloomy in the extreme.” Cockburn,
S.A. Agent-General, has been elected a director, and
Adelaide Critic believes that “from the point of taking
fees he will make an excellent guinea-pig.”
* * * »
Trent, general manager of North Lyell, is
nothing if not trenchant. His latest letters to London
will shock the shareholders. He says that he was
deceived as to the value of the mine. In London he was
told that the average value of the ore was 16 per cent. ;
but his sampling showed it to be less than 8. Still,
“plenty of money could be made therefrom with proper
facilities.” Concentration was essential, but the directors
at first paid no heed to his recommendations, and forced
him to smelt against his own judgment. All the hue-
and-cry about blast furnaces “comes from those who
have worthless flux (?) mines that they want to swindle
the company with.” The directors are told that
for them “to be influenced by a lot of inexperienced
amateur metallurgists, and the other blatant
know-nothings, is ridiculous, obnoxious, and inde-
cent. I do not demand payment for drinking
or gambling, nor for defrauding the company by neglect-
ing my duties. It is all very well to echo malicious
remarks of vindictive and jealous lunatics, and old men
in their dotage. I have been attacked and vilified for
over three months by a newspaper, owned and controlled
by three directors —two of these are members of the
London board. If the co. does not take steps to protect
me I will defend myself. Especially will I defend the
shareholders against such waste, stupidity, and fraud as
they have suffered from.” The letter from which these
extracts are made puts a plain issue to the shareholders—
either they must sack the directors or Trent. They may
find a grain of comfort in Trent’s opinion : “ We have all
the elements of a magnificent enterprise, but it has eaten
its own life, assisted by the greatest of living parasites,
the Mount Lyell Co."
* * * *
From Westralia: Golden Horseshoe directors
have been considering a proposal that any new plant they
may want shall be built at the seaside. Kaufman, one of
the directors, in a circular issued from the office, talks of
another 100 head as justified. On the lease they are
cramped for room. 0. G. Kliig, commissioned by the
Govt, to recommend ore-reducing works for Phillips
River field, breaks new ground. He favors not local
works at all, but State organisation of shipments to
eastern works, with State advances against parcels thus
dealt with. He thinks that thousand-ton lots could be
more cheaply sent round to the Sydney side for treatment
than smelted on the spot. Another shuffle of wardens
and resident magistrates comes on soon. Among the
changes will most likely be the retirement of My-God-I-
am-proud-of-Bigjohn Brown, after very long service to
Groperdom in Parliament and on the bench.
* * * *
Half-yearly report of Ivanhoe Junction, ad-
joining Ivanhoe South Extended on west, isn’t over-
exhilarating. Ore at 500 ft. occurs in small lenses, or
patches, and, where found, averages at most lldwt.,
merely confirming experience of shallower levels. Yet
chairman of Kalgoorlie Consols, still further west, in
proposing the third reconstruction of that wild cat, stated
that the Junction had struck a lode at 600 ft. worth
22dwt., “and, therefore, the chance of making a good
discovery in our ground is distinctly more favorable.”
This blarney had the usual result, and an assessment of
Is. will provide another £lO,OOO to squander.
* * * *
As the English Courts blocked the Paringa
directors from issuing the 10s. reserve shares at 70 per
cent, discount, directors now threaten to reconstruct.
This co. has been an absolute cat from its inception, and
nobody but a fool would put another cent into the ven-
ture. Shareholders were told that Sulman and Picard
had been appointed managers of mine, and they “antici-
pated considerable saving in the cost of treating telluride
ores, and think that the new oil-process may be of great
value in the treatment of ores from the Brown Hill-Iron
Duke lode.” As the Iron Duke is working this lode 300
to 400 ft. east of the Paringa at the same depth—6soft.—at
which the Paringa management alleges that it has it, the
conclusion is obvious. The Paringa has no ore, telluride
or otherwise, on its property except in a leader on
Cassidy Hill. Co. is £26,000 in debt, hence the anxiety
to go to the milch-cow.
* » * *
Algernon Moreing still tries to fool holders of
Associated Northern and Oroya that their salvation lies
in amalgamating with the worked-out Brown Hill, sad-
dled with its made-in-Germany white elephants. There
must be something mesmeric about the air of N.S. Wales,
since four of the leading lights in the Kangaroo market —
viz., Algernon Moreing, O. E. Hogg, Frank Gardner, and
Black Jack Collins, are quondam Cornstalks. An admir-
able quartette truly.
* • * *
Bewick, Moreing, and Co. have now been
permanently put in charge of Lake View Consols,, and, at
present rate of progress, will shortly control every mine
in the State. It is hardly a subject for congratulation,
all things considered. Mr. J. R. M‘Kay, the popular
accountant, as a result of the change, has resigned and is
off to Sydney.
*m* • *
Island Eureka produced another 5070 z. from
380 tons, which ensures a further 3d. div. this month.
From all accounts mine continues to open up well.
Main weakness is that 70,000 out of 80,000 shares were
originally held by a syndicate of four, who are gradually
panning them out ; when they are fairly distributed
market should strengthen.
¥ * * *
First clean-up of Hampton Plains battery, from
stone from the Merry Hampton mine, has taken place,
and the result is such that no particulars are forthcoming.
As this appears to be the show mine of the group, an
early collapse of the venture looks probable.

G-reat Oversight, Bulong, comes along with
another rich parcel, this time from the Welcome syndi-
cate claim. Including dollied gold, 7170 z. were recovered
from 64£ tons. This is the first parcel from the claim in
question, and many more of equal quality are predicted.
* * * *
The diamond-drill bores at the bottom level of
Boulder Main Reef have tested the full width of the lease
without locating anything of value. It looks very much
as if lodes or gold had quite petered out below the 600 ft.
level, as nothing of value has been found under that.
Future of company apparently very gloomy.

Gwalia Consolidated, a re-liash of some half-
dozen duffers, has now migrated to Lake Way district,
where some half-mile square of alleged auriferous land
has been acquired, with 10-head battery. This is another
of Bewick, Moreing’s ventures. •>.
« * * * '
The Boulder mines own 28 Griffin mills in all—
-12 at Perseverance, 10 at Gt. Boulder, and sax at S.
Kalgurli. On an average 50 per cent, of the shafts and
pans are broken monthly by unexploded dynamite and
caps finding their way into the mill. The wear and tear
and loss of time for repairs is enormous and must increase
treatment-cost several shillings a ton, but one never hears
of this. The money goes, all the same.

A COMMON OCCUPATION.
TEACHER (taking necessary particulars of new scholar): “ And what does your father do?”
NEW BOY : “ Please, sir, he yets drunk at Murphy's."
A recent row of some dimensions at
Bathurst (N.S.W.) seems to have arisen in the
first place because an aboriginal found the Irish
lady on whom he was mashed driving out with a
Chinaman and a Jew. Things get a good deal
mixed at times in this rising young country,
though N.S.W. is not a badly-mixed State as
things go hereabouts. A thousand miles further
north you may be hit with a badly-aimed beer-
glass any day because the black man from Goa,
who came with the Malay half-breed into the
Irishman’s public-house, found a brown Dutch
subject from Java trying to spoon with the half-
Chinese barmaid, and swore at all and sundry in
Portuguese, thereby leading to a shindy in ten
languages.
* * • *
The great O’Sullivan, lecturing recently
in Sydney on “The Future of Australia,” casta
blazing eye into futurity, and saw this country, 50
or 100 years hence, with a population of 60 or 70
millions, and the “arbiter and controller of the
Southern world.” It is more likely, unless rain
comes more frequently, to muster some eight or
ten millions with a few billion rabbits, and no
O’Sullivan; and it is always worth considering
that South America may yet want to argue the
point before it is quite blotted out of the Southern
world; still, O’Sullivan’s garish polysyllables
do no harm, and when he whirls his irre-
sponsible limbs in patriotic fervor he raises a
pleasant breeze on a hot day and blows the hats
of his audience out of the door into the street.
Nobody takes the O’Sullivan’s mental flappings
quite seriously, but his energy is refreshing to look
upon in a languid country, and he can get to a
place faster, and say more while he is there, and
get away more suddenly than anybody else in this
country; also he looks fresher, after travelling
500 miles at a cost of £lOO to open a bridge worth
£45, than any other man of his years in these
parts.
* * * *
A returned cohentingenter pours into the
sympathetic ear of a Sydney pro-Gore paper his
wail. It seems he carried his military credentials
to—
every department of the Public Service to be met
everywhere with snubs, and treated in some quarters as
almost a cadger. Finding it impossible to get State
employment as a returned soldier, he at last burnt all his
military references, denied that he had ever been in
Africa, and ultimately got a junior position—ensuring
board and lodging—in a private business.
Good ! “He burnt all his military references.”
We look forward cheerfully to the day when Aus-
tralia as a nation will be burning all its military
references.
• * * •
At a meeting held in Sydney by the
Bushmen who were not able to enlist in the
Federal cohentingent, the chairman consoled the
men by telling them that they might get over to
S’ Africa and join an irregular corps, and con-
cluded : “If they did join an irregular corps they
would meet with all kinds —some queer sorts of
men, lots of whom would ‘blow them out ’ as soon
as any Boer.” Evidently the Bush veldt Buccaneer
business was a very well-understood one officially :
it was even understood that among such ruffians
dog would eat dog.
• * • *
At a welcome-home to some cohen-
tingenters at Carlton, Melbourne, Mayor Gillott
addressing trooper Riddock, who had lost a leg in
the war, said he “felt sure a grateful country—
gallant trooper's noble sacrifice—deep sympathy,’
—&c., &c. Unhappy cripple !Itis a poor divi-
dend on the investment of a limb, the sympathy
of the Sussamuels.
* * * *
Certain Jingo bishops are agitating for a
day of public prayer for “ Peace, if it may be
rightly had.”
We pray Thee, Lord, our Afric strife to cease ;
Through War’s dark clouds let shine Thy light all glad.
But, Lord, we ask not for the balm of Peace
Unless Thou knowest it be rightly had :
In short, we pray Thee end the battle’s din
On one condition, Lord—that we may win.
D.

John Smith, policeman :
I don't like arristing a drunk woman. Gimme the
biggest man in town, I don’t care how bastely drunk, an’
I’m on to him with a willin’ mind, but a drunk woman !
I’d take two bates rather than tackle her. What does
she do ? Ask me something aisy. She’s the very divil.
Look at me nose, mhan. A man lies like a log or wants
to fight, an’ I make meself ready ; but a woman wants to
do both an’ twinty other things at the same time, an’ ye
can’t be prepared. She clats and scrats an’ bites an’
squeals. An’ the crowd sides with her. What is the
worst thing she does? Well, when the divil’s powerful
strong in her she makes love to me—tore, mhan, love. ■ A
drunk woman is the horriblist thing on feet, I tell ye.
REDUCTIO AD ABSURDUM.
MRS. EROSMONT: “ I'm sure I
can't imagine what the men
see in Miss Frail’s figure.
Why, if she were an ace thinner
she'd have no figure at all."
A tough ruling (by N.S.W. Judge Gibson,
sitting as a Supreme Judge) which lawyers are
still chewing : Several attempts, some successful,
had been made at Broken Hill to burn a number
of houses owned by the same man. A constable,
set to watch, saw a woman going to one of the
houses at 2 a. m. with a bottle of kerosene. He
told her he was a miner coming from work, and
she told him something. His Honor (rightly)
threw that out. Later on, when the woman,
having been arrested, knew that her “ miner ” was
a constable, she made further statements to
other officers. ’Sonah (and this is the part
that some find tough) threw those statements out
as well, on the ground that the woman was
possibly influenced in making them by the fact
that she had been duped into making the first
statement. Though undefended, except by the
Judge, the woman was acquitted.
Though a Circuit Court is held at Broken Hill
at least twice a year, it is over four years since a
Supreme Court Judge sat there. Every time a
commission has been issued to either a practising
barrister or a lower-court J udge. Broken Hill,
you see, is “a beastly place to get to.” Moral:
Appoint younger Supreme Court Judges.

The clock of the world moves on. A
M.L. borough council recently got a notice from
the Health Department that a house in which
there had been a case of tuberculosis would have
to be thoroughly disinfected and renovated.
• • * *
The other night, at a little
Italian restaurant off Gfeorge-st., Sydney
(much frequented by artists, musicians and
scribblers), a fierce-eyed Dago undertook,
fora wager, to drink, within seven minutes,
three bottles of wine and three of lemon-
ade. His preparations were awe-inspiring.
To stop the circulation of blood, presum-
ably, he had his arms and legs tightly
bound with handkerchiefs. He got through
all right; but, ’twere best not to pursue
the matter further.
W * • •
That Nowra (N.S.W.) girl of 17
who poisoned herself because she wasn’t
allowed to go to church on a wet Sunday
is surely a perfectly new Australian
development. The awful craving for
church—the church-hunger or kirk-drun-
kenness —is by no means a common
disease in this country.
• • * ♦
There is little hope of the formation
of the proposed Sydney Clerks’ Union :
the same cause which makes a man a
clerk makes him a non-unionist. It. is
difficult to imagine a man, who is willing
to work for 255. a week in a “genteel”
occupation and wear a high collar and
a black coat, whilst there are stones to be
broken or drains to be swept at £2 a
week, having enough spirit to go on strike
for a living wage.
* * * *
London Daily Chronicle having
printed (with an evident deep pride in its
knowledge) the fact that Sydney Parlia-
ment buildings are not of a magnificent
character, an Australian in London gener-
ously supplements the statement
with the information that he has repeatedly
seen scores of interested politicians seated on
the kerbstone outside on summer evenings
smoking their pipes, listening to the speeches,
and expressing applause or dissent. The voices
of the speakers were almost as audible without
as within, and the Labor members, being particu-
larly sturdy of lung, could be clearly followed on
the opposite side of Macquarie-street.
This is, or was, more or less a true bill.
Present writer, who used to lodge in Mac-
quarie-st., nearly opposite Parliament
House, used to have to keep his bedroom-
window shut, to partially exclude the noise
of the late John M‘Elhone, M.P., when he
bawled at the House with 40-bull power.
* * * •
A marrow-freezer :
A butcher, suing his employers for damages in a Vic.
Court, owing to the loss of a couple of fingers snatched
off in a sausage-machine, stated that he didn’t know what
happened to the sausage-meat after the fingers fell into it.
Pass the sausages! Apropos, this writer was supping
once at a street coffee-stall with a noted Sydney cricketer,
and the latter got (as he imagined) a piece of gristle in his
mouth. Closer examination showed it to be the first
joint of a man’s finger, nail and all, which had evidently
been chopped off and swept into the pie. But—horror !
that pie may have been all Man !
A gentle, blue-eyed, flower-faced school-
angel turned up at a little somnolent M.L. town-
ship the other day to vary her humdrum existence
with the usual tale. The “criminal” was a local
resident, who was promptly arrested, but the con-
stable, who seems to have been endowed with
horse-sense, was equally prompt in collaring the
girl and bringing her to a doctor for examination.
And the doctor swore that the girl’s yarn must be
false. In discharging the accused the bench com-
mended the constable’s sense, but didn’t make any
suggestion about the girl. Yet a citizen can’t
hope to always have the benefit of a sensible con-
stable’s action when he runs up against a girl of
this sort.
An information was, aPfew days ago, laid in a
N.S.W. town by a girl who weighs 12st., or
thereabouts, against a young man, who, she
alleged, caught her in the main street as she was
gassing the house where he was boarding, carried
er about 20 yards over a fence, and down a
narrow passage into his room, and committed the
usual assault. No one in the house knew of the
crime until the landlady saw the girl leaving the
premises. The case was dismissed by an unfeel-
ing bench. Evidently simple faith in a lady’s
statement is a, vanishing quantity in N.S.W., and
the age of chivalry is passing away.

connection with the letting of the contract.

A picnic party, mostly girls, recently
returned to an up-country M.L. town and told the
Eolice that one of their number was lost in the
ush, and possibly drowned. They had missed
him towards evening. A search party turned out
and hunted round all night. At 3 a.m. they
got back, to be told that the young man was safe
in bed. While scrambling over a log he had tom
his pants badly, and had slipped home by a back
way. That was all.

The Jones-M‘Kenzie horror, as related in
court by Jones and others, at Lisrnore (N.S.W.),
may be condensed thus : M’Kenzie seduced his
own daughter,. who shot herself with a revolver.
He seduced his other daughter, who afterwards
married Jones. She apparently told Jones, who
thought about it till he went mad and tried to
shoot M’Kenzie, but failed. M'Kenzie shot him-
self rather than go into needless explanations in
court. Jones is shut up as a lunatic. And here,
it is to be hoped, ends the Jones-M’Kenzie
horror.
• * * «
The Port Adelaide highway-robbery case
was only ended last week, when the Full
Court considered whether Chief-Justice Way had
administered the law properly at the trial. He,
of course, thought he had, and it would have
needed Judges Boucaut and Bundey both to differ
from him to upset the conviction. The only
trouble was whether Flynn offered to make a
statement when it’ was technically past his time
for doing so. But the Judges played the game
strictly, and decided that his counsel had “ closed
the case ” before that. So he got 10 years.
Lawson had already received 20, and nobody
seems to trouble about his vehement assertion,
which was possibly quite true, that Flynn was not
in the robbery at all.
• * • *
A Tasmanian paper remarks that someone
has been inquiring into the old and musty yarn of
a schooner Osprey, which traded between San
Francisco and Hongkong and Hobart at the time
when the real Roger Tichhorne was probably
drowned, along with all the other passengers and
crew of the Bella. Seems that there was an
Osprey, which might have picked up a ship-
wrecked Roger, but the captain, in after years,
couldn’t remember such a happening, and, as the
interval between the loss of the Bella and the end
of the Tichborne case was not more than 20 years,
the chances are that the Hobartian Osprey never
brought any Roger to Australia, as was alleged
by that beautiful liar, the Tichborne Claimant,
and his unveracious witness, the late Jean Luie.
Yet there will always be an interest attaching to
genuine Osprey stories until the last student of
the Tichborne case is physically or mentally
defunct. The Claimant’s story of the alleged
rescue was crushed by what seemed to be satisfac-
tory proof that no three-masted schooner Osprey
had been known in Australian waters, or any
other waters, at the time of the Bella disaster.
The Tichborne parties who contested the claim of
the Wagga Wagga butcher, and showed him to
be a liar as regards his evidence re his own iden-
tity, were just as keen on proving that the real
Sir Roger passed out of existence with the Bella
aforesaid, and never got to Australia, anyhow.
They did prove it to the satisfaction of a jury at
the time. But since then the Australian evidence
of a possible Sir Roger’s landing in this country
has grown stronger, without weakening the con-
viction that the Wagga Wagga man was an utter
fraud.
• • * •
There is again serious trouble in Sydney
medical circles on the “boycott” question. A
suburban doctor, against whom a decree of boycott
was promulgated by N.S.W. branch of British
Medical Association for “unprofessional con-
duct” in acting for a certain lodge, summoned
another suburban man to his assistance for an
operation. And the other man, loyal to the
8.M.A., wouldn’t go. Later, he found that three
of the very biggest guns of the association hadn’t
scrupled to assist the boycotted one (and their
own bank funds) by meeting him in consultation,
and now he complains that the B.M. A. spreads
its net for little offenders only, and allows the big
ones to go free. Discussion of the event is causing
much bitterness.

In one Australian State, now perturbed
by various undiscovered murderers, the law’s
sleuthhounds “do in ” their silver by trying to
get inspiration from the various fortune-tellers.
When times are slack the prophets will probably
be run-in under “the vag. just now they are
basking in the favor of Sherlock Holmes and Co.
MARRIAGE IS A LOTTERY IN WHICH
ONE INVARIABLY "DRAWS” SOME-
THING.
A good many returned cohentingenters
have set up in business as barbers. Just imagine
having your head held back by an ex-member of
the Bushveldt Carbineers (exhaling a strong
whisky-breath) as he, firmly grasping your nose,
operated with his razor on your stubbly throat,
the while excitedly describing his combat with the
last six Dutchmen he “ bagged ” ! A trustful and
unimaginative person is the Australee man.
* * • »
“Boko” : The extension of the Sydney
tram system to Little Bay has surely been a
success from a revenue point of view. Every day
sees many travellers, while on Saturdays and
Sundays it is often hard to procure a seat. But it is
a questionable benefit to the hospital to have a
crowd overrunning the place and picnicking in the
grounds. The authorities cannot very well refuse
entrance to relatives; the difficulty is in dis-
crimination —a hospital for infectious diseases is
hardly a place for promiscuous holiday-making.

“ What was her maiden name?” “Her maiden aim was money.”

"TTTEALTHY Widower again willing to risk it with
an agreeable, kind woman of soul. Affinity,
Age Office.
evoked 92 replies. Answering matrimonial advts.
grows into a habit with some women. It is
possible to meet with women who have never
answered a matrimonial ad., but it is rare to find
any who have answered only one. They keep on
answering and meeting strange men till they get
married—or otherwise. Of course, nearly all
women who answer such advts. are willing to try
very temporary marriage indeed. This fact is so
well known that it is rather surprising that
“respectable” daily newspapers should be willing
to publish announcements of the character.

A correspondent complaining of street
noises to a Melbourne paper :
In Sydney the nuisance is beginning to be recognised,
and any milkman or rowdy person yelling on a Sunday is
fined or “ run in ” by the police.
Distant hills are ever green. Sydneyites know
nothing of police action against yellers on Sunday,
or any other day.
A S.A. veteran priest tells this story.
While doing his district he stopped at a small and
unfrequented backblox pub. The domestic,
bustling around excitedly putting things in order
for so distinguished a guest, suddenly asked:
“Are you a Catholic priest, sir?” “Yes, my
dear, I am.” “ Oh, are you really ? lam so glad.”
“So am I!”

“ His assailants knocked him to the
ground again and while in that position bru-
tally maltreated him, the accused in particular
making himself conspicuous in the attack.
He kicked the prostrate man in the face,”
&c. That isn’t the account of how some
alleged Boers treated a wounded Australian
in time of war, but of how a wounded Austra-
lian was treated by other Australians in time
of peace. Though horribly disfigured he
escaped with his life by mere accident. And
an Australian jury, finding these things
proved, recommended the barbarian to mercy
out of its sympathy for outrages of the kind,
as Australian juries are accustomed to do, and
he got off with a light sentence. What is the
good of this country affecting to be shocked at
any other country’s “ atrocities ? ”
* * • V
Government has long since floored the
old wheeze about a man’s home being his
castle, by compelling the householder to keep
his castle in a reasonably clean state, and it is
quite time that it extended its functions to in-
dividuals by requiring them to wash their
clothes and persons occasionally, under pain
of being proclaimed infected areas to be pub-
licly scrubbed and disinfected. One often
sees in Australian streets men—not unem-
ployed—wearing clothes which are a menace
to the public health.
* * * *
As soon as financial prospects permit,
the See Govt, will negotiate with Britain for
the ground on which Sydney Mint now
stands, with the view of adding the site and
buildings to the hospital alongside. It will
be necessary, however, to present Britain with
another site and a structure equally service-
able.

—lO5 hours being a very common thing.

Sydney “Soup Kitchen,” a privately-
