The troubles of Preacher Hay, the gentle-
man whose habit of performing cheap marriages at
matrimonial agencies was judicially alluded to with
some vigor a year or so ago, again helped to break
the drab monotony of Sydney last week. Preacher
Hay, by his own account, paid 255. a week for the
use of three rooms on the premises of a violent and
ill-regulated family; though the family account
was that it had made the mistake of taking in a
fierce and ungovernable pastor on terms which
were not at all commensurate with his ferocity.
The family testified that it only threatened to
have the preacher run-in, and that, in boring holes
in the roof and walls of his room, it hadn’t been
actuated by any evil motive ; it merely wanted, as
a matter of curiosity, to see him performing the
marriage ceremony. It denied pouring ink on the
preacher through the holes, or throwing a jug of
water over him, though it admitted sending sulphur
fumes through his broken window, and pounding a
tambourine outside his premises, and making reflec-
tions on his veracity. It was solid on the fact that
it never battered the aged caretaker of his Sus-
pended Deleterious Church, or some such name,
so that that person would never be in proper
repair again. On the other hand, the preacher
declared that he didn’t try to wreck the sign-
board, that he didn’t throw red ink at the family
on Easter Monday, or hit it eight times with a
chisel, to its serious personal injury; that he
didn’t swear, or kick anybody’s young brother;
but that the family “ took possession of his body ”
and shoved him off his own legally-hired pre-
mises. The legal decision was, that it was all an
incomprehensible cat-fight, and the four cases
were dismissed with un-Christian feelings all
round.
* * • *
“ E.E.” describes an Assyrian duel fought
in a Sydney suburb: —
The two angry combatants are escorted to a park by
their respective seconds, who pray aloud on their way.
On arrival at the selected spot (it is an early morning
function), the combatants, who are armed with sticks,
kneel down and pray for about five minutes. When the
prayer is finished, the seconds lift them off their knees,
take them about eleven paces apart, and sool them at one
another like bull-terriers. On being released, the com-
batants yell and belt one another across the head with
their sticks. When one of the duellists falls stunned, his
seconds carry him home, but the victor is left in the
park, where he prays aloud —presumably for the battered
one’s recovery.

CHOICE.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
That man who has no smaller earth
Can claim the world his home,
For it will yield him life and place
Wherever he shall roam ;
The man who feels no foolish love
Shall never see its end,
And he, alone with all the world,
Can make the world his friend.
Yet for my choice I could not live
Without— however free —
The dearer ties that life can give,
The love that claimeth me.
My soul protests that it is best,
While I have heart and breath,
To live upon the lips of love
Or mourn upon its death.
HAMER.
ANOTHER BALLAD OF KISSES.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
There are three Kisses woman’s lips do know
That make the Love-song of her blossoming years.
The first—at which her blushes come and go —
The awakening Kiss 1 How golden life appears 1
It is the only Kiss which knows no tears.
The second Kiss, we none of us forget,
Its riot runs through every singing vein,
The Girl and Woman here have duly met
And Love disputes his egipiry with pain.
That second Kiss !—we never feel again.
The third—the Careless Kiss —is ours for aye,
It breathes so sadly that Romance is dead—
A spray of roses and a Summer Day—
And down the hill the golden years have fled—
Yet nothing of this kiss have Poets said.
N.S.W.
HELEN JEROME.

A WOMAN’S LETTER.
My Drab Aran.- Bjdn6r ' April 22 - 1902 '
Some distinguished visitors have patronised
Sydney lately: Sir Arthur Lawley, Gov. of W A
is said to be over for pure love and affection of
his former schoolfellow, Major-General Downe;
and is talking “Stalky and Co.” to Downe,
n D • ! whose objective m Australia is remounts!
Officials have filled the visitor full of Sydney
information ; he has inspected “Cranbrook,” the
new State Govt. House ; done some station visit-
ing; and a harbor excursion with Mr. See as
helmsman. Mr. Seddon, Bun Tuck of M.L.
passed through, as one of his admirers remarked,’
like a theodolite.” Presumably the speaker
meant meteorite, but Bun Tuck wouldn’t know the
difference. Mr. Seddon told “foxes’’about the
Maoris. I hey were as generous to foes as any man
iti j that is true, we must have a fine
set or fiends at the front. Maoris as warriors have
not been tested since the war of some 40 years ago,
when they tortured their prisoners, wounded and
otherwise, in Red Indian fashion. In their tribal
i V m rS j too ’ Prisoners n ot required for slaves were
killed, cooked and eaten. With the memory of
Revs. Grace and Volkner and the gouging-out of
their living eyes still fresh, Mr. Seddon says the
Maoris conducted their tribal wars with humanity.
Wonder what the family of Major von Tempsky
thinks of the Seddon sentiments ?
Bnt Premiers, Governors and such are common
enough ; the unique visitor was Amy Castles,
ui?’Ji wa y, ought certainly to put a few
ballads on her programme. Maorilanders can
appreciate the glories of Italian ring-tailed music
quite as much as N.S. Welshmen, but they would
f 6 cs 6 *'^ le Deal” and the “Last Rose
of Summer” as much as we would appreciate
them. Dam, when he had heard for the first
time the first dozen notes of Amy’s song, said, in
his own Doric, of “I hear now the voice
of a celebrity.”
Dr. Tidswell, Govt. MicroWSiogist, was married
k° -Miss Edith Millie Jones, a daughter of
the Hon. Richard Jones, at Homebush. The mar-
nage was as fashionable as Sydney society, money
and frocks could make it; but the honeymoon was
shortened by a paddymelon’s death. Married on
Thursday, dissecting bubonic paddymelons, other-
wise kangaroo-rats on Saturday—that’s Dr. Tids-
well, bridegroom and devoted man of science. The
Zoo has been closed for a fortnight. Plague was
introduced into the gardens by rats which for
uaany years past have helped the captive
animals eat Govt, rations. The rats are quite
tame and trot about in public view with perfect
unconcern in the lions’ and tigers’ cages. They
lookjupon themselves evidently as favored exhibits.
On Saturday last Miss Emily Evans, 8.A., was
decorated with the degree of LL.B., at the hands
of the Chancellor of Sydney University. Before
Miss Evans can enter the profession, however,
Parliament will have to be asked to give her a
special permit. Both the Judges and members of
the Bar will view this new introduction with awe.
A pretty and very proper Society lady was
walking through certain Botanical Gardens on
a recent morning. She lingered to admire a rose-
bush covered with beautiful flowers, when a
gardener asked her if she would like one. The
lady assented, and the man cut her a rose from
the bush. Some weeks later the same lady was
again passing through those Gardens, and, as she
stood admiring a cluster of carnations, the gar-
dener made his appearance on the scene. He in-
stantly recognised her and stopped. “Excuse
me, mum,” he said politely, “but I’m sorry I
can’t give you a flower to-day. You see, the boss
is about. But I’ll tell you. I’ve got a garden of
my own at ■, and I’ll bring you a bunch of
roses if you’ll meet me to-morrow night in front
of the G.P.O. at 8 o’clock.”
The exhibition of pictures of old Sydney closed
on Saturday, after drawing crowds daily for a
month. It was an interesting show, and some of
the 145 pictures were well-painted studies of
places that Mr. O’Sullivan is going to improve
off the face of the State. Deep cuttings, sheer
stone walls, houses perched aloft with wooden
ladders leading from doors ; women gossiping at
gates ; ragged children, fowls, and goats about the
lane-ways. There has been a wonderful unanimity
of opinion as to the admission of the William
Goat in the studies : Valiant, long-bearded, fear-
somely horned, he stands in gutters and on veran-
dahs and spoils for a fight. Most of the artists
seem to have painted these pictures when old
Sydney was doing its washing, for most of the
cottages have a wash on a line, stretched between,
say, a gatepost and a tree. It is a decorous wash
all the time—a quilt, two towels, and an onion-bag
is the “hang out” of my recollection. Govt,
bought 14 pictures, at a cost of £235, off artists
Ashton, Leist, Long, Spong, Backhouse, Alice
Muskett and Stuart Wilson. Outside the State
purchases there was a solitary little one guinea
study bought. Verily, the crowd that visited the
ex. doesn’t want mementoes of its dark, historic
places. Dr. Andrew Houison lent a pen-colored
engraving of views of Sydney as she was 70
years ago. The insurance on these was £250, so
the ex-president of our Historical Soc. must value
them.
•In Chicago(U.S.A.) the domestic servants have
formed a union. They demand 8-hour shifts,
“ company ” in the kitchen at nights, days off
each week, and definitely-prescribed work. The
demand for domestic servants far exceeds supply.
The Irish won’t fill the bill—now they rule the
cities. The Italians are there, but they cannot
adapt themselves to the duties, the Germans work
for themselves, the Swedes are too few, the Japs
won’t go round, and the Labor unions won’t let in
the Chinaman. At our Sydney State Registry
office I saw the manageress and a worried-looking
lady walk into the servants’ waiting - room
where about 20 cooks, “generals,” and house-
maids sat in stolid silence “waiting engagements.”
“ Will one of you girls go to Milson’s Point ? ”
asks the former. “ A good place, 12s. a week and
only two ladies in family.” And the damsels
begin to study their boots very earnestly. Would-
be employer said to a suitable-looking woman,
“ Why won’t you take the place?” and got the
answer: “I don’t care to live out of George-street
or Pitt-street; every other plice is too lonely! ”
Fresh Air League Ball occurs on 27th of next
month. The leaguers must be very financial this
year, for they have already in hand as donations,
money for the rent of the hall, music and
decorations. New Governor Rawson will make
his first ball-room appearance at the F.A.L.B.
“ Done For ” sends me this:—
I am very far advanced in consumption, so far that my
case is utterly hopeless. Some 16 months ago I lived in
a South Yarra boarding-house which I left to go into the
country in a vain search of health. I returned to the
city, the other day, terribly exhausted after a long rail-
way ride in hot weather; so done up that on getting
out of the cab at the door of my old location I had to sit
on the kerb to gain breath. I have fallen in weight from
12st. 41b. to 7st. or under, and everybody can see it. I
have doom written on my face, and shudder every time I
find myself in front of a street shop mirror ; so no
wonder the people at my old boarding-house, who were
very kind to me in the days ere their boarders knew so
much about the contagiousness of phthisis, wouldn’t take
me in. I don’t blame them ; for to receive me would be
to lose all their other guests. I can’t get into any decent
place and no public hospital will find room for me.
It is time the Government hurried up with con-
sumptive sanatoria. There’s a good field for
private philanthropy, or even private enterprise,
in this matter. A cottage with a garden, five or
six rooms to begin with, in a clear, open district;
a nurse or two, and a doctor to call weekly. If
“Done For,” even in his far-gone stage, would
buy a tent, and camp on some high, sunny
ground, there are good odds in favor of at least
his partial recovery, I know of a man who could
hardly crawl, so far gone was he in phthisis.
Doctors said he couldn’t live three months. Then
the man from whom he used to get money to pay
for his room left the neighborhood, and the
phthisicky one was compelled to sleep in the
Domain. That was six years ago, and he looks
pretty strong to-day and can work. But nothing
but a rainy night would induce him to sleep under
a roof.
A price-list of diplomatic uniforms for the
Coronation is to hand from a London firm of
artist-tailors. I find I can get a “ blue superfine
cloth coat, richly embroidered in best gold em-
broidery, lined throughout with silk” for £lOB,
and a “pair of blue trousers, richly laced for £5
155.; a cocked hat, feather and loop for £5 155.,
and a best quality mercurial-gilt diplomatic sword
for £2 155.” Sword-knots and frog-belts are also
quoted. I would like to get some of these things
—the cocked-hat for choice —and wear it when I
write to you. The most humorous item is the
“ diplomatic sword.” It has no business point or
side, you may be sure, and is not to be used on
Boers, or for duelling, but simply for ornament,
and to get between its wearer’s legs and trip him
up.
Writes my Melbourne correspondent:—
“ Flemington sparkled on Saturday with all the
golden glamor of a mid-summer meeting. The
Hopetouns put in an appearance early with their
three lean, aides. The Countess
brightened her pallor with a geranium-pink outfit
—quite a lively hue of fine satin cloth. This was
cut Eton-wise and foamed at the neck into a ruff
of cream. Mrs. Corbet left the V.R. pen for the
afternoon, and chummed with other racy dames.
She wore a hop-green rig-out, a blue chiffon toque
and black ruff with her familiar careless air. The
Gov.-General lawned it with his consort, and
showed up particularly yellow in the light of that
vivid pink frock.
“ The Brough couple had a day of much feting
at Flemington. The Hopetouns made a bee-line
for them when the eminent mummers hove into
sight, and all society gazed with envy as the
Countess turned on her most amiable and pleasant
smile in pump-handling Mrs. Brough. The quar-
tette broke up only after a long and animated
confab. The Naicest followed suit and nearly
broke their necks in a rush to lionise the favored
pair. Mr. and Mrs. Ernest Raleigh closed on
them and bore them off to tea. A popular trio
from Her Majesty’s show watched the mobbing
of the Broughs with a good-natured laugh. Their
troubles about ’lgh Society.
“ A most interesting crowd occupied the stand
in the neighborhood of the V.R. pens. Mrs. E.
Barton, minus the Prime Minister, was a pleasant
figure in an impressive pink muslin frock and
black toque. John Forrest’s wife repeated the
disastrous blend of pinks and heliotrope previously
perpetrated on a smart world. P.M.-G. Drake’s
wife was a large solid figure in brown, with
spacious allotments of a fearful shrimp-pink.
Rupert Clarke incessantly patrolled the Terrace
with the dressy Mrs. Lawson. Black voile, dust-
collecting a yard behind, and broken up with
hurdles of costly lace, was calculated to show-off
Mrs. Lawson’s up-standing figure. Mrs. Frank
Keating, a neat little lady, trotted forth one of
her latest ’Ome confections—a very handsome
white silk, covered up with chiffon and real lace.
Anotherreturned globe-trotter, Miss Claire Wilson,
showed a slighter figure than of yore, and a thinner
face, but she scored for a certain pert prettiness—
accentuated since gondola-swimming—in a navy-
blue voile, a sweet-17 lace collar, and a black hat
backed up with much fine plumage. The wealthy
widow, Mrs. David M’Caughey, whose engage-
ment to a certain much-trumpeted Sydney mili-
tary hero was announced one week and announced
to be off a few weeks later, was a conspicuous
frocker. She wore a cinnamon brown cloth with
Maltese lace collar and fancy silk and furbishings,
and a large girlish cinnamon-colored hat. Mrs.
M'Caughey is the most youthful mother of four
big children. Miss Millie Carter tossed a white
head as gaily as anyone, She was garbed in a
wonderful dress which boasted a rivalry in layers
of exquisite lace and strips of hand-painted
ribbon.
“ Lauri was sandwiched between Carrie Moore
and Maud Chetwynd at the races. Carrie’s lean
figure appeared quite broad in a heavy snuff-
brown frieze with scarlet helpings. The chirpy
Maud was in dancing spirits, and toed the terrace
in a minuet step. She wore blue, with top-story
relief of much guipure.
“The‘Daughters of the Court’ have not yet
got over the effects of the frigid Countess’s
presence at their recent annual meeting. One
very icy incident that has escaped mention thus-
far was Her Matchless Reservety’s refusal of a
cup of tea which a glowing young Daughter
offered her on a tray. The cup that joy had
filled so gaily threatened to run over with the
damsel’s tear when she got out of sight, but the
Countess seemed quite unconscious of having
hurt .anybody’s loyal feelings. The fact is her
constitutional shyness doesn’t allow her to think
of anything but her own uncomfortable position
when she is under close scrutiny at these polite
gatherings. She is a stage-frighted actress who
might get along pretty well if she had a
promptress to whisper instructions and ‘ give her
a word’ occasionally. As ill-luck will have it
Her Bendless Timidity is somewhat deaf, so she
cannot be whispered as she would wish.
“Lady Clarke, wife of our State Governor,
was expected to be present at a meeting of the
Spinners held the other day at ‘ Myoora,’ Toorak.
The members rolled up in large numbers, but no
Lady Clarke. At last came a message from the
waited one saying that she could not, as the
Governor’s wife, visit a private house. Lady
Brassey must have been very incorrect, as
she observed no such rule. It is a pity that
Captain Better-not-Wallington is not on the
State Governor’s staff; for he is the greatest
authority in Australia—barring Lord Hopetoun—
on points of etiquette, and could have saved the
misunderstanding and delay.
* The Quixotic Marshall-Hall has been airing
his views in students’ ciroles as to the dress of
Dairies. As usual, he is downright, humorous,
very much in earnest. The shaggy giant of
musical Melbourne disapproves of the long,
shimmering, diaphanous drapery in vogue among
the stage fairies of the present day. He likes to
see the limbs free of trappings, even the vaguest,
most transparent trappings—but compromises
with the proprieties by favoring the old-fashioned
lamp-shade frills—those rigid starch tiers of silk
and lace that stand out from the hips, and leave
the limbs their honest exposition. If any of the
Marshall-Hall devotees turn stage fairies, we may
expect them to resurrect the brief bright frills
denoted above.
“ Different circles of Melbourne are in a flutter
over the blazing of Hymen’s torch next week.
Toorak bristles up over the swagger knot-tying of
merchant Robert Harper’s daughter with Mr. A.
Taylor, one of several straight-backed, long-
nosed, stiff-necked brothers. Thespians rustle
over the matrimonial stakes of the hardy Jim
M'Evoy, and stylish Evelyn Muret, the fair lady
who exchanges the life of a chorus charmer for
that of elderly, gilded domestic bliss. The third
section. of expectant Melbourne is engaged in the
discussion of young barrister Clive Morrison’s
marriage with a Murchison girl, Miss Dyer, who
makes music better than anyone else in that part of
the State. Young Morrison is the youngest mem-
ber of the Geelong College family which has given
two clever brothers to the world, and is a tall,
lean, blue-eyed youth. The marriage takes place
in .the R.C. church at Murchison, and is to be
quiet. The Morrisons are all Anglican.
“ All last week a constant stream of she-mum-
mers were pilgrimming to a certain drapery estab-
lishment to worship at the shrine of a trousseau.
Excitement ran high.. The costly laces, the
sheeny silks, the choice textures, the dressing-
gowns, the kimonos, the dinner blouses, to say
nothing of the real lace-decked sleeping-gowns,
suggested that all the gold of Midas had been
available to the purchaser. No actress ever
before started on a wedded career in Melbourne
with such a wardrobe. All the sister mummers
are gasping in. wonder. The man’s properties?
Oh, no one is invited to examine them. A
bachelor’s luxury only runs to fancy braces, silk-
reins of leadership.
‘“Dr. Bill’s’ first night (April 19th), tended to
smart frocking neither on the stage nor off.
Mrs. Brough dressed the doctor’s ex-actress
patient for visiting in grey-silk skirt, with
red-velvet stripes and red-velvet jacket, while
indoors she trailed round in pale-blue satin,
built on redingote lines. Miss Temple’s version
of the plumage of a “ frivolity ” houri was all-
red. In real life one rarely meets an actress off
the boards in scarlet or yellow. Mrs. Bland Holt
has been seen at the races in orange-sarsenet, but
white, especially white muslin, seems the most
popular private wear with ladies of the pro-
fession. Of course, they all wear their white
with a difference. Nellie Stewart tricks out
her ‘ blancherie ’ with Frenchy freaks, turquoise
trinkets, and rose-trimmed millinery. On the
other hand, ‘ butter won’t melt in the mouth,’ so
to speak, of Ada Willoughby’s innocent little bit
of white dimity. Grace Palotta and Aadyth
Latimer, also fond of the virginal color, don their
lawns and laces with an eye to more medium
effect.
“Emma Temple had a chance of airing her
figure in some dressy gowns in the ‘ Tyranny of
Tears.’ A pale-mustard silk was a success, also a
white satin, chiffon-edged, was a fair fact, and a
swagger powder-pink evening cloak, cut in points
and bordered with black laoe, was another pleas-
ing truth. Emma has, however, put on some hip-
padding, which worries her terribly. It is s 0
obviously detachable; in fact, this scribe
thought of pneumatic tyres every time the hard
round-tubed padding caught her eye. Miss
Temple, in other respects, adheres to the small-
waisted, primed-up figure, and her dresses fit
faultlessly. But that patent padding made her
look 15 years too old for the character. Mrs.
Brough played the role of secretary in a cornflower-
blue garb, of a severe description ; later in a black
circumscribed evening frock ; and, lastly, in a
Poverty-pointing white muslin. A fair wig was
a real relief—Mrs. Brough is so seldom blonde.
“Last night of the ‘Runaway Girl’ (April
18th) little Maud Chetwynd (Mrs. What’s-her-
name), in Florence Young’s place as the fugitive
convent-miss, looked the school-belle to the life,
thanks to smart, spry feet, and extra-straight
back, but her song-efforts were piteously flat
and faint-hearted. If little Chetwynd had
altered the start of the Runaway’s first song
to ‘l’m only a little dancing girl,’ it would
have packed her pleas for indulgence into a nut-
shell.
Kenningham looked very glum at the ‘ Run-
away’s ’ farewell. He’s docked in his solo allow-
ance nowadays, and Florence Young’s absence
that night spoilt his one vocal chance—the duet,
‘ I think ’twould break my heart! ’ Lauri parted
from the ‘ Runaway ’ in great fettle for waggery
or gaggery—the two words don’t always mean the
same thing. Gags tend to put fellow-mu turners
out in their words, as Hugh Ward found that
night. But Ward, as a funny man, is said to find
no favor in Lauri’s eyes. A while ago, when he
and Maud Chetwynd worked up a ‘cane-dance,’
it was regarded by some as poaching on the Moore-
Lauri preserves.
“In the d.c. on ‘Runaway's’ last night was
John Forrest’s liege-lady, wearing grey-cloth
theatre-cloak, its yoke stamped out in little holes
oyer white satin. With her came her Westralian
visitors—the widow and daughters of her late
brother, one of the Hammersleys. The Misses
Haminersley are tall, rather elegant girls, in a
quiet way, with simple, ‘no-side’ manners. Old
John Forrest, with no bairns of his own, likes
someone young about him, and these nieces have
had a gay time in Melbourne before now under
his wing. Just at present they’re only with the
Forrests for a few days at the Grand, as they are
off to Maoriland, where the elder sister is anxiously
awaited by her bridegroom-elect, Dr. Logan.
The ‘Six Families ’ begin to disperse. Before the
scatter began, the eldest sister of these girls paired
off with a Lefroy and lives near the home of her
youth and the tombs of the Six Families, but her
juniors go farther to fare as well, let us hope.
“ A packed house melted at the ‘ Dorothy ’ first
night. Carrie Moore heaved her shoulders out of a
white satin frock and the left hand box, to applaud
her chums in the show. Blanche Wallace starred
it in the front row of the stalls, where circles and
gods had the chance of admiring her generous
figure and rare white shoulders in an eye-stunning
frock of blue. The theatrical McEvoy party, with
Evelyn Muret its particular pet, fronted the stalls.
There, too, Mrs. Lawson, blazing in diamonds, sat
with her husband. The circle had a ‘swagger’if
less interesting bevy. Miss Hilda Cumming, in a
delicate blue frock and a garniture of pink roses,
was the fairest and the plumpest target for first-
night starers. Dandy Croker’s second daughter,
about to debut, looked lily-fair in pale pink. Her
fair thatch was a charming structure. A Sydney
matron, sporting a Romney curl in straw-coiored
strands and a wonderfully shaded complexion in
rare art tints, was a-glimmer with many iridescent
ornaments in front of the circle.
“ Benjamin Hoare, who commands the Roman
Catholic young men of Victoria and adjacent
States to holy and pious practices when he’s not
leader-writing for the Age, has married a rather
religious lady of Irish parentage, who, however,
leavens her holiness with the humor of her race.
Benjamin is tall, but his new wife is half a head
higher in the world, and, though leagues past
girlhood, beams over with kittenishness.
“ Betrothals are a long-drawn-out matter, as a
rule, in Toorak; and it was a severe shock to see
the marriage notice of Miss May Dempster to one
Mr. Geo. Dunlop Jackson before the announce-
ment of the engagement. The affair has a savour
of romance in it. The marriage blossomed out of
a board-ship acquaintance and the pair were
wedded at the first stopping-place on the line,
which happened to be Bombay. Miss Dempster
was doing the Indian trip with another maiden
sister ; both have nice annuities by the grace and
benevolence of their late brother-in-law, Wesley -
Hall.

A man arrested for using bad language in
the street was brought before a Sydney Bench.
“What’s the man charged with?” enquired the
Magistrate. “The Hutton habit,” replied the
sargint.
Thomas Thompson, aged 92, who died at
Eden (N.S.W.), last week, was, for seven years,
convict-overseer at Norfolk Island (1839-46) under
some rough commandants. He had lived at Eden
for 55 years.
Chief-Justice Darley’s leave of absence and
Justice M. H. Stephen’s retirement will now
bring Justice Owen into the acting N.S.W. C.-J.-
ship. Mr. Owen before missed the C.-J.-ship by a
mere fluke. He was on a tour when Chief-Justice
Martin died, and an offer of the position was
cabled him, but he had left for Australia before
its transmission. Mr. Owen was called to the Bar
in 1859 and was appointed Judge in 1887.

Printery is another refuge for the ancient. This
year Mr. Griffiths, super, of the office, will soon
complete his half-century there. If any man m N.S.W.
knows his work it ought to be A. C. Budge, clerk of the
Executive Council, who has held that dozy billet for 38
years. Astronomer Russell has done his 43rd year ; like an
orderly mathematician he began on Jan. 1. Mr. Caswell,
P.M. and Warden, dates back to ’47 ; of the S.M. s Mr.
Mair has 40 years service and Mr. Edwards 39. Like the
Lands, the Mines is mostly officered by new-chums—
Under-Sec. McLachlan joined in ’69, Geologist Pittman in
77, and Chief-Inspector Slee in ’76.
The election of cold-water Canon Boyce to
be president of the newly-formed N.S.W. British
Empire League spells disaster to that cause.
There can be no British Empire without grog-
shops—and lots of ’em.
At Heidelberg, near Melbourne, three
brothers got married at one gathering the other
week. No explanation of this epidemic was
published, except perhaps the fact that the
brethren were grandsons of Rev. D. Fraser.

seems rather paltry, for they can’t with any
grace give, as a reason for the refusal, that the
affair is mostly I. R. Cohen and T. M. Slattery.

gratulated. But Hobart Mercury took the
congratulations quite seriously.
Prime Minister Barton, when an under-
Si. at Sydney University, won the “Cooper
olarship” No. 1, of £5O, for classics. This
was in 1867. Thirty-five years afterwards, he has
-the unique experience of seeing this same scholar-
ship awarded to his second son.

Judge Casey has determined to see the
Coronation. As a reporter Casey was “great on ”
sights. Weddings and funerals were his choice
functions when a junior on the N.Y. press. As a
member of the Victorian country press his agri-
cultural-show reports were florid masterpieces.
Methuen, the champion unlucky man of
the Boer war, happens to have been the only
British general in the campaign who was a pupil
of the famous and ever-victorious Von Moltke.
Ketchener 'saw service (under Chanzy, on the
Loire) in opposition to Moltke in the war of 1870-
71.
E. W. O’Sullivan now writes verse about
Our own, our native land !
Their much-loved early homes our fathers left, land of
their choice, for thee ;
Yet still count it their proudest boast Britannia’s sons to
be.
Of their choice ? It was a case of Hobson’s choice
with many of ’em ; hence the name Hobson’s Bay.
“ Janny” Leahy, Q. Minister for Railways,
who objects to the wives of Ms.L.A. travelling
gratis on the railways with their husbands, hails
from Cork, Ireland. He has been nearly 30 years
in Queensland, and succeeded so well at country
storekeeping that he is now President of Brisbane
Chamber of Commerce. He is under 50 years of
age.
Lord Tennyson strongly objects to being
snap-shotted —especially with his hat off, for his
thatch is thin—and exciting contests take place
between His Ex. and the photographers for the
illustrated papers. At a recent function he dodged
incessantly, but was at last corralled, for, being
surrounded by the camera brigade, he had to face
one of them.
“Lex ” to The Bulletin :
It is not a fact that Justice Hodges (Vic.) was once a
curate. He studied with a view to taking holy orders,
when a young man, but before passing any examination
he decided, after consultation with Chief Justice Stawell,
to abandon the Church for the Bar, on the ground that
he was afflicted with infirmity of temper.
Talk about humor!
Governor-General Hopetoun, who has
strong ambitions to be useful, is often saddened
by the thought that his physique doesn’t match
his aspirations. “ Here am I,” said he the other
day, “an earl, rich, Governor of the Common-
wealth, and yet what’s the good of it after all ?
I’ve got no ” well, he didn’t say “ bingey ” but
put it in Saxon. It’s a familiar story—the spirit
willing, the flesh weak.
Senator Symon is almost as well acquainted
with Shakespeare as with his law-books, and is
quite as apt at quotation from him. For one
thing, he rigidly adheres to the rule which reli-
giously excludes any reference to his office work
from his beautiful country home at “Manoah,” on
the hills near Adelaide, whereas Shakespeare has
free access into his law chambers.
King O’Malley, Tasmania’s picturesque
contribution to the House of Reps., is said to have
inaugurated a religious sect in America, in his
book-canvassing days there. The sect is known
as the “Water Lilies.” O’Malley looks like a
man who could and might inaugurate a new
religion, and “Water Lilies ” is something like the
title a man like him would think of.
Macnamara, hanged the other day in
Melbourne, had tried to burn his wife alive in
their wooden cottage iii order to recover the £250
for which they were jointly insured—the money
payable to either of them on the death of the
other. Now, he having been officially “ scragged”
for this she gets the £250. Which seems to have
a peculiar fitness about it somewhere.
A pretty close approach to perpetual-
motion was attained by a Mr. Beverley, of
Dunedin (M.L.) in 1860. He invented a clock
which worked without any other impulse than
that derived from temperature-changes. A glass
cylinder filled with air, resting on a surface of
castor oil, supplies the power, every change in
temperature changing the pressure. The force thus
generated is taken-up by an ingenious mechanical
contrivance and carried to the works. The clock
has been going for over 40 years and will probably
keep on until it wears out.

Meyer Blashki, a young Australian, late of
Sydney, seems to have impressed New York, per
medium of a landscape shown by him at the Lotos
Club exhibition. The local Journal, of March 4,
prints a column appreciation of the “new man in
our art,” by Henri Pene du Bois. Certainly
H. P. D. Bois, as a writer, is an unspeakable
driveller —a dealer in the worst sort of pretentious
shoddy—but he may know more of art than of
letters.

paringa Racing Club, who stands for a delight-
fully Tory S.A. Council district, described the
Household Suffrage Bill, in turf vernacular, as “ a
black gelding by Kingston out of Bluff.” His
general views on politics are just about as lumin-
ous as that, but he is “a jolly good fellow” with
countless admirers, and so is likely to win on the
ancient Tory stallion Selfishness by Impudence
out of Rapacity.

A re marque proof of Tom Roberts
picture of the opening of the Federal Parliament
has been ordered by the Prince of Wales—which
is an excellent poster advt. for the venture.
Roberts’ canvas is 17ft. by 10, into which space
he has contracted to crowd all the Senators,
Reps., and distinguished visitors. Melb. Age
says it promises to be an artistic success. At best
it can only be a clever solution of a problem, but

Dean O’Haran’s show for the Bendigo
(Vic.) see “is thought good.” Dr. Reville, the
retiring bishop, is a many years’ friend, and as the
Cardinal and Dr. O’Haran will be in Rome when

fluences (if the Cardinal has real influence) are

A venerable Protestant Dean (now defunct)
of an Australian capital hailed originally from
“ the Black North,” and was vividly anti-Roman.
He met Brown, one day, who told him of Jones’
secession from “ the Church ” that he had verted
to Roman Catholicism. Gloomily the Dean
shook his head. Next day again he and Brown
met, and the latter explained that Jones after
leaving “ the Church ” had not become a Holy
Roman, but an Atheist. “Thank God! came
from the full heart of the relieved Dean.
From a Tommy’s letter (not about the
Buccaneers—the regulars this time) :
“ I went to see Commandant Scheepers shot last Satur-
day at three o’clock. They brought him from towiyn an
ambulance van with a band playing, and the firing party
following behind. When they got him to his grave he
begged to be allowed to stand up and face death. Rut
they tied him down in a chair, and blindfolded him, and
15 of the Coldstream Guards stood ten paces from him
and fired. It almost blew one side of him away, and it
was a sickening sight. He must have been a brave man.
He did not flinch, nor turn pale. They buried him as he
was, and broke the chair up, throwing the pieces on top
of him.”

If report speaks truly, Gov-
ernor Havelock, of Tasmania,
does not intend to be a Governor
in name only ; he seems dangerously
inclined to take himself as a ruler quite
seriously. The other day he is said to
have soundly rated a prisoner working
in Govt. House grounds for not toiling
hard enough. The man explained that he was
Buffering from a jarred hand or something. The
Gov. evidently considered this a mere tale (as it
possibly was), and ordered the doctor to examine
the alleged hurt. The doctor confirmed the
Gov.’s suspicions, and the prisoner was punished
for malingering. Minus the cat, it reads like h
chapter of old-time history.

The American methods in the Philippines
seem to far out-ketch Ketchener. Mr. J. A.
Hobson, in London Daily News, charges :
Devastation and reconcentration, the death penalty for
any aid or comfort to an enemy, and even, as serious wit-
nesses allege, the deliberate and frequent use of torture
to extract evidence, have all been transferred from
Spanish to American practice along with the transference
of a policy of national obliteration.
And sworn evidence before an American commit-
tee of inquiry seems to bear out Mr. Hobson’s
charges.
Dear Bulletin. — Some enthusiastic idiot
has been pulling the legs of the Sydney morning
papers, even to the length of a column apiece, anent
a wonderful new locomotive valve-gear, and now
writer smiles to see Bulletin (12th inst.) also drawn
in. Excitement began with London Times, Feb. 20,
when Mr. Cautley, M.P., announced to a breath-
less world that “ we are on the eve of a mechanical
revolution such as has never been seen since the
introduction of steam.” Bare fact is that valves,
working on principles old in stationary and marine
engineering, have been applied to locomotives, and
not for the first time either; and a small economy
has resulted. Steam engineers familiar with
thermodynamics are pleased to get a small
economy, because it is all they expect, but it is no
use to offer the public less than a Revolution every
time. — Herbert R.
The execution of arsonist Macnamara at
Melbourne was a shapeless and disorganised epi-
sode. A little over an hour before the “ dull,
sickening thud ” was to eventuate, Macnamara
ran across his cell and violently banged his head
against the barred door, inflicting a serious wound
and causing unconsciousness. Spur doctors were
hastily summoned, but for long it looked as if the
law would have to hang an unconscious man or
postpone the function. And the law hates to nang
anybody while he is in merciful oblivion. But the
unwearied efforts of science prevailed, and at the
appointed time a patched and plastered criminal,
with knocking knees and ashen face, was sup-
ported by two warders on to the stage to perform
his part before a select audience of about 30.
Related —was it of Jay Gould 1- that his
handwriting was so bad as to be illegible to the
old man himself. A letter of his replying to an
application for employment on one of his numer-
ous railways was, on the strength of the signature,
used by the recipient as a pass over the line for
many years. In later days the typewriter has
done much to prevent fraud and mistakes formerly
traceable to bad writing. Had J.G., like The
Bulletin, used a “ New Century ’ machine his
reputation as a letter-writer would be better to-
day New Century Typewriters, the latest from
Yankee-land, are sold by the United Typewriter
and Supplies Cos., of Sydney, Melbourne and
Perth.*

MALLEE-COOKING IN SUMMER.
“ Didn't I tell ye to turn this meat !"
“ W’y, mother, I’m baked dry myself :
“A.G.F.”: During this severe drought
the ground has cracked to such an extent on what
were swampy places on this station (Wilberoi,
N.S.W.), that I often find sheep dead and dying,
stuck in the cracks, same as if they were bogged.
Have noticed during this awful season the common
black-and-white magpie picking the eyes out of
sheep that were not able to get up. I never saw
it before ; has any other man on the land observed
it?
* * * *
“ Scotty the Wrinkler ” re the human-
ostrich par., B. 12/4/’O2 :
It is as well to mention names, dates, and facts, so
here goes : I have seen several army officers eat up wine-
glasses, notably CElrichs, 35th Foot, at Mooltan (1866). I
backed him to eat a lamp-shade, if he’d tackle it. In
1873, at table, in a Macquarie-st., Sydney, house, I made
that statement, and the house-mother publicly derided
me, but, referring to Mr. L. A. Fosbery, late of
the R.N., now of Wagga, she was informed that he had
often seen it done. Old Kilmartin, a Riverina fencer, con-
stantly ate glass for liquor. He was named the “ Glass-
eater.” There is a myth that ground-glass will kill dogs;
another, that it will kill worms in dogs ; still another,
that it will make dogs fight— all myths.
* * * •
M.L. Govt., which professes to discounte-
nance the exportation of Maori curios, was
recently offered the magnificent collection of
Major General Robley in England, but didn’t
even reply to the offer. Robley has now sold
elsewhere at a much higher figure. He still has a
gruesome collection of 32 preserved Maori heads,
elaborately tattooed, for which a Yankee museum
is in treaty.
“ R.R.R.” : “ Tahune Linah’s ” par re
human ostrioh (8., 12/4/’O2), reminds me of the
man who, in Southern Riverina, about 10 years
ago, gave himself up to the police, saying that he
had tried to suicide by swallowing a lot of broken
glass. He was taken 25 miles to the nearest
doctor, who, though incredulous, put the patient
on a diet of bread and cheese, with very little
water, and then, after a few days, administered a
large dose of castor-oil. Result, 60-odd pieces of
broken bottle. Fact. The man was afterwards
tried at Deniliquin for attempted suicide.
* * * *
A voice which can’t be disregarded begins
to make itself heard in Maoriland. The curse of
the North Island bush settler will shortly disturb
the jingo Premier in his sleep. Owing to the cost
of cohentingenting not only has the making of
roads been stopped, but those that are already
made are falling into disrepair, and the bush
backblock settler finds difficulty in getting his
produce to the railway. The North Island is a
country in which a road has to be heavily metalled
every year or in winter it becomes impassable and
the lonely settler and his team—wagon, produce
and everything—simply vanish, only to be found
next summer when the sun has dried the mud and
the loud nor’-wester has swept it away in clouds,
and left it in drifts along the edge of the nearest
bush.
» * * *
This, from a London paper, will interest
Australian drovers:
A stampede of cattle can usually be stopped by strewing
some salt on the ground in front of the cattle. When the
cattle reach the salt they invariably stop and lick the
ground where the salt is.
They have frequently been stopped by this method in
South America.
Please note that the salt is to be put in front of
the cattle—not on their tails.
» * * •
Examination of a large number of moa
bones found in a swamp near Wanganui River
(M.L.) tended to show that the big bird has not
been so long extinct as has been supposed. The
bones were found under 3ft. of bog earth, in a
layer Ift. 6in. in thickness, underlain by a foot of
river deposit, and natural grease was found in
many of them. They were mostly leg-bones, and
it is surmised that a large number of the birds got
bogged in the flood-deposit, their bodies, as they
decayed, being washed away.

“Fortuna”: Re animals living on “dry-
tack ” and in close confinement. The following
from Captain M‘Clintock’s diary of his voyage in
the Fox may bear reprinting:
A few days ago [Sept., 1858],
a large cask of biscuits was
opened, and a living mouse
discovered therein ! It was
small, but mature in years.
The cask —a strong, watertight
one —was packed on shore, at
Aberdeen, in June, 1857, and
remained ever after unopened ;
there was no hole by which the
mouse could have got in or out;
besides, it is the only one seen
on board. Ship’s biscuit is
certainly dry feeding ; but who
dares assert, after the experi-
ences of our mouse, that it is
not wonderfully nutritious.
“H.J. Re finding the cardinal points
on a cloudy day (“ Cecil P.,” B. 12/4/’O2). It’s a
poor bushman that, in timbered country, can’t
find north and south. The thin-barked, sun-
burned side is invariably due north from an
imaginary line passing through the tree from the
centre of the rough, thick-barked side, which is
always the south side. No blackpheller ever
resorted to “C.P.’s” , penknife and-thumbnail
device. Even on a timberless plain every shrub or
weed has its north side distinctly indicated to a
bushman’s eye.
• « * «
“ J.A.C.” : Re getting the sun on a cloudy
day (“ Cecil P., B. 12/4/’O2). There is a simple
way of getting the cardinal points without a com-
pass if one has a watch. Hold the watch as
nearly horizontal as possible, dial uppermost, and
in the guard-ring insert, say, a pencil, perpen-
dicularly, at right angles to the dial. Then turn
the watch until the shadow cast by the pencil falls
across from the 12 to the 6, and the hour hand
will then point due north, if the watch is correct
time.

“ nlick ” ; How is this to show the fer-
tility of land in the back country with the aid of
a little bore-water and manual labor. At Wal-
gett, last Jan. —with the glass playing round 113
in the shade —a selector who has a drain of bore-
water running through his selection decided to
plough and irrigate 40 acres to grow sorghum.
Within nine weeks he had a crop lift, high, which
he estimates will cut 20 tons to the acre. He will
be able to keep his 5000 sheep on this for at least
four months.
The Boyces, Langleys, and
other lemonade-drinking N.S.
Wales church-builders should
keep in their mind’s eye the
historic bill of the Rev. Richard
Johnson, builder of Sydney’s
first church. Rev. Johnson was
of frugal mind, so he sent his
account for raising the humble
structure to the British Govt.,
and, after years of backstairs-
influenoe, he got paid. His
filed account runs :—“ Paid
workmen 20| gallons spirits,
116ewt. flour, 18|lb. pork, 35Jlb.
beef, 51b. tobacco, 31b. tea.”
Pastor Johnson (his church
paid for in kind, cost a little
over £74) argued that liquor was
local currency, and that if he
waited for sober men to build
a church N.S.W. would never
possess such a structure.
* * * *
Bolting horses in city
streets cause thousands of acci-
dents. A recent invention, a
stiff leather sheath to clasp the
whole of the foreleg of a sta-
tionary horse, preventing the
bending of the knee, promises
to be fairly effectual, but does
not meet the case of the trades-
man’s cart. Too much time
would be wasted.

“ H.P.”: Testimonial sent
to Horsham (Yic.) Times setting
forth the qualifications of a
laborer in the malice, written
by an applicant for a position :
I am a total abstainer, and you
can with confidence leave your
bottle on your shelf. I know how
annoying it is to plant it. I require
no “ tie-backs,” but can do a fair
day’s work if allowed 15 hours a day
to do it in. lam willing to cart
water half a day on Sunday, but
must have the other half to do my
washing. I am a very small eater,
but fond of treacle and pollard.

“C.L.”: Coming down
from the West per C.Q.R., and
getting into the tick-quarantine
area, two ladies entered the carriage, with some
children prettily dressed, for town. One young-
ster kept rubbing its eyes and blinking, till the
mother leant forwards and got something out of
its eyes with the aid of a handkerchief. She also
picked something off the children’s clothes here
and there, and opened her hand to show cattle
ticks! “I can’t keep them off,” she explained.
“We nave a patch of grass near the house, and
the children will play on it. I really think there
is nothing for it but dip them.”
* * * *
Dear Bulletin.—Two true horse yarns.
In ’6B I rode a three-year-old unbroken filly into
Wollindilly River, Burragorang, and when she
got out of her depth she simply walked across on
the bottom. She did the same thing on my re-
crossing the river. Once afterwards, in the Mur-
rumbidgee River, near Queanbeyan, in ’74, an
aged mare I was swimming across sank 14ft. to
the bottom, like a stone, but came up again
instantly and swam across all right.—H. J.
M‘Cooey.
A tree was recently struck by lightning at
Yeppoon (Q.), and an iron bolt, which had
been driven into the timber when young,
was propelled with great force through
the window of an adjacent house—
smashing a pickle-bottle on the table.
• • *
T’other day a Yic. farmer, after bath-
ing in a creek, found that his favorite cow
had eaten his shirt and under-shirt; also
that the leg of his pants was disappearing down
the animal’s throat.
• * *
A strange discovery was made by a Gover-
nor’s Bay (M.L.) settler. While sawing through
a big blue-gum tree, a mass of honeycomb and
dead bees was found in the heart of it. How they
got there is one of Nature’s mysteries.
SKETCH ON MONOINDI ROAD (Q.)
A ring-barking party on the wallaby.
“The M‘C.” : I knew a dog once which
was bitten nine times in two years by venomous
snakes and became poison-imune. The second
time he was bitten he was very bad, and lay in
the river groaning and lapping the water for over
16 hours. After that he suffered very little. I
had him bitten by a captured tiger-snake, and he
went to the river, lay in the water an hour or so,
and then became all right again. The same even-
ing I let loose the snake in a clear spot, and the
dog, in worrying it, was bitten several times but
suffered not at all.
NORTH QUEENSLAND ITEM.
“I want to send a telegram to mg wife."
“Well, write it at that desk,
“ But, yer see, the old woman can't read my writin’. I’d like
you to write it .”
“ C.W.” sends this to The Bulletin
Who has not read of the mobile Boers, each one with a
yard or two of biltong looped lasso-like on his saddle,
with a similar length of twisted tobacco-leaf, a huge pipe,
his weapons—and no more ? He sleeps nightly in a farm-
house, or failing that, in the stable, with his faithful
steed at his side, and at cock-crow is off (after a hearty
breakfast cooked by the vrow and kinder) in search of
fresh kopjes and soldiers new. Now, what would happen
to “ Joe” Boer, if he were a Maoriland contingenter?
They would give him a slouch-hat, a field-service-cap
(which would destroy his sight or get him a sunstroke if
he wore it); two uniforms (one for church-parade), three
shirts, three pairs drawers, three under-shirts, three
cholera-belts (most useful of all), two pairs boots,' two
pairs shoes, a hold-all, a “ housewife ” (for sewing-gear,
etc.), several pairs socks, handkerchiefs galore,
one tin blacking, two boot-brushes, a large knife
on a lanyard to hang round his neck, a tooth-
brush, and stick, an overcoat, three blankets, a
waterproof-sheet, and a sack. For his horse he has saddle
and bridle, surcingle and pad, blanket, picketing-rope
and peg, heel-rope, headstall and rope, brush and curry-
comb, nosebag and hay-nets. Then, with rifle, bayonet,
bucket, bandolier and pouches with 150 rounds of ammu-
nition, haversack containing a tin canteen and a day’s
provisions, water-bottle, hay-nets full, and feed of
oats in his nosebag, it would take an elephant
to hump the lot with any kind of ease. Un-
officially, also, he would have with him other matters
—including, probably, a batch of photos, a ream or so of
letters—and loot. But does he take all this to the front ?
Not he. At the first landing-station, dump goes all but
one blanket, the waterproof sheet, a change of under-
clothing, and the horse necessaries. Could M.L. Govern-
ment but realise this, and also the fact that fortunes are
being made out of the tons and tons of clothing wasted
in S’Africa every month, there would be less trouble in
the despatch of contingents, and considerable diminution
in the little bill Maoriland will have to foot one of these
days.

Mr. P. J. Gillen, reporting on the Spencer-
Gillen expedition :
We saw no cannibalistic ceremonies—unfortunately no
one died during our stay amongst the Gulf tribes.
A Queensland paper,
recording the fact that a
just - sentenced prisoner
hurled his heavy boot at
the head of the presiding
magistrate, winds up
with —“But, fortunately,
the missile only struck a
constable, so no harm
was done.”

The beautiful Maori
idyll of Hinemoa and
Tutanekai is likely to be
exploded as regards loca-
tion. The legend makes
Rotorua and the island
of Mokia the places of
the romantic happen-
ings, but Mr. Stowell
(Hare Kingi) who is col-
lecting information of
historic interest for M.L. Govt., has discovered
that the exact spot was Hawiiki.
* * * ♦ .
“Right Angle” : There is a noticeable
difference between the King Country Maori and
the dweller on the coast. The latter is copper-
color, but the Kinglander inclines to blackness.
The coast native is phlegmatic, thick-set, and has
rounded limbs. The King Country native is full
of bottled energy, quick in movement, and wild-
eyed. His limbs are inclined to flatness. He is,
on an average, longer in the limb and finer at the
extremities. The hair is thick, and mopped like
a Fijian’s. Neither type is particularly trust-
worthy, but, if you have to choose, take the coast
native. Unless driven by necessity, however,
don’t take either.”
A GAOL-WALL INSCRIPTION.
A volley-crack, a puflf of smoke,
And dead the Murderer grins,
-Come, cover with the Charity-cloak
That multitude of sins.
And though some blame and count it shame
I won’t withhold the tear
For the cold heart, the bold heart
That ceased its beating here.
They say his debts he oft forgot,
—But-one he settled up,
They say he used to drink a lot,
—His last was a bitter cup.
And right or wrong, or weak or strong,
I can’t keep back the tear
For the Devil-heart, the revel-heart
That ceased its beating here.
I know he went from bad to worse,
I know what ill he wrought,
But I have seen him on a horse,
And heard of how he fought;
And, fool or wise, I own my eyes
Are troubled with a tear
For the rough heart, the tough heart
That ceased its beating here.
A sorry life of drink and debt,
That finished with the shrift
Men give the murderer, and yet
Was his the singer’s gift ;
A scrap of song ’gainst a world of wrong 1
I know—but here’s a tear
For the Crime-heart, the Rhyme-her
That ceased its beating here.
Some heels may spurn “ The Breaker’s” grave,
Some mouths thereon may spit,
But some have owned to hands that gave
A wreath to even it;
And here’s a meed of poor word-weed
Would fain express the tear
For that Other-heart, that Brother-heart
That ceased its beating here.

23 HUNTER STREET.
“ Trotter ” ; The Kanaka-labor advocate
says the white can’t cut cane in Mackay (N.Q.)
district, owing to the climate. Well, cane-cutting
is now in full swing, and a better climate it would
be hard to find this time of year. A sugaroo,
t’other day, told me that he had never employed
a nig. on nis farm. He had only 20 acres of cane
under cultivation, giving 20 tons per acre. This,
sold at 12s. per ton, gives the respectable yearly
return of £240. Of course, he and his family did
all the work. But, if you do away with the
Kanaka in this district, you have the Chow, Jap,
and Hindu to contend with. This year, most of
the cutting has been done by contract by these
races.
* • • *
“ Alone ” : Have waited patiently for some
of the hundreds of bushmen who have put a boil-
ing billy on the palm of the hand to
set The Bulletin right on the point.
That well-intentioned advice, “Picric
acid is good for the resultant burns,” is
prompted by a misapprehension, for
there would be no resultant burns. If
the quart-pot is boiling on the coals,
with the lid off, anybody may safely
grip it with the ends of the fingers
of one hand (taking care that the
fingers only touch it below the water-
level), and place it on the palm of
the other hand —no hurry necessary,
for no uncomfortable heat will be felt.
The thing has some small scientific
interest, and would be as well eluci-
dated. Have not had a chance
lately to try the experiment with the
lid of billy on, but, by theory, believe
The Bulletin’s recommendation to
have the picric acid ready would be
good then. With the lid off, I have
seen the boiling billy on a dozen
hands besides my own, and it is
astonishing how long it takes the heat to begin
radiating outwards unpleasantly.
• • * *
“The joke I>f the Seddon speech about
letting loose 5000 Maoris on the Boers,” said a
Maorilander tq The Bulletin the other day,
“is that it would be the Boers who would,
metaphorically of course, eat up the Maoris.. The
dusky tattoed gentleman took some blood in the
old days : now he’s only got about enough energy
to take a warm bath. Even at his best the Maori
couldn’t compare with the Boer as a fighter, else
he would never have been worsted by a tiny force
of whites. Nowadays the Maori is an utter
degenerate, who can kill * with his mouth,’ but
never could get a spry enemy to his teeth. If
5000 Maoris went to S’Africa, a commando of
500 Boers would be able to chase them from
Mafeking to Ladysmith.”
The S’African meat-contractors, who made
a million or two at selling meat to the British
troops at lOd. to lid. per lb. for the first year of
the war, and who now have the contract at 7d.
per lb., are practically all Jews —and mostly
foreign Jews. Here are their names: Morris
Bergl (foreign Jew), Samuel Weil (f.J.), David
Harris (Jew), Carl Meyer (f. J.), Carl Hanan (f. J.),
John Stroyan, Benjamin Weil (f.J.), Isaac Lewis
(Jew), Agar Beit (f.J.), and Solomon Joel (f.J.)
Truly this is the war of the Chosen People, on the
Boer’s country and the Englishman’s purse.
This, from a Maorilander in S’Africa, lets
the cat out of the bag a little:—
Many people wonder that so many are in arms against
us, notwithstanding the subtractions which our reports
account for. An explanation may be found in the
exigencies of service. If an officer loses men there will
be a mark against him, unless he can. show that he
inflicted a heavier loss on the enemy. This is not a work
of insuperable difficulty. There will be a check in the
number captured, but who is to say how many were
killed ? They are not supposed to return a list of men
killed unless the bodies have been counted ; but it is
possible to make mistakes. The Oommander-in-Ohief js
not likely to send an inspector to verify the report; so it
generally comes about that we inflict greater loss than
we sustain ourselves.

From an English paper, a 20 years’ old
copy:
To Mr. Chamberlain the Tory party is the fiend incar-
nate, the embodiment of evil, the source of all the ills
that afflict humanity. It is the Tories who are responsible
for cholera in Egypt, for the high price of champagne, for
the slackness of the coal trade, and above all for the
roughness of the English Channel. It is the Tories, too,
who have filled Ireland with anarchy and misery, and
altogether it would be well to sweep such villains from the
face of the earth.

The Federal Government has a poor sense
of its dignity in refusing to pay water-rates on its
offices in Sydney. The Cabinet shouldn’t allow
personal idiosyncrasies to dictate its political
actions.
The Weary Trenwith has the pluck to tell
a few wholesome truths on occasion. A Toora
(Gippsland) deputation caught him en route
t’other day and casually asked for a new road
from Smith’s paddock to the Back of Beyond.
“ Make your own roads,” replied Minister Tren-
with; “pay your rates; and, if they are not
enough, rate higher.”
The Peacock Ministry threatens to beat
the record of J. B. Patterson’s Government as a
“ picnic party.” The other day Lands Minister
Duggan “ inspected ” the Fumina district, accom-
panied by over 100 people, with cooks and camp-
followers and grub galore. A file of Melbourne
artillerymen went ahead to plant tents and pre-
pare the way, and the party, taking a point from,
the Johnnie officers suffering at the front, carried
its cooking-stoves and musical instruments along.
The little outing cost about £450.

LOVE and LANGUAGE.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
Cl AID the English Johnny: “ Your eyes are pools
AJ As deep as a mountain tarn,
Your mouth is a red, soft flower that cools ”
Said the Austral maiden: “ Qarn! ”
Said the English Johnny : “ Your brow inspires
The holiest thoughts that live in us:
’Tis a temple pure, where the sacred fires ”
Said the maiden: “ Wotcher givin’ us!"
Said the English Johnny : “In vain a saint
Such grace from Heaven ivould beg’’—
And then he fell on the earth in a faint
As she murmured, “Don’t pull me leg’.’’

One of the well-known engineers on the Midland

good standing, so what he says for the benefit of

upon as truthful and of value.
27, Bishopstone Road, Gloucester,
Dr. W. S. Rice. July 2i; iOJI. ■
Dear Sir,—l am very pleased to say that I am
cured of Rupture after using your treatment for
three months, having been ruptured for seventeen
ye i have already spent a lot of money on treatment,
having paid one doctor sixteen guineas without
getting much good, and I had lost all hope of being
cured until I read your advertisement in Lloyds
acted strictly on your advice, and I have not been
troubled with my Rupture since the first week I tried
your treatment, and although I get a lot of shaking
about (being a locomotive engine driver on the
Midland Railway), I have not worn a Truss for about
two months, and I do not feel the slightest weakness
now, and am enjoying better health than I have
had for seventeen years.
You can make what use you like of this letter, as
I am anxious for all sufferers to know of your treat-
ment; and I shall advise all my friends afflicted with
this complaint to use it, as I know it will cure per-
manently.—Your faithfully, George Jordan.

“A dame of the Western district, where they
all keep dozens of servants, don’t you know,
recently scored one for her side by showing the
hollowness of the old proverb, ‘ When the cat's
away the mice will play.’ She was lying upstairs
ill in bed, in fact her baby was but a day old,
when, her nurse being somewhere out of the
way, she heard some unusually lively playing
up downstairs. Being a person of great strength
of will, she got up, and, suddenly appearing in the
midst of the amazed staff, sacked six servants on
the spot. Of course, it nearly killed her, but
imagine her feeling of victory as she crept under-
neath the blankets again !
“Lately one of the fashionable votaries of one
of our small creeds introduced a daughter into
the world by means of nothing more potent, than
her own sublime faith. Her fellow-believers
tell the story proudly. She had a bath at
8 o’clock, and the child was ushered into the
world an hour later ;no nurses, no doctors. The
new arrival is a lusty infant, and the mother (one
of Toorak’s wealthiest) was not in the least per-
turbed at the event. This sort of thing happens
not infrequently in Scotch Presbyterian circles in
the Highlands, but the Presbyterians, never
thought of advertising their religion on the
strength of it as an easy one to be born in.
“It has been hinted many times within the last
six weeks that now was the chance for a woman
doctor to make the Women’s Hospital catmittee
sit up and accept one of its own for-many-years-
rejected sex as resident. But the girl doctor wasn’t
taking any. Now comes alo ig Dr. Margaret
Whyte Martell, who has bfeen married and out of
the running these half-dozen years, and to
whom a professional boycott signifies as much
or as little as to hoary and pensioned Dr.
Shields. She’s smart enough,' though. A great
surgeon said of her when she graduated
that not only was she the cleverest sur-
gical student of her years, but the cleverest
for many years. She is a daughter of old Pat
Whyte, who started the Model School in Spring-
street in the fifties, and she has a number of bright
and brainy sisters.
“Macedon residents, or most of them, feel
happier than they have done since ‘ dear Lady
Loch’ left Victoria. The Hopetouns, from the
first, neglected Govt, cottage at Macedon, and
favored firstly, Camperdown, secondly, the most
remote station they could get at Ararat. The
Brasseys spent their holidays falling from the
masthead of their yacht on the high seas, or
tumbling off bikes in Raspberry-land. State-
Governor Clarke and family have fallen in love
with the Macedon place, and, better still, want to
be friendly with their holiday neighbors, and are
making it known that they wish them to call.
“ A popular comedian and a captivating mate
caused a flutter in a Bourke-st. chiffon establish-
ment, the other day. The comedian was delving
deep into his sovereign-case at every counter
at which they anchored. At last he reached the
limit of his purse, or his patience, and was leaving
shopland for ever. But the sprightly siren in
his charge led him on to further destruction
—he had to part with more of his good
earnings at the fur department, where the
saucy one had set covetous eyes and hands
on a muff. He sought then to fly from
the place. But that jolly comedian was
dragged further on his affliction pilgrimage, and
found his final punishment at a port-of-call in the
millinery launching-place where he had to see
several hats on a pretty head ere he was
released from his arduous duties. It was the
best day’s entertainment that the shop-employees
had ever enjoyed—a kind of impromptu matinee.
“ A house-agent tells this tale. A plump, good-
humored Irish matron entered his office, house-
hunting. ‘ Have you any family, madam ? ’ in-
quired agent, putting the usual question, as some
house-owners object to tenants with children
(possibly destructive). ‘Oh, none to spake of,’
the lady replied airily, ‘ only nine little bhoys.’
That’s the spirit for a new country ! No talk of
falling birth-rate if all women took their responsi-
bilities thus gaily.
“ According to one of them, who gravely writes
to inform me, the domestic servants of Toorak are
getting up a petition to His Majesty the King,
numbly praying him to set his face against Sunday
labor. ‘Some time ago,’ writes my correspon-
dent, the cable stated ‘the King gave a dinner-
party on Sunday.’ Gov.-Gen. Hopetoun followed
suit in Cup week with a dinner-party. Then a
heathen woman in Toorak said she would go one
better, and gave the largest ‘ At Home ’ ever seen
in the State on a Sunday. The outcome of this
is that Sunday has become the great visiting day
in Toorak, and some 2000 of us are kept at work
all the seven days round, for your genuine
Toorakienne pays no more attention to the
Sabbath than does a crow.’ I shall be glad to
hear King Tummy’s reply to the petition.
“Melbourne clubs and Toorak boudoirs are
busy speculating on the chances of the best-looking
‘killer’ of smart sassiety capturing the very
wealthy widow whom he is plying with attentions.
What a catch it would be for him, after all these
years philandering and waiting ! There is actually
betting over it in a plutocratic club.
“ Our Rotten Row, alias Alexandra-avenue, re-
mains almost neglected of our Naicest, who keep
away from Yarra’s banks in great crowds. The
river’s associations with red-flag spouters, also its
fearsome smells and frequent dead babies, seem to
kill all toney aspirations. One personage to
use it is our No. 1 Topside Governor, who
comes down from his big house on the hill and
prances on a bob-tailed nag in a lonely canter,
followed by an aide at a respectful distance.
“Lady Braddon is making herself a factor in
our Women’s Edification circles. Whatever has
for its object the advancement of Women’s Morals
or Manners has the Tasmanienne’s support. Yet
she is a shy, timid speaker, nervous at giving forth
her opinions, and always wearing her bonnet-
toque a-tilt to one side, and her pretty hair out of
curl.
“At a Toorak ‘at home,’ the other night, two
smart sisters flourished the Romney ringlet over
the left shoulder, with pink and red rose respec-
tively resting. at the root of the curl. It has
arrived. Hope Mayne wears it in ‘The Wrong
Mr. Wright,’ with good effect. But the costume
must harmonise. As well put champagne in a
china mug as sport the Romney ringlet with a
work-a-day dress.
“ Commandant Hutton, with his wife, Captain
Dangar (the stout wag whose Christian name is
‘ Horace, ’ not Gussie) and Miss Duff, put a bit of
starch into the Princess’s circle the other night.
The Federal military despot viewed the vagaries
of ‘ Sweet and Twenty ’ over his glasses. He wore
a severe look the while, as if inspecting the
‘ ranks,’ and laughed not a whit even during Miss
Vaughan’s irresistible monologue, which she
termed ‘ our little chat.’ But Lady Hutton was
in a pleasant glow of smiles. She has the saving
grace of humor.—Yours affectionately, AkenehL

POLITICAL
POINTS
A section of the farmers in Port Douglas
district, representing about 20,000 tons of cane,
are paying white men £1 per acre for trashing
cane, and getting plenty of them at the price.
Truly they are the Englishmen of Asia,
these Japanese. Their latest proposal is to adopt
Christianity as a nation, “ as likely to be advan-
tageous in political negotiations with the West.”
State aid to religion in N.S.W. in 1901 :
C.E., £2141; R.C., £1100; Presbyterian, £552
and Wesleyan, £450. These are the remnants
from the days when the clergy were a sort of State
civil service.
S. M. Herald does not hold freetrade
bigotedly. Discussing the new British Budget in
which a penny additional is put upon income and
3d. or sd. on breadstuffs, it hails with satisfaction
the assurance that the income-tax will be the first
reduced.
Governors Lawley (W.A.), Clarke (Vic.)
and Tennyson (S.A.) foregathering in Adelaide
the other day, take the first step in a contemplated
new practice. It is being arranged that the State
Governors shall periodically confer, with the
Govemor-Genasal in the chair, in -one capital or
another.
Soutl* Australia has made a political record
—it is the first Australian State to effect a
simultaneous dissolution of Council and Assembly.
All the members of both Houses are to be elected
on Saturday, May 3, and as that is a big race-day,
the Labor party is very anxious concerning the
result, especially as its
followers are showing
extreme apathy. Two
of their meetings in
Adelaide fell through
for l ick of attendance,
more Labor candidates
and reporters being
present at each than
there were voters to listen*
