The last scraps of the White Australia
policy are vanishing. Mr. Barton has now
allowed the pearl-shelling industry of Burns,
Philp & Co. a three-months’ exemption from the
provisions of all anti-colored labor - legislation !
When the three months’ exemption has expired,
Parliament will be out of session, and the Prime
Minister out of the country, so an extension of
the exemption will be a matter of , course.
B. R. Wise’s eulogy of the “ great Eng-
lishman ” (Seddon), at Saturday’s banquet in the
Hotel Australia, Sydney, was, in the principles
it laid down, as nearly as possible the direct con-
tradiction of the anti-Soudan war opinions he
held while under the tutelage of the late Parkes.
Cabled that “ the notorious ” Kruitzinger,
the valiant Boer raider, has been acquitted through
an “intercepted letter showing that the prisoner
reproached Scheepers for burning houses and
otherwise expressed humane and honorable senti-
ments.” So it isn’t “ humane and honorable ” (for
a Boer) to burn houses !
The Victorian Employers’ Federation
tacks this motto to its manifesto : “ Men are the
best judges of their own interests.” And yet the
V.E.F.’s policy is mainly to squelch factory-legis-
lation. “ Men are the best judges of their own
interests ” is an excellent motto for a Society of
Anarchists if looked at in the V.E.F.’s light.
Regarded in another way it is an excellent excuse
for the Vic. Factories Act.
The only clear course for the Federal
Government to follow in re the 57th clause of the
Postal Act is to suppress all gambling practices
making use of post-offices, or to introduce a new
definition discriminating between what it regards
as noxious lotteries and innocent ones, or between
those the profits of which go to individuals, and
those the profits of whioh are devoted to public
purposes, such as charities.
There are vacancies for five members in
Torrens (Adelaide suburban) electorate, and among
the candidates are two Liberals, three Labor, and
two Ass. candidates. The result is very open, and
there is much betting as to whether Opposition-
leader Darling, Premier Jenkins, or Laborite
Coneybeer will head the poll, a feat which will
need at least 9000 votes. Labor-leader Price is
also a candidate, but he will probably get fourth
or fifth place.
The acceptance of an office of profit under
the Crown by a member of the Leg. Assembly
should be promptly followed by the member’s
resignation of his seat. The growing tendency in
N.S.W. is to stick to the seat until the new duties
actually begin. The Speaker has a certain discre-
tion in issuing the writ for election, especially if
the House be in recess, and he seems to drop into
the same view as the member. But a member’s
influence as a member should cease as soon as he
formally accepts a paid billet.
When Victoria decided to abolish poli-
tical influence by making employment in
the civil service dependent on competi-
tive examination instead of on the
possession of an uncle in Parlia-
ment, it made one ghastly mis-
take. “Temporary
employees ” may be
taken on without
passing the neces-
sary examina-
tion ; may re-
main on any
time, get any
salary, and fill
almost any kind
of billet. Parlia-
ment intended,
in its burst of
honest indigna-
tion, merely to
provide that in
any department
an odd-job man
might be em-
ployed for a
few days or
weeks, but it
forgot. In
several depart-
ments the
“temporary ”
staff exceeds
the permanent
staff now, is
better paid,
has been
longerin office,
and pretty
nearly all of it
has an uncle
in Parliament,
or some other
greasy person
breathing in the
large, flushed ear of
authority.
Thus “Kiwi ” from M.L. :
I am a republican out-and-out. But republicanism is
too good a thing to be spoilt by a rabble unworthy of
better things than they have got. The people haven’t as
yet shown themselves worthy of anything better than
pale, attenuated monarchy. Imagine a republic in
England just now 1 Joe Chamberlain would be President,
and the great helpless crowd would be no better off. Or
suppose a republic in Australia. Barton or Reid would
be President 1 A republic in Maoriland would mean
Seddon as President, a calamity which I shudder to think
of. Give me poor, spindle-legged, aimless, dawdling,
good-hearted Ranfurly and “ our glorious Constitution ”
any day before such a republic.
Surely Federal Labor-leader Watson must
be backsliding. David Syme’s illustrated weekly
gave him a page portrait last week, and the
accompanying letter-press was mainly butter
about his “ moderation,” etc. Next day G. H.
Reid, in the House of Reps, eye-glassed Watson
and said more nice things about his fairness and
the assistance he had given to the Freetrade party.
Watson has had a pretty rough time of it this
session. His party is about as united as a bag of
Kilkenny cats on the fiscal and many other
questions.
The Peacock Government’s existence
appears to be a continuous struggle to find a new
excuse for diddling a few more wrecks out
of their old-age pensions, and its success in
this respect is fiendish. The natural
tendency of an old-age-pension system
is for the payments to steadily increase
with the growth of population, but
in Victoria the bill grows smaller at
each fortnightly pay. On the last
eight pay-days the amounts paid
were respectively £10,854, £10,769,
£10,694, £10,558, £10,384, £10,223,
£9931, £9778. Since January, 1872 pen-
sioners have been got rid of. Of these
580 are “suspended” and 1292 are
cancelled. Of the 1292, eight were
proved to have acquired their pen-
sions without being entitled to
them, nine were ejected for drunk-
enness, 107 are now supported by
their children, and a few more
were cut off for tangible reasons,
good or bad, but as regards the
great majority (1059) the report
simply sets forth that their pen-
sions were stopped—apparently
for no reason except to save money.
It comes so near to being mean, dirty
spieling and sneak-thievery that it
is hardly worth while to call it any-
thing else.
This, from the correspondent of
a leading M.L. paper, shows that Jingo loyal
grief at ’Ome is just as insincere and short-
lived as it is here :
The great national memorial to the late Queen hangs
fire hopelessly as ever. Not half the sum needed for the
execution of the design adopted is forthcoming, and
there is no likelihood of any substantial accretion to the
amount subscribed at the outset. The whole affair has
proved a fiasco, and it is most deplorable that, such a
slight should be cast on the memory of our greatest
Sovereign. The only chance appears to be that Parlia-
ment may vote the balance. But that would in itself be
a publication of discreditable failure.
It is cabled that the British Government
calculates on screwing a revenue of £5,000,000 or
more out of the desolated Transvaal during the
coming year. And when the Kruger Government
collected £4,800,000 (it only reached that amount
during one year) by means of a tariff not quite
half as heavy as Tasmania’s —this when the
country was at the height of its prosperity !—the
howl about “iniquitous taxation” disturbed the
globe in its sleep.
Every other male and four out of every
five females have no vote for 9. A. Leg.
Council, though the popular Tory gag is that
there is “practically adult suffrage” for both
State Houses, as there certainly is for both
Federal Houses. The actual figures are: —
Assembly, 76,767 males and 71,682 females ; Coun-
cil, 38,458 males and 13,463 females. There are
6873 fewer males on the Assembly rolls than there
were three years ago.
In the last ten years the six Common-
wealth States have only gained 8335 people by
immigration. This, combined with the diminished
birth-rate, isn’t calculated to realise O’Sullivan’s
wild and whirling vision about a population of
60,000,000 half-a-century hence. Over 3000 of the
immigrants, too, had their passages paid by the
State, and the rest were niggers. N. S. Wales’s
share of the immigrants was 864—and it was the
third best State on the list.
It is pretty evident that the Jenkins taxa-
tion schemes, if agreed to at all, will be consider-
ably modified by the new S. A. Parliament. City
candidates condemn the suggested reduction of
the income-tax exemption to £lOO, and think £l5O
low enough. Country candidates howl against the
land-tax, and the shoppy community protests
that the extra stamp-taxes will “dislocate” busi-
ness. All unite in crying for economy, and are
solid in their objection to any retrenchment which
affects themselves or their constituents.
Hardly any of the S.A. general election
candidates in and around Adelaide have had
decently-attended meetings, Premier Jenkins and
Opposition-Leader Darling excepted. The Labor-
ites, in particular, have given their men scant
encouragement. When 8 p.m. came there were
four candidates, four reporters and four electors
at the Laborites Town Hall meeting ! And two
of the best-known and straightest Labor men—one
an old Parliamentarian—could not get a meeting
at all at their chief centre, North Adelaide.
Premier Seddon, in the course of a recent
lengthy speech at Wellington (M.L.), read an
enormously long letter cursing the Boers which he
had written to the Governor; also, a rather per-
functory epistle from Lord Roberts in answer to
a letter which Seddon had sent him advising him
how to govern the conquered territories. Poor
old Dick does these things quite seriously, too.
His sense of humor has departed with his increas-
ing waistcoat, and he is capable now of
expostulating with the archangel Gabriel about
the inefficient way he blows his trumpet.
Australia is really in a tight place for food,
and may have to import. Despite the great falling
off in the area under cultivation the harvest was
more than enough for the country’s requirements,
but reckless exporting before the situation was
fully understood reduced the stock. Having sent
a lot of wheat to the other end of the world and
sold it cheap, it may yet be necessary to buy the
same or similar wheat dear and drag it back again.
Still the process increases the exports and imports,
and a large class of people measures the country’s
prosperity solely by its exports and imports.
Mr. Seddon, The Bulletin is glad to see,
has the decency to be ashamed of the disgracefully
savage speech recently attributed to him by the
Australian and the Maoriland papers. He says
TOO TRUTHFUL.
MR. BEOWN: “My dear, I have always told you
everything
MRS. B.: “ Yes! that is why I always suspect
you.”
now that it was not he who was so infamously
blood-thirsty as to suggest letting loose cannibal
Maoris on the Boers, but that all the reporters
were so misguided as to put words “ spoken by a
Maori chief, and used metaphorically years ago,”
into his mouth. It is a highly convincing explana-
tion ; but is welcome as showing that, in cold
bUjd, Mr. Seddon does not favor savage massacre.
Senator Henry Dobson, of Tasmania,
writes to the press, saying that he sees “no
objection to Tasmania accepting a loan from the
Federal Government of £lOO,OOO a year without
interest” to keep the island’s finances afloat.
Tasmania sent a considerable body of Freetraders
and low-tariffists to the House of Reps., and their
clamor for low duties helped to leave Tasmania,
Queensland and S.A. in a very bad hole. And'
Dobson sees no reason why Tasmania should not
accept, partly at the expense of these other dis-
tressed States, a loan of £lOO,OOO a year with no
interest and not too good security ! This is really
kind of Dobson.
The cry which really carrred Snow’s
Adelaide Tramway grab through S.A. Parlia-
ment was that the Snow push would set to work
immediately the Bill was passed, and would
thereby supply work for the unemployed during
the coming winter. Everybody who objected was
denounced as an enemy who desired to prevent the
unemployed getting work during the coming
winter. Everybody who doubted was asked what
better scheme he could devise to help the unem-
ployed through the coming winter. Now the Bill
is passed, and nothing is done. Snow is off to
London on various financial missions, and nobody
connected with his scheme seems to care a rap
whether the unemployed starve next winter, and
the winter after that, or not. And the syndicators,
who were damp with tears (till the Bill passed)
over the woes of the unemployed next winter,
are quite dry now.
Before leaving for the Coronation, Premier
Seddon girded uji his bingey and went north and
spake unto his children, the Maoris. He told
them that he intended to establish mounted,
volunteer corps of Maoris all over the island;,
under their own chiefs, trained at Government-
expense, as British officers. The officers would be
elected, and in six months time Seddon hopes to
have 5000 of these new soldiers of the King under
arms. In the event of the white forces being
drafted away for the defence of Kidderminster or
other portions of the Empire, the task of defend-
ing Maoriland would devolve on the Maoris. The
announcement was received with wild cheers; and
Bun Tuck, like a thoughtful mob orator, sat down
to refreshments without making any unpleasant
remarks as to the cost of all these elaborate ideas.
Anyhow, what sort of a place would Maoriland be
to live in if all the able-bodied fighting whites
were withdrawn, and the women and children left
under the care of the Maoris ? Also, when these
mounted Maori corps are formed, Premier Seddon
will find a lot more difficulty in collecting the dog-
tax ; and when the white troops are away storm-
ing Moscow, or driving the Mexican mounted
fleet off the summit of Popocatelpetl, he won’t be
able to collect the dog-tax at all.
If Premier Seddon contrives to build up
his preferential British tariff, and foreign States
retaliate against Maoriland produce, that province
will probably get a violent surprise. Maoriland,
on paper, sells almost everything to England, and
has no need to consider other countries. But it
only does that because it has no lines of steamers
going directly to France, Germany, Italy, &c.
all the supplies that these countries take from
Maoriland go via England, and rank as English
imports. If Maoriland provokes them to dis-
criminate against its goods, it will then find out
for the first time how much it depends on them.
At one time N.S.W. also imagined that
Britain took practically all its exports. Then
French and German steamship lines came to
Sydney, and the goods which France and Ger-
many used to buy through England began to go
direct to those countries, and N.S.W. discovered,
with a shock, that England takes less than half
its goods, and even of that half a great deal is for
foreign countries which have no direct steam
communication with Sydney. The solemn fact
seems to be that England takes, on its own
account, about a fourth of our exports, and the
preferential tariff idea is really a proposal that the
people who take the other three-fourths shall be
deliberately incited to boycott us. That will be
bad for us. It will also be bad for England, which
still makes a handsome profit out of the carriage
of much of the produce which foreign countries
buy from us, to say nothing of what it makes in
the way of middleman’s commission also.

A Sydney Doctor said
it was Dry Pleurisy.
Exhibition House, 29 Drummond Street,
Oarlton, Melbourne, Victoria.
July 15, 1897.
To Mr. W. WEBBER, Proprietor
VITADATIO
Launceston, Tasmania.
Dear Sir, —When your agent arrived at Melbourne, and
claimed to be able to do much good with your VITADATIO
for all who suffer, I really thought he was an impostor.
Previous to his arrival I heard of VITADATIO, so I wrote
to my friends (in fact, my brother and his family) at
Launceston, Tasmania, to know if Mr. Palmer is the
authorised agent, and received a favorable reply. As I
had been ailing for some years with pains in my back,
which doubtless took its rise from a disordered state of
the liver and kidneys, as well as a periodical attack of
pains about the breast, and extending to the shoulder,
the two latter I was told by one doctor was “ Dry
Pleuritis ” (or pleurisy), and after years of suffering and
“treatment,” and no good results, I gave your VITA-
DATIO a trial, and have taken in all half-a-dozen large
bottles. It gives me pleasure to inform you I am quite
released from my complaint, and heartily recommend
the use of VITADATIO to any who may require a reliable
medicine. I have no objection to your publishing this
note.— Yours truly,
a GRIFFITHS.
For further particulars,
S'. A. PALMER,
184 Pitt Street, Sydney.

GROSVENOR STREET
O’Sullivan’s Dream—O’Sullivan’s Polly.
When the State of N.S.W. resumed the Rocks
area of Sydney city it found itself landlord of
some of the most insanitary dog-holes that ever
disgraced a white man’s city. In fact, the
resumption was brought about by the smellful
failure of private landlordism to keep the locality
habitable, when its appalling filthiness came to
light through the plague outbreak, and the smell-
ful failure of the municipal authorities to make
private landlordism any cleaner. So unspeakable
was the state of the whole district that it was
recognised that improvement was impossible ; the
only remedy was complete demolition and rebuild-
ing. _ So the State Govt, resumed it, and is now
considering how to improve it and make the im-
provement pay.
* * * *
Had the O’Seeullivan Govt, paid for the
resumption out of revenue it could have dotted
the whole area over with workmen’s cottages—
roomy, airy, and well apart—and made a hand-
some profit out of the transaction. But it didn’t.
As usual, it paid, or will pay, for all the land out
of loans ; and it will compensate the owners of
the demolished buildings out of loans also ; and
the interest that has accumulated on the compen-
sation money will be paid out of loans ; and
the new structures which replace the demolished
dens, will be paid for out of more loans, and all the
repairs, and the annual paintings and other
expenses will also be defrayed out of loans ; and
the comfort and polish of the district will be
fretted away by five great masses of grinding
interest.
» • * •
Living in the Rocks area are between three
and four thousand people who depend for
an existence on coal-lumping and general
wharf work. They must live in that neigh-
borhood, because their breadwinners must be
near to their work, as they are called up
at all sorts of unseasonable times, and often have
long waits, owing to the uncertainty of the time
of arrival of steamers. Had the O’Seeullivan
Govt, built proper cottage-homes, it would have
: a 1 assured constant tenants. But, owing to
the shiftless, crude manipulation of the financial
part of the business, the ground must be made to
yield a very large revenue and cannot be spared
for workmen’s homes. Therefore the O’Seeullivan
will economise the space for dwelling purposes
and devote the greater portion of the property to
more “profitable” uses. The workers, whose
dens (they cannot be called homes) are about to be
demolished, are to be housed in two huge terrace
structures, at a cost of £190,000. The buildings
are to be five storeys high, built round a court-
yard, “ replete with every modern convenience,
and with the acme of privacy possible in a tene-
ment terrace.”
• * * *
There are several designs, but the best put for-
ward appears to be that of architect Varney
Parkes. If the Govt, adopts the Parkes design,
it will be making the best of a very bad
business. Mr. Parkes’ design provides for a
gigantic skeleton of steel, veneered with glazed
terra-cotta, and faced on the outside with stone.
From turret to foundation there will be nothing
to burn except the furniture, and a general con-
flagration will be impossible. At the corners of
the colossal pile will stand great towers of stone,
the alternate ones containing steel staircases, 15ft.
wide, opening on to the public corridors of every
floor. The public corridors will be merely elevated
sidewalks, running along the front of the structure.
Inside will be miniature gardens protected with
neat iron railings. Behind the gardens will be
the verandahs, and each occupier may have
a six, four, or two-roomed house, whichever
he chooses. It is not expected that the stairs
will be used very much, as the building will be
plentifully provided with lifts. The great virtue
of the Parkes design is that each dwelling has its
own yard, bath-room, and outhouses, also its gas
cooker. Even the little one-room-bed-sitting
apartment has its own bath cabinet. The six,
four, and two-roomed houses are to be let
at 12s. 6d., 10s. 6d. and 7s. 6d. a week respec-
tively. The single rooms with their batb cabinets,
for single men and women, will be in another
building, will have no kitchens or yards, and
they will be let for 3s. 6d. per week.
* » * •
In the great basement will be swimming baths,
a co-operative kitchen, co-operative laundries,
libraries and billiard-rooms. The arrangement
for the removal of refuse is elaborate. Along the
back of each tenement will run a tiny electric
railway, along which will travel at regular inter-
vals rubbish-tumbrils, into which the housewife
can deposit her refuse as it passes. The tumbrils
will automatically discharge their cargo into a
collector, whence it will be removed in the ordi-
nary way. 4
• * • *
So much for the O’Sullivan dream —which
seems to need only one or two improve-
ments to make it perfect. In the centre
of the building there should be a patent
cellular contrivance for burning tobaccoes, and
the tenements should all be connected with
the contrivance by tubes, which could be switched
on at will to the particular cell in which a certain
kind of tobacco is being consumed. A small
indicator-board on the verandah would show from
which tube the thrifty wharf lumper could draw
choice Havanna and the spendthrift puff the
smoke of the local-made plug. Also, by a
penny-in-the-slot contrivance the building might
arrange for a convenient supply of liquor ; the
machine would shoot mixed drinks direct
into the mouth of coalheaver O’Dooley without
giving him the trouble of draining a glass. Prob-
ably as soon as his attention is called to these
matters, Mr. O’Sullivan will provide for them in
the specifications.
* * * *
It is the dream of The O’Sullivan to build a
vast pile that, pyramid-like, will go down solidly
through the crumbling ages as an eternal monu-
ment to O’Sullivan —a people’s palace, on the
verandahs of which the gentle ’longshoreman will
spend his hours of ease in a deck-chair puffing at
the smokeometer and reading the latest maga-
zines, while his wife waters the liliputian garden,
and his daughter inside plays an exquisite little
gem of her own setting on the piano.
* • • *
In reality the building, if ever erected, will
most probably be known as O’Sullivan’s Jug. The
empty garden spaces will be filled with empty
jam-tins (the kids always sell the beer-bottles to
the bottle-O’ man) over which Dooley and Doolan
will roll in a ferocious embrace to decide what
really was trumps. The small boys will take ex-
cursions on the rubbish tumbrils with results that
will swell Coghlan’s juvenile-mortality statistics.
The rubbish-service will be disorganised by the
kids playing Britons and Boers, and the de-railing
of British armored trains. Then passengers in the
lifts will be annoyed by sudden deluges of slops and
showers of fish-heads being flung down the lift-
well because that British armored train is off the
line. The place will smell sweet and fresh till
the more enterprising individualists commence
keeping pigs and fowls in the glazed back-yards ;
and as the paint wears off, and the lumpers strike
matches on the glazed terracotta, the establish-
ment will assume a frowsy appearance, which will
grow gradually worse while the Govt, is trying to
float a loan in London with which to fix the place
up. For the people who are to see that this place
is kept clean and healthy are just the same shift-
less crowd that has never yet kept anything clean
and healthy in Sydney. And even the Hanging
Gardens of Babylon would become an unclean
disgrace without proper supervision.
* * « •
But the chief end will be served. The lumpers
will have got a sort of a shelter, and O’Sullivan
will have got his monument—Facing the Dawn.
SPORTING NOTIONS.
Happenings at Canterbury Park (Sydney) R.C.
meet on Saturday were such as to make the hair of the
toughest turf parasite stand on end. Race after race saw
a big array of non-triers line up, and some of them, to
avoid winning, had to do glaring things that fairly para-
lysed hoary-headed old professional punters and made
even Mo. redden to his ear tips, Still, they were not strong
enough to attract official notice. Instances occurred of
the public rushing and backing nags down to 2 to 1, and,
though heaps of coin found its way into Mo.’s satchels, he
was yelping tens at flag fall. Some of those responsible
for the stiffest of this great foregathering of stiff ’uns
were not ordinary racecourse “ guns,” trainers, jockeys or
the like, but alleged reputable people. Maoriland lepper
Regalia 11. was served up scorchingly hot for the Jumpers’
Flat race and duly won, but not till the throng who laid
the odds had a bad scare, through which, till near the
end, it looked like a possible runaway win by oberon
(30 to 1). Way the coin came labelled Burraneer “ sure ”
for the Canterbury Handicap, and the report he had
romped over Royal Purple in a trial made the nag look
the good thing, but he shaped like a cart horse. Outsider
Pegasus (12 to 1) won, with first favorite (Horace) second,
and well backed Bira third.
* * * •
Told that immediately after the old top-notch
lepper Chelsea had blundered at the stone wall in
Onkaparinga steeplechase and was shot through having
broken his leg, a ghoulish horde of human vultures
rushed the animal and even while life yet remained
knifed off the lower portions of his fore and hind legs
and literally slashed him to pieces in quest of mementoes
from the hide, mane, tail, and jaw. He should never
have been sent for the event seeing that he had to hump
13st. and was anything but steady on his pins.
* » * •
Crack three-year colt Grasspan has been sold to
go to S’Africa. Figure paid probably exceeds 600g5., as
the prad was passed-in at latest Randwick sales when
bidding stuck at 550 gs. Grasspan annexed last V.R.C.
St. Leger, and, ’tis insisted by those who should know
most about him, that but for bad luck both V.R.C. and
A.J.C. Derbies would have been his. He is brother to
Brakpan (the season’s crack 2-year-old), by Grafton from
Piecrust, by Martini-Henry. At different times, V. A.T.C.
Mona Nursery, A.J.C. Easter Stakes, Rosehill Hopetoun
Stakes, A.J.C. New Stakes, and Duff Memorial Stakes
were won by him.
• * *
Turf axiom, “Horses for courses,” has rarely
been more forcibly exemplified than in the case of
Niagara-Welcome 6yr.-old gelding Canadian, which ap-
propriated Shorts Handicap at Canterbury (Sydney),
Saturday last. This nag, though figuring in races for
years, hasn’t been successful anywhere but at Canter-
bury. His first triumph was in a Park Stakes, then he
closed on a double in one afternoon (the Flying and
Shorts Handicaps), and next came latest cop. Each time
he has been backed with heaps of spirit and loads of coin,
and won easily on every occasion.
* * * *
Epsom (Vic.) latest meet produced something
unusual. Austerlitz and Altairs “ weighed"" out ” for
Jumpers’ Flat Race, but reached the post too late to take
part. Respective trainers were fined £lO each—the
maximum. Both nags were in the betting—Austerlitz at
sevens, and Altairs at fives—but as neither was in the
hands of the starter, Mo. had to unload, and thus stand
a nasty knock through having laid a false price against
the ultimate winner (Destroyer) in the belief that Altairs
and Austerlitz would line up. One backer is said to have
supported all three named, and had the satisfaction of
getting his Altairs and Austerlitz money refunded and
collecting on Destroyer at the same time. The difference
between rules of the two States: had the same thing
occurred in N.S.W. backers of Austerlitz and Altairs
would have lost.
* * * •
Utter darned foolishness of horse nomenclature
begins to call loudly for A.J.C. action. Two nags at
recent Canterbury Park were entered as “ You’re-a-Liar ”
and “ You’re Another.” First-named is a three-year-old
colt by His Reverence from Atropos, and t’other is a filly
by the same sire from Blowingstone. Well-known Syd-
ney sport Mark Foy owns both. The latest horror is
“ Here-I-come-with-my-eye -knocked-ont” from Mel-
bourne.
* * * *
S.T.C. entries for May meeting closed Monday
and weren’t satisfactory by a great deal as far as the
lepping events are concerned. Fairly liberally endowed
(£175) Steeple only attracted half-a-dozen, without a
thorough good one among them, while Hurdles induced
10 —added money is 100 sovs. Twenty-six nominated for
May Stakes, 18 for Turf Clbb and 36 for Farewell Handi-
caps ; several top-notchers are included.
* » • »
Famous brood mare Footstep, foaled in ’79 was
destroyed at Fulham Park t’other day through injuries
suffered. Mr. J. E. Savill was the first man to race
Footstep. She was by Countryman from Instep (imp )
whose family is regarded by many as the best Australia
can boast. Aura (dam of Auraria, Aurum and Aurous) is
a daughter of Instep. Another that lived to a reasonable
age was Sandal, who performed better than Aura or
Footstep. Footstep gave the Australian turf Footprint
The Admiral, Port Adelaide, Captain Cook, Roval Ad’-
miral, Fleet Admiral and Footbolt.
Four pious people, bent on securing practical
evidence of the great gambling curse, turned up at
a Maoriland trot and cast disapproving eyes upon
the “machine.” The “ goodies,” after many conscience-
qualms, decided to have a pound on among ’em, “just
to see how the thing worked.” Their horse won easily
and paid £4O, whereupon the excited quartette cabbed
it home, hilariously declaring that the tote wasn’t such
a dreadful thing after all.
• • • *
Yaldburst Stead has had phenomenal luck in
the 0.J.0. Champagne Stakes, Orloff (Stepniak—■
Sortie) who scored t’other day being his tenth successive
winner of that event, and the thirteenth since its estab-
lishment in ’74. Stead’s three first victories were with
Trenton, Maxim and Medallion. That galloping gem,
Cruciform, also notched two races at Christchurch, the
£lOOO Easter Handicap, under 9.9 (7 fur.) and the Tenth
Challenge Stakes, which she won last year. Stead
secured this race in three successive years with Multiform.
* • * •
The hook-nosed are in for a parlous time in
N.Q.; race clubs there are taking steps to completely
shut Mo. out as a menace to tote takings, which are
mainly depended upon to keep things going. Charters
Towers turfmen have taken the initiative and decreed
that backers of the “fee-uld” shall be absentees from
their May meeting. The persecuted are suffering much
anxiety of mind regarding the probable attitude of other
Northern racing bodies.
* * * •
Someone went into the thing and ascertained
that the famous Tucka Tuclra nag Gozo had, during tht
past 10 years, sired 213 winners of 442 races, which meane
£68,100 in prize money. Another Tucka Tucka horse,
Metal (imp.), in eight years got 143 winners of 325 races,
worth £24,950.
» * * *
How the tote helps a club was well shown at
last meeting of Adelaide Racing Club ; £725 in stakes
made a decent programme for an “ off ” afternoon.
£10,400 went through the machine (against £8250 last
year); commission, at 7£ per cent., £780—£65 more than
the stakes! And here are the dividends backers got:—
£lO 165., £7 25., £3175., £lO 55., £5 Bs., and £3 3s.
* * * *
A real sporting event is the (English) National
Hunt Steeple, decided last month (and annually). Distance
four miles and a bit; a lot of it over ploughed land.
Prize, £lOO0 —£825 to winner, and four placed. Open to
all horses that have never won a race of any kind, and
12st. up. The day’s programme had seven events,
between 2 and 5 p.m.—four steeplechases, and the rest
hurdle races 1
* * » »
A mare named Matamataharaki went out the
least fancied in a field of eight for the big mile-and-a-half
handicap, third day of Auckland (M.L.) meet., and just
got there, by half the length of her name.
* * » •
The graceful girl depicted in Fred Deist’s
sketch, printed in this issue, is Miss Kellermann, holder of
the Ladies’ 100yds., quarter-mile and mile championships
of N.S.W. She is the daughter of those well-known
musicians, Mr. Fred and Madame Charbonnet-Kellermann,
and is studying for a dramatic career. Swimming is her
favorite pastime, and Miss Kellermann is doubly lucky
in having been gifted with a passion for a pastime that
gives, in addition to pleasure, grace to the form and
beauty to the complexion. Swimming is an ideal pastime
for a lady studying for a dramatic future. The 100yds.
and quarter-mile championships were won some time ago,
but the mile championship was contested at Hellings’
Domain Baths (Sydney) on Monday week by a youthful
trio—Miss Kellermann, Miss Vera Buttel and Miss Dorothy
Hill. After the first three laps Miss Kellerman shot
away, and won easily by 150yds. in 33min. 49sec. Miss’
Buttel came second in 37min. 1 2-5 sec. This feat beat
the latest English lady’s record by no less than 6min.
• • • »
At inter-State 8-oar race in Adelaide on May
10 all sections of the Commonwealth, bar Tas., will be
represented. Every crew has suffered through with-
drawals and patches. N.S.W. got the hardest knock
owing to stroke M'Gregor dropping out only a few weeks
before the convincing day. Westralians' embrace the
champion four of their section, and look like showing
well, though said to be under ’97 standard. Six of S.A.’s
team are veterans. Bananalanders are confident, and
will have much weight in their shell—two of them scale
over 13st. each. Victorians work strongly, and are getting
fine pace, though team has not been finally chosen.
Selection committee resent the V.R.A. committee’s chip-
ping in and taking the matter of filling vacancies out of
former’s hands.
• • * *
N.S.W. inter-State 8-oar crew shaped well in
their first outing in a racing-shell, Saturday. Most of
them belong to the long-backed and short-legged section
of man, and consequently are built for the game, but
there’s one among them of the spidery order, with limbs
particularly long and lathy, and a trunk somewhat
abbreviated, comparatively. The contrast is consequently
very noticeable. Though crew is doing well now it will
improve the next week or two. N.S.W.’s prospects are
not nearly so gray as a week or two back. Stroke
Hickson owns the record of having won 13 races in
succession.
* • * *
According to English papers, A. C. Maclaren
doesn’t yet quite know whether he will play next season
for Hampshire (his old county) or Lancashire. The
secretary of the Hampshire club on the point “If it
came to a question of finance, Lancashire had plenty of
money behind it, and might outbid Hampshire for
Maclaren’s services.” Yet “Mr.” Maclaren would be
deeply pained if you hinted he was anything else but a
lily-white amateur. What is the English distinction
between amateur and professional ?
* * * *
Abo. Marsh’s deadly bowling was a big factor
in his club’s win of Sydney cricket supremacy. Finishing
struggle saw him send six opponents pavilionwards for
47. His season’s work (9’35 runs per wicket for 58)
stands unequalled. Though much below front-rank form
with the bat, Marsh is no duffer, contributions from him
average 6, with top score at 16. While Marsh’s deliveries
break bewilderingly, he can also send in switchbackers,
but most damage has so far been done by a medium-
paced ball that swerves and instantly breaks from the off
just when almost anything else might be expected.
* * * *
Vic. cricket season concluded on Saturday in an
awful state of muddle, leaving E. Melbourne nominal
winners, by default. First round, for which only two
Saturday afternoons were allowed, brought “ East” and
Oarlton together, a draw resulting. Though Carlton had
the better average, V.C.A. ordered a play-off. Carlton
skipper Worrall couldn’t gather a decent eleven ; so on
Saturday the Easterns scored by default.
• • • *
Sydney first-grade cricket ended Saturday, when
Sydney 0.0. wiped Leichhardt-Balmain out by a wicket
and 115 runs.
* • • *
Plugger Bilmartin had a parlous time making
his M.L. hour and 25-mile records. At 15m. the front
tyre punctured, and he lost 7min. fitting another wheel.
In the 24th mile the back tyre of the motor-tandem came
off, and pacers Sutherland and W. Palmer fell heavily.
Martin just escaped, and was paced by individual riders
until the hour ended. Spite of all, the ’Merkan marvel
covered the 25m. in 52min. 2 2-ssec., and traversed 27m.
1022yds. inside the 60min., thus cutting much off the
prior bests ; he also created a new sm. record for Aus-
tralasia by finishing that distance in 8.48 1 the previous
speediest (9.4-5) was also his.
S’African yellow-skinned bruiser Weiner again
scrapped with Sydney heavy-weight Jack Mason at
Golden Gate A.O. on Saturday night. Previously Weiner
won in seven rounds ; and it looked as if, with some im-
provement, Mason might win in another go. Return
match broke calculations all up, and after a white-heat
journey Weiner finished points to the good each round
till the third, when sustained heavy body punches
reduced Mason to a limp and weary condition that ended
in his being counted out in a state of collapse.
’Murkan bruiser, Jim Scanlan, badly slugged
Victorian “pug.” Sullivan at Sydney Gaiety A 0 last
week, and appropriated the fairly fat purse very easily
Sullivan came to the Gaiety management recommended
by feather champion Mick Walsh, and, looking the thing
in physique, was promptly matched, with disastrous
results to self and pug. business. He proved an utter
duffer, without knowledge or inclination, and thoroughly
scared from the jump. As there wasn’t a hole or a trap-
door to crawl through he “chucked it” in his corner
after round 2. The crowd hooted and screeched its dis-
gust, which was poor satisfaction for the coin parted
with. Old man Jim Barron, who acted as referee, just a
second or two before stripped himself to the waist
evidently anticipating something of a sheolish nature in
the next round, and it never came.
• * • *
“ Fedder ” bruiser “ Micko ” Walsh complained
to Melbourne sporting scribes that referee pu«\ Jack
Baxter, mutually agreed to by both, and apparently even
more acceptable to Walsh than Turner, was there to see
that Turner won, &c. ’Tis a thankless job to referee a
fight 1 Walsh hit low palpably, and, though cautioned at
least a dozen times, continued doing so, and plainly
courted an adverse verdict on a foul. If ever referee
appeared to lean towards one principal more than
another, Baxter did to Walsh, and yet no one in the
position has been more abused from the side most favored.
Having a decision given against him on a foul is certainly
no new experience for Walsh.
* » * »
Inter-State thumping match ’tween Australian
light-weight premier pug Jack McGowan and ex-Broken
Hill miner-bruiser Mitchie brought a big crowd to Sydney
Gaiety A.O. Monday night as it looked like supplying
something uncommon in the way of fight. Each showed
tip-top condition and both were exceptions in the way
of physique—stocky-built, deep-chested, heavy-limbed,
exceptionally muscular, and hard as nails. For the whole
20 rounds only McGowan really fought while Mitchie’s
chief object was apparently to keep as far off possible
danger as the ropes would permit. When he did try to
do anything (which was rarely) his. efforts looked crude
against McGowan’s evasions. When opposed to his own
class Mitchie impresses more than favorably. Every
time he rushed bull-like Mac would elude' adroitly
and see his man flop to the boards on all
fours. If Mitchie found he couldn’t jig far enough
away he’d clinch and hang on like a wild cat. McGowan
got first blood early, and drove in some solid punches
about the stomach and face, besides a couple of telling
uppercuts, one of which might have brought about the
end but for the gong’s sending them to their corners
immediately afterwards. Mitchie scored a few times on
the back of the neck, and thrice on the mouth—all hard
blows, the latter lot bringing blood each time. Ulti-
mately McGown gained the palm on points, of which he
piled up a great stock, but it was a wearying thing. Pro-
bable future opponents of the stunted South Melbourne
giant, are Jack Willis, and George Johns, each of whom
may give him a switchback time.
* * * *
“Stevenson, England’s champion billiardist,’
writes The Bulletin’s London correspondent, “ showed
immeasurable improvement in his recent match with
ex-champion Dawson, to whom he never ‘ gave a show.’
Quick, brilliant and consistent, his hundreds flew up in
minutes, and all his ‘ tail-down ’ nervousness of a year or
so back had absolutely fled. Thorough medical study of
and treatment for his nerves—which so often mar or
make the would-be crack——is said to be the secret of his
success. The ‘funk’ and the ‘cocktail’ at many con-
tests will, in the days to come, be made a hero by study
and treatment of his temperament. Stevenson is York-
shire born and S’African bred, a clean-limbed, fair,
shapely man of about 30.”
* » » *
“Brumby”: Was recently present at a big
Sydney “two-up” school in full swing, and shuddered
when the spinner occasionally licked the “kip”—from
which the pennies are tossed. As each player spins in
turn, and the majority either lick or spit upon the kip,
one can imagine the interchange of microbes that goes
on. The same kip is generally in use for months —and
looks it. What a crop of untold horrors a bacteriologist
might produce from that little bit of wood !
* * * *
Sydney police raided some more at the “witch-
ing hour ” of Saturday last—this time an alleged “ two-
up school” at North Sydney, and something over half-a-
hundred people were bagged for “ heading ’em ” in a
building which precluded all possibility of the “ unco’
guid ” being offended in eye or ear. Meanwhile “ two-
up ’’and “ hazard ” schools flourish in the heart of the
city under the management of back-number pugs, &c.,
who have large well-paid staffs and wear diamonds and
jewellery and boast good bank-balances. Some of these
above gentry easily average a clear hundred a week over and
exes., and big sums are lost and won in all the schools
every night. Though profits are handsome every player
gets a straight deal in the actual gambling; that’s in-
sisted upon under pain of being shut out for varying
periods up to life and all eternity. As all “ schools,” in-
cluding the head one in Sheol, act together in these
cases, would-be swindlers hesitate to take risks.

Cubed and Praises the Remedy.—
Indigestion and Biliousness Banished.
Mr. Samuel Cole, a resident of Woodend,
Vic., furnishes us with an instance of endur-
ance. He writes expressing his gratitude. It
is best told in his own words:—“ I have much
pleasure in writing to tell you of the many
benefits I have derived from the use of your
Dr. Morse’s Indian Root Pills. I have been a
great sufferer for some time with indigestion and
biliousness, for which I tried many patent
medicines, but received no benefit from any of
them until I used your pills. I commenced their
use by taking them according to directions, and
found, after taking three doses, that they had
made a great difference in my health; however,
I continued their use, and have now used several
bottles, and can honestly say they have cured me
of indigestion and biliousness, as I never have
an attack now. Since using these pills, I have
recommended them, and even given them, to
many, and the results have been good. You may
use this letter, as all I have stated in it is the
truth, and if anyone would like to know what
my case was like, I will be only too happy to tell
them.” Dr. Morse’s Indian Root Pills are a
perfect blood purifier, and a positive cure for
biliousness, indigestion, constipation, headaches,
sallow complexion, liver and kidney troubles,
piles, pimples, boils and blotches, and for
female ailments. Sold by chemists and store-
keepers, Is. l£d per bottle, or six bottles 6s. 6d.

In the case of a lad charged at Ballarat
(Yic.) with stealing three books fromashop counter,
the prosecuting police inspector got off the usual
gag— “ Another instance of the pernicious effect
of reading ‘ Deadwood Dick ’ literature, your
worships.” An intelligent bench happened to
ask what the stolen books were, and the clerk
read out, “ Hill’s Oratorical Trainer,” “ Pitman’s
Phonographic Reporter,” and “ How to Dance,”
A boy who will steal books in order to fit himself
for preaching, presswork, and pleasing ladies
deserves to make a success of all three.

There are several advantages in drinking James

getting good drink to obtain.*

Lake View Consols, March return, 6,2050 z. from
6,214 tons, but no particulars are given. Reporters have
received an invitation to view the mine, so that, for the
first time for years, independent opinions will be expressed
as to its workings. Whether the opinions formed on a
casual and hurried visit are worth much is another story.
» • • *
Great Boulder’s March output, 13,6740 z. from
8326 tons. For some reason or other the figures from
this mine have been rather erratic of late.
* * • *
Kalgurli’s output, announced as 29050 z. from
2600 tons—rather disappointing, as a larger tonnage was
promised. Costs given at 235. a ton ex administration-
expenses. Nothing said of mining costs. Extraction
alleged to be 92 per cent., but no one swallows this.
• » * *
Great Boulder Perseverance treated 12,302 tons
last month for 15,8380 z. —its record output. Manager
Nichols and his engineer (Blakesley) leave this week for
an extended trip abroad, and, during their absence, the
mine will be run by the shift boss, and the machinery be
supervised by Blakesley’s brother. This mine is now
experimenting with electric rock-drills, which are alleged
to work at twice the speed of compressed air-drills, with
considerably less horse-power.
* * * »
Owing to the marvellous developments of the
Great Fingall Consols all sorts of rotters have been foisted
on the market under the glitter of that name. _ It should
be remembered that the Fingall Consols underits present
title, and previously as the Consolidated Murchison, has
been over seven years in existence, and that boundaries
were adjusted to take in everything of value. West Fin-
gall, a creation of Bewick, Moreing and Co., is an off-shoot
of the parent mine. Great Fingall South Blocks owes its
existence to Birkbeck, Hoffman and co., and Great Fin-
gall South to the Chaffers co. All three are well worth
leaving alone. Fingall Proprietary is a third reconstruc-
tion of the Cuddingwara, and was promoted by the
Paringa gang. It is situated in an entirely different dis-
trict, and the name is wholly deceiving.
» * » *
The Queen Margaret, Bulong, continues to feel
very sick; last crushing (4840 z. from 768 tons) looks
decidedly ominous. The new 10-head stamps appear
wholly unjustified by developments. Strange how often
an increase of plant precedes a collapse 1
* * * *
Associated management again to be changed.
Mr. Hewitson is to retire shortly, and, in future, to fill
the sinecure billet of consulting-engineer in London. As
Hewitson confirmed Hartmann’s management of Lake
View Consols, and has run the Associated at a loss, his
value as an adviser will not be too obvious to everyone.
Local press alleges that the continuation of the Brown
Hill-Iron Duke chute is in the property, but there is
absolutely no proof yet of the statement.
* * • •
Kaufmann, on recent return to London, told
shareholders of Golden Horseshoe that the mine could
easily maintain an output of 18,000 to 20,0000 z. monthly
for years, and, were there room on the lease for the
stamps, could easily supply another 100 head with ore.
Doubtless the liquidator of the Standard Exploration
Co. would only be too glad to let the Horseshoe have for
a trifle the Golden Treasure’s 50 acres adjoining the
Horseshoe on the south. This was one of Kaufmann’s
own numerous hopeless flotations, which he so con-
veniently overlooks nowadays.
* ♦ * »
At recent meeting of Hannan’s Central Ex-
tended, chairman told shareholders that Kalgoorlie
Electric Power Co. offered to supply power for driving
battery, hoisting and pumping, including pumps and
hoists, at a total cost of £8 4s. 6d. a day, against a present
cost of £20 —a saving of over £4OOO a year. The Poyver
Co., after supplying the trams, will have a large surplus
of power for the mines, and its adoption will apparently
materially reduce costs, especially on small shows.
Hannan’s Central Extended has produced 75000 z. alluvial
gold and is practically a worked-out proposition.
* • • *
Those scoffers who characterise Victoria’s brown
coal deposits as superior fire-resistine material have, just
now, a hard nut to crack in the Great Morwell mine. The
face of this mine is open—is, in fact, a steep cutting;
and back of this are millions of tons of coal. Accidentally
or purposely, someone lighted this face and, as the mine
is idle through a lawsuit, it got a good start before any-
one noticed it, and is now a wall of flame. The directors
put men on at once, but, as these have to hump water
150yds. and then splash it on from buckets, they are not
making much progress ; in fact, they are losing ground.
Brilliant Central (Charters Towers) record for
past half-year: 11,930 tons, value of products, £61,666.
Dividends £35,000. Probable that divs. will be Is. 6d.
per month during current half-year. This sounds well,
but on £5 stone ought not more profit to be made ?
» * * *
Prospectus of the Belleview G.M. Co. of N.W.
Australia glitters with gold. Expert reports that on an
initial expenditure of £4OOO the mine will return a profit
of £37,440 per year, with three years’ work in sight. Ibis
stated that for “ obvious reasons it is not desirable to dis-
close the exact location of the property.” As leases and
water rights have been secured the obviousness is rather
obscure. The Mystery G.M. Co. would seem a more
appropriate title. By the way, one or two of the
Sydneyites connected with the show have earned for
themselves a reputation for credulity—and bad luck.
* * * *
Waihi (M.L.): Total production has now ex
ceeded £2,000,000. The 90-stamp mill is being converted
from dry to wet crushing, consequent on the increasing
percentage of sulphide in the ore and the necessity for
concentration. To maintain past profits there will have
to be an increase in ore-value.
m * * •
The Nelson dredge, Greymouth (M.L.), the
other day, headed the list with 3050 z. for 132 hours’ work.
It is stated that the dredge has months of work on similar
wash. The average recent output of M.L. gold dredges
is given at about 31Joz. per week.
• * * *
The vintage in S.A., now about over, is esti-
mated at about 2J million gallons of wine ; last year, it
was 2f millions, but that was a “record.” The summer
has been unusually cool, also unusually dry, and both
facts told against the productiveness of the grape.
• * * *
Long Reef, Lennonville, one of Bewick,
Moreing and Oo.’s numerous daisies, which has migrated
three times, has closed its battery for six months—no
more stone in sight. Returns have gradually been tailing
off to an unpayable point. Six months ago shares were
quoted in neighborhood at 15s. ; now 35., and dear at that.
* * * *
Teams are said to be competing successfully
with the railways between Sydney and Burraga (188
miles). It seems a strange state of things, but the man
who uses the railway has to pay interest on money
borrowed for construction purposes, and the man who
uses the road hasn’t. He only pays cost of haulage. If
there was only cost of haulage to be paid on the railways
freights might be cut down one-half.
• • • *
The Deddickers are at work again upon the
Gippsland sympathies of the Vic. Govt. The syndicate,
which originally talked vaguely of a £300,000 undertaking,
has now boiled its proposals down to something more
definite and less satisfactory. Practically the syndicate
stipulates for the grant of 1000 acres at Mount Deddick at
a yearly rental of 11s. per acre, also the free grant of all
the Crown land required for a railway thereto, and Govt,
assistance towards obtaining other land on the line of rail-
way which is privately owned or let on lease, and in return
for all this it undertakes to expend £25,000 during the
next 20 years. As for the rest of the agreement it seems
to mean that the syndicate will construct some 90 miles
of standard gauge railway from Bairnsdale or Cunning-
hame to Mt. Deddick, provided that it floats its co. and
discovers the alleged rich mineral deposits in that much-
paragraphed district. But inasmuch as the railway
operations will presumably start from the precious
Mount, they are not likely to go very far before the
mineral question is decided one way or other. Should the
line come to pass, the Govt, will have the right to buy it
at cost price plus 5 per cent, interest within 12 years,
and if this means that the “land required for the railway”
is part and parcel with the constructed line, the possi-
bilities of the syndicate’s land-grab are confined to the
1000 acres at Mt. Deddick. And when—if ever—does the
11s. per acre lease expire ?
At Poverty Point.
A good deal of nonsense has been written about the
late “Billy” Emerson, “the greatest of all minstrel
players,” who seems to have gone rapidly to the dogs
after returning to America from his last Australian trip.
Emerson, although a graceful step-dancer in a very quiet
sort of way, and a pretty tenor singer, was never a
specially amusing “ turn.” His voice had a strain of
melancholy which suited maudlin ballads of the “ Little
Empty Cradle ” order, but cast a funereal gloom over his
addresses to “ Pretty Jane.” One remembers Emerson
for his dispiriting influence. His most successful item
the last time he visited Australia—a good many years ago
—was a song called “Could I only back the Winner,”
which Lance Lenton wrote for him. The doggerel ditty
had a hopeful sentiment, but poor Billy’s musical voice
converted it into a song of despair. One felt that the
weary minstrel was drifting towards impecuniosity and
the luck would never turn. It didn’t turn.
Lydia Yeamans-Titus, now contributing the star-turn
at Sydney Tivoli, has a voice good enough for opera and
an acting ability good enough for comedy, but she
probably finds, like many other clever people, that “ the
halls” bring in more money than “ legitimate work.”
An Australian by birth, her chief successes seem to have
been scored in America. The lady with the long name is
accompanied by a magnificent husband, with an impas-
sive face, imposing mannahs and noble clothes—altogether
a man of ideally-ducal aspect.
Salerno, now at Sydney Palace, allows the audience to
test the billiard balls which he balances between cues ;
and they are real ones. Nevertheless the feat strikes
many to be one of “magic” rather than balance ; it
seems incredible that three ivory spheres could be
balanced and then made to support a cue.
The “ Message from Mars ” co., much reconstructed
since Sydney and Melbourne saw the pretty little show,
has been doing great business in the West. Judging from
Perth notices Hawtrey has found a satisfactory substitute
for Herbert Ross. One Gregor Macmahon, who
graduated with the Brough co. in a minor capacity, is
said to play the part of Gilbert Parker a lot better than
was expected.
“‘Flyman” : Amy Castles on the concert platform and
Amy Castles in the street are two different personages.
In the street, Amy is much shorter than she appears to
be when singing to an audience. If it were not for her
expressive dark eyes her face would be lacking in anima-
tion ; that she is doubly attractive beneath the gaslight
indicates that she has the proper artistic temperament,
which is only roused under certain conditions. There is
a rumbling note of sadness in the Castles boom. Amy
Castles is still a mere girl, and she is getting the wild
adulation of the crowd before she has w'orked for it.
Her sisters, cousins and aunts are all elated, but the man
who got up at 6 a.m. to get his ticket to see a much-
boomed singer is wondering now why he did it.
Rickards has put vim into the net-ball game at Melb.
Opera House by offering a prize of £lO to the team
scoring most goals during the season ; and how the girls
do roll into the contest! They bump one another on the
floor with all their might, hair is pulled down and
blouses are torn in the rousing scuffles. Considering
that football scrimmages, not half so rough, frequently
end in a male player being left standing “ mid nodings
on," the chances are that, ere the net-ball season is over,
some damsel will find herself before the audience in a
condition that will make Madame Lotty’s subsequent act a
tame and unrealistic affair.
R. Needham, the baritone of the Amy Castles co. (he
didn’t appear fit the Melb. concerts) is a Maorilander who
gave up a promising billet in a Melbourne business house
when the present Engagement offered. Boss Williamson
heard him for the first time at Bendigo and was taken by
his singing, whilst Dani greeted the new-comer—in
English—and told him he had “ lofely voice.” A plump
tenor who is going strong can afford to be generous to an
unknown baritone. -Needham will pick up popularity as
he goes along.

At Katoomba (3,300 ft. above sea-level
and 2J hours from Sydney by rail) a private
hospital on a small scale has been started by two
trained nurses. As excellent medical care is
locally available the little institution should
prosper.


WINCHESTER
MODEL 1900 SINGLE-SHOT RIFLE
m
HIS rifle sells at a price within reach of almost every-
body’s pocketbook, but it is made with the same care and
exactness as Winchester rifles that cost five times as
much. The name “Winchester” on this rifle is a
guarantee in itself of its quality. The Model 1900 is a
hammerless take-down, and can be taken apart easily
and quickly. It is made with an 18-inch round barrel,
fitted with open front and rear sights. It shoots .22 Short and .22
Long Rim-Fire cartridges, and Bullet Breech caps. These cartridges cost
so little that one can afford to shoot as many times as he likes as often
as he likes. Some makes of .22 Caliber rifles are a few pence cheaper,
but they are a great deal cheaper in quality than they are in price.
We don’t try to see how cheap we can make this rifle, but how good.

TWENTY-EIGHTH REPORT,
Presented to the Shareholders at the Ordinary General Meeting: on 30th April, 1002.
THE DIRECTORS have pleasure in submitting the annexed Balance-sheet and Statement of Accounts for the
Half-year ending 31st March, duly audited and certified.
The net profits (including £6809 9s. lid. brought from last half-year) amount to .. £13,869 17 11
Which it is proposed to allocate as follows, viz. :
To payment of a dividend at the rate of 5 per cent, per annum .. . .£3,750 0 0
To transfer to the reserve fund (making it £22,500) ... 7,500 0 0
£11,250 0 0
And to carry forward the balance £2,619 17 11
. During the half-year Mr. Charles Smith, one of the original Directors, having felt it necessary to retire, owing to
ill-health, the Board accepted his resignation with much regret. The vacancy thus created has been filled by the
appointment of the Honorable Sir Samuel Gillott, M.L.A., and the Directors are pleased to be able to congratulate
Shareholders on the acquisition of a gentleman so well qualified for the position.
The Directors point with pleasure to the continued progress of the Bank, which can scarcely fail to be regarded
as satisfactory by a ! l interested in the institution, and they trust that Shareholders will continue to show their
appreciation in a practical fashion, by bringing and influencing business to the Bank wherever possible.
By order of the Board.
H. T. WILSON, Manager.

Or its tones can be modulated to the soft, sweet tones of the German Zither.
In addition to its wonderful tone quality, the Harp-Zither has a great many
advantages over all other Zithers. It is the only Zither which may be played
while holding vertically like the Harp, or it may be laid upon a table, as is
necessary with the ordinary Zither.
Observe the diagonally crossed strings, almost the same as in a piano, the
melody strings passing over the chord strings. By means of this improvement
in construction the similarity in tone and volume of the piano is produced.
Beautiful in Design, Grand, Resonant Tone, Perfection in Every Point,
and it is the easiest to learn to play of any musical instrument in existence.
A child can play it almost at sight. The reason anyone can play this instru*
ment on first trial, even though the person may know absolutely nothing
about music, or may not have an ear for music, is this : Each string is num-
bered, as is each note in the music, so all one has to do to render the most
difficult selections is to strike the strings as indicated by the numbers; hence
we guarantee that anyone able to read figures can learn to play.
The Harp-Zither is built on the lines of the large harp which sells at
£2O and upwards, and to the astonishment of all the Harp-Zither has the
louder tone of the two ; in fact, its tone is similar to that of a piano.
PF" SATISFACTION GUARANTEED -»B
As a parlor ornament, the instrument, with its classical outlines, is
unique. For the serenade, the musicale, or any class of entertainment, the
Harp-Zither excels all other instruments of its class. Its Deep, sympathetic
Tones Penetrate even those Insensible to the Charms of Music.

HERR RASSMUSSEN (HANS PETER RASMUSSEN),
The Celebrated Danish Botanist,
Proprietor and Discoverer of the World-Famed Alfaline Herbal Remedies,
which have been the means of effecting the most marvellous Cures for Blood,
Skin and Nervous Diseases ever heard of in Australia.
Herr Rassmussen’s Australian Career:
It is about Twenty Years since Herb
Rassmussen introduced his Herbal Remedies
to the Australian Public. At that time
he was only known to the public as a Botanist
pure and simple, having devoted almost his
whole life to the study of that most useful
and interesting branch of science. It how-
ever, soon dawned on Herb Rassmussen that
immense virtues existed in many plants which
were entirely unknown to medical men, and
that it was his duty towards his fellow man
to bring these new discovered Herbal Remedies
within the means of suffering humanity.
Herb Rassmussen at once set to work,
and his entire energy was directed towards
extracting the medical properties from all
kinds of plants, and putting them up in the
most suitable and practical form, according
to the nature of the complaints which they
had been found a cure for.
Pills and Powders were found to be the
most practical form to administer these new
Herbal Remedies.
It is unnecessary to particularise here the
immense success Herb Rassmussen met with
in his venture twenty years ago ; suffice it to
say that in less than twelve months the
great and wonderful cures brought about by
his Alfaline Herbal Remedies were known
throughout Australia, New Zealand, and many
other parts of the world. Testimonials and
Honors arrived from all parts. Gold Medals
from Paris and other places were awarded, and
Herr R ass muss ion’s name has been a house-
hold word ever since. (Sydney JPress.J

Percy Rampage, or Ramage, the notorious
Vic. prisoner, is now in Pentridge again, where a
special labor-yard and a special cell and special
warders are detailed for him. He is the most
celebrated kicker of his day. The first sentence
he served was for kicking a cab-horse to death.
He pleaded that the ‘ ‘ bloomin’ ’orse ” started on
him first.
“Kakapo ” uttereth a groan :
Gore (Maoriland) is populated principally by Sandy and
Mac, who wear oatmeal in their speech and thistles in
their whiskers. It is represented in Parliament by
the M‘Nab. The Mayor is M‘Gibbon. Everybody else
is M‘Something or other. Nearly all the women are
called Agnes, and almost everybody goes to bed at nine.
As a young man Ketchener ran away from
Woolwich to enlist under General Chanzy in
1870, and he fought bravely in the French Army
of the Loire right through the campaign. When
the war was over he came back to England, and
it was only by very powerful influence that he was
allowed to resume his military studies.

TO THE MEMBERS OF THE AUSTRALIAN MUTUAL
PROVIDENT SOCIETY.
Ladies and Gentlemen.
In accordance with the provisions of the By-laws,
it becomes necessary for us to retire from the Board at
the approaching annual meeting. We are, however,
eligible for re-election, and we now offer our services.
We are encouraged to hope that the success which has
attended the operations of the Society under its present
administration will satisfy you, and we respectfully
request that you will signify your approval of such
administration by re-electing us to a position which we
highly prize, and in which we think we can be of further
service to you.
We remain, Ladies and Gentlemen,
Yours faithfully,
THOMAS LITTLEJOHN.

THE DR. McLAUGHLIN COMPANY, Sydney.
Dear Sirs,—lt gives me the greatest of pleasure to state,
in answer to your letter, that the Electric Belt I got from
you has done my back a world of good. The pains in my
back are not nearly so bad ; also, my general health is much
improved. I have been wearing the Belt a month now, and
expect to be completely cured in two more months.—l
remain, yours gratefully,
HENRY HALSEY.

and troubles ?
We have devoted many years to the development of vigor m both men and
women. We’ve made a great success. Our methods are approved by the greatest doctors,
and copied. . „ , ... , ,
We have proven that electricity is the basis of all animal life, and that weak men,
dyspeptics, rheumatics, sufferers from inertia, weak kidneys, pains in the back, head,

weeks, and cures every case where specially recommended.
The weak man does not live who cannot be made a better man by wearing our
Electric Belt. None of us are perfect, and anything that will add more to such vitality

Epitaph satirically said to have been chosen
by millionaire Carnegie for his tombstone
Here lies a man who knew how to get round him a
great many men much cleverer than himself.
Premier Barton stated in the press last
Tuesday that when he goes to England to be
button-holed and earwigged by the astute
Chamberlain he “ will not take any important step
of policy which will commit the Federal Parlia-
ment without first consulting that Parliament,”
etc. This would be good enough if it came from a
man whose assurance could be relied upon, but
Barton gave his equally solemn promise that
he wouldn’t commit the Commonwealth to
the despatch of a Federal contingent without
consulting Parliament, and his word on that occa-
sion proved to be less negotiable than an abori-
ginal’s promissory note, and a great deal less
accurate than Schalk Burger’s war despatches.
Now, having proved that his word is not binding
on him when Chamberlain invites him to break it,
Barton offers the broken object again as security
on a most vital point. Australia wants something
better than that.
Sydney D.T. gets from Maoriland a flat
contradiction of Premier Seddon’s disavowal of
his disgustingly blood-thirsty speech at Papawai:
His speech on that occasion was taken by two of the
most competent reporters in the colony, whose notes
agree on every point. The speech was published in every
paper in the colony, and was the subject of many con-
demnatory editorials and deprecatory resolutions by
ministers’ and other associations. The accuracy of the
report was never even questioned by Mr. Seddon in
subsequent speeches.
But who on earth believed Seddon’s obvious
falsehood? He spoke after dinner at Papawai
and, when the dinner is in, the truth of a man’s
nature comes out. Now, in more sober and more
politic mood, Mr. Seddon wants to disclaim senti-
ments unsuitable even for an ex-pugilist, and he
disclaims them with a falsehood. Well, The
Bulletin welcomes even the lie; it shows some
shame.

When Kossuth, the Hungarian patriot,
visited England, he was treated by all classes
like a prince, the populace sometimes taking
the horses out of his hired carriage and draw-
ing the vehicle themselves. This was most
irritating to the Austrian Government. Also, the
English lost no opportunity of showing their sen-
timental sympathy with Poland in her hopeless
struggles against Russia, and with the rebel,
Garibaldi. But the same people got their hair
off when a little ordinary civility was shown by
America, France, and Germany to Kruger’s peace-
envoys.
“X.” moralises :
A returned soldier applied, recently, in Melbourne, for
a “ Bottle O! ” license. Beak Panton, in a voice choking
with emotion, patriotism and flabbergastedness, granted
the application, and hoped the hero would soon be able to
get into a better position, which, considering that many
returned cohentingenters cannot get a position at all,
sounds somewhat like irony. I wonder how the soldier-
slobberers of Melbourne—say, for instance, Purves, Madden
or Pitzgibbon—would act if they found said “ Bottle O ! ”
in their back premises. Would they enthuse over the
hero who fought for the Hempire, and bring him in as a
brother to the whisky and roast goose ? Or would they
order an extra-sharp look-out to be kept on him, owing to
the experience he must have gained in Boer back-yards ?

Plumptoniana.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
Alfred Plumpton, news of whose recent death
in London is cabled, was a man of many sides
—like and unlike Gladstone. The stoppage of
his pendulum makes a silence, through which
curious memories arise—some only to be greeted
with Mephistophelean sniggers in Melbourne clubs
and coteries, others quotable. _
Alfred was the best musical critic, one of the
best musicians, and the hardest-working con-
ductor in Australia. A fearful and wonderful
worker in every way and from early days, his face
bewrayed him. A light thatch of hair on a skull
rather coned than domed; weak eyes behind
heavily-rimmed, gold spectacles ; anaemic com-
plexion, and spare figure. His time of best pro-
mise was when Tasca gave him her hand, for
Carlotta Tasca could write sweet verses, and
Alfred knew all about wedding word and note;
but of that promise there was not much fulfilment.
Poor Tasca !
Alfred in those days conducted the best choir in
Melbourne. He had his own methods. An ora-
torio chorus was once in rehearsal, and he tried for
an hour to get out some decent interpretation of
its sentiment. It was quite in vain till, quitting
all musical terms and usual methods, he flung
down the baton, spectacles, and score on the floor
together, and asked in the name of the Trinity
how they imagined their row was anything like the
voice of the people crying in the —ahem ! wilder-
ness. When at his work, that cry, as any other
great sentiment or passion, possessed him utterly.
When work was over it was sometimes otherwise.
Not always undesirably so.
There are still a few in Melbourne who remem-
ber nights or mornings when Alfred, with old
Zerbini and Rhymer, who played the big fiddle as
few others could, till spiritualism (not spirits)
bedevilled him, and Keiley of The Argus, who
knew all the literature of music and could have
talked down even Dr. Samuel Johnson, would sit
in a ring. Sometimes the piano or violin
would show what somebody really meant, and he
who sat on ihe outskirts of the ring grew wiser.
Now the last part of the ring is gone, and with
them good old Julius Herz and David Lee, who
were hardly of the same class, yet in their way
good. And they did queer things in their lives.
Four of them—Plumpton, Keiley, Zerbini, and
David Lee —sat about a table at 2 am., and grew
hot in argument over the authorship of a “Dead
March” ascribed to Beethoven. “We’ll go
across,” said David Lee, who had the key of the
house —that house being the Town Hall right
opposite. Two candles were borrowed, the
watchman opened the doors, and in the vasty
space, feebly illuminated, the tattered old score
was spread on the keyboard of the great organ,
and unmistakeable Beethoven pealed out. The
thunder of the thing, the incongruous weirdness
of all the surroundings, come back strangely now
when we know that the four of them are in their
graves.
And how many more with them, good men of
the same set who made things too hot, and other-
wise went off through an incomprehensible
ordination of affairs —“ Augur ” Chapman, of the
Australasian; “TheVag.”; Garnett, that frail
but lovable brother of the old chief of the British
Museum; old Grattan Riggs—but why continue
the count? It is doubtful if those who linger
have the best of it.
Alfred Plumpton had a Devil of audacity, a
mine of resource in him, which nothing could
subdue or exhaust. He picked up (as the tall
spire of a city church falls over a row of houses,
and hardly in benediction) a ghoulish, lemur-like
shadow, but it could not smother him. He had
bailiffs at his heels for decades, but they could not
spoil either his work or play.
TTis last big work in Australia was in the con-
ducting, actually the making, of “Paul Jones,”
when Miss Nellie Stewart for a while took the
responsibilities of management. There were two
ends to that season. One was high festival when
the business was practically over. It was mild.
One o’clock, the stage all ablaze beyond the empty
auditorium ; Nellie, queen of her court of princi-
pals, chorus and ballet. Law, medicine, com-
merce and finance, just as well represented as
letters and art; champagne fizzing, chicken-and-
turkey and pate-de-foie-gras and caviare vanish-
ing. Big Dr. Wilmot hardly needing a chair, so
easily he towered over all the others, proposing
healths. Mirth it was, hearty and free and with-
out much madness. Two o’clock and Alfred
orating, insisting everywhere and in everything
on the “ same old graft. ” Three, and everybody
talking, hats and ties making open proclamations
in the light that had no mercy. Four, and the
stage thin, but all the doors open and half-a-
hundred cabs outside. Enter then, uninvited,
bookmaker and ex-pugilist, a music-hall man
volunteering 1 ‘ Knocked ’Em in the Old Kent
Road,” just to wind up the show. Curtain then,
as was proper.
But Alfred had a benefit. He wanted the pro-
ceeds—so did some other people. All through
the rehearsals of the season there had been in-
terruptions, men approaching the conductor’s
desk with a furtive step and bits of blue paper
from Abraham, Isaac, and Jacob. “There’s
plenty of room on the file, put it with the rest.
And the baton did not miss a beat. But a busi-
ness man who let rooms and sold pianos insisted
on dividing, and couldn’t be put off with any
promises. “I must have half the ‘door ’or I will
put in a receiver and arrest you the minute the
curtain goes down.”
“What is to be, will be,” replied Alfred, and
the night arrived. The business man was at the
gate, the writ of attachment in the bailiff’s pocket.
But, when the curtain rang up another man ap-
peared at the conductor’s desk. The beneficiare
was on the high seas, homeward-bound with the
balance of £l5O (for which he had sold the house a
month before) safe under his belt.
They wanted him in London. He started work
within a week of his arrival and kept right on till
a day or two back. The Old Fellow, who Can’t be
Put Off, called then, and Alfred passed in his
checks, doubtless just as composedly as he used to
pay out at hazard.
F.
A carious action was lately brought against a
Wanganui (M.L.) shopkeeper under the Photograph
Copyright Act. A railway-clerk contracted to
represent the tradesman at a poster-ball in the
character of “Monkey Brand Soap.” He was
photo’d; and subsequently the shopkeeper had the
beauteous picture reproduced in the press as an
advt. Life was thus made a burden to the poor
clerk and hence the action. Judge Kettle, how-
ever, ruled that the disguised picture wasn t a
proper “ portrait ” of prosecutor, as defined by the
Act, and dismissed the case.
“Getting Up”: An Idyll of
Dairy Farming.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
I’m staying as a visitor at an Illawarra dairy
farm. The “cottage” is built of slabs —ventila-
tion, consequently, excellent. The interior is
canvas-partitioned into queer-shapen rooms ; the
minutest sound flies round the whole building.
The girls put in last night at Fitzgeralds’ Circus ;
I heard them come in at one, so they’ve only had
three hours’ sleep. So there is a hoarse noise of
throat-clearing, and thump! thump I towards 4
o’clock.
“Bless me sowl an’ body! Past 4 o’clock!
Hey, girls! Mary-Ann, Selina, Elizabeth, Kate!
git up ! The sun’s risin’ over the ’ills. Come on,
now, no dodgin’! ”
Audible yawns and sighs from various rooms.
“Eh—(ah-h) —wasser matter ?” followed by idiotic
laughter from one dreaming of the clown.
Ten minutes later. The silence is painful. A
fierce grunt from the old man, who has turned in
for five minutes more. “ What ! ” in stentorian
tones, “not up yet? Confound the girls ! Mary-
Ann ! Selina ! ELIZABETH, KATE ! ”
Chorus of “ Orlright, father,” (yawns) “we’re
gittin’ up ! ”
“ Well, git up, then, confound ye ! D’yer want
to miss the milk-train ?” The old man turns in
again.
Fifteen minutes later. Silence deeper than
ever. Noise as of a body flinging out of bed. He
is wild now. This is unprecedented. Then fol-
lows such a stream of lurid imprecation and
oblique blessing on the heads of those devoted
girls as fairly frightens them. I am lost in
admiration at the vast resources of his vocabulary.
Hurried sounds of dressing are heard—boots
bumping, water splashing, etc. —followed by
clumping footsteps towards the back door.
The boss is up himself now. I hear him at the
men’s room outside. They had finished up the
night with a howling spree. Hence, instead of
having the cows yarded, they are snoring in
delightfully regular alternation. Bill’s is a high
baritone snore ; Dan’s a sepulchral bass rivalling
Fob’s deepest efforts. Alternated they are un-
speakable ; in unison they are like the noise of a
trombone played by a grampus.
“Dan ! Bill ! ” —sharply. The snoring deepens.
It is in unison now. One has turned over.
“Dan !—blast—Bill! —curse— Dan Bill ! Yer
goin’ to lay in all the blanky day ? Any one ’d
think yer was gittin’ a thousand a year,” sarcas-
tically. (Just bore, Bill, dreaming of the goat-
and-monkey turn, the night before, ejaculates
encouragingly : “Go it, ole feller, keep it up ! ”)
“ Keep it —gurroo-o ! ” He is almost speechless.
“Where’s my —flamin’—(gasp)—whip? I’ll cut
the blessed tongue —(gulp)—outer yer, yer pair o*
55
The rest is a sort of inarticulate gurgle. His
rage has got into his throat. It next flies to his
head, which promptly becomes a battering-ram,
directed at the door.
Commotion heard in the room. They’ve never
known the old man as bad as this before. Slipping
on shirt and pants, they await their chance.
“ Now ! ” But, just as they open the door, he is
in the act of hurling himself against it. He
strikes nothing, and brings up against a broken
bedpost, causing a rush of air that fairly whistles.
The men dodge the water-jug and two boots,
and flee for the paddock. Their flight is accele-
rated by various missiles. The old man hurls
himself after the missiles. Despairing of further
sleep, I followed the old man.
And this is one way of “getting-up,” on an
Illawarra dairy-farm.
