THE GHOST-SHARK.
A Sydneyite lately wrote to an Italian
convent —which possesses the secret of a reputed
cure for rheumatism —to secure the sole Aus-
tralasian agency, and promised if this were given
him to advertise the nostrum well. He got this
in reply, addressed to “Sir Jones ” :
Sir, —I reply directly to your request with send to you
the prospects that you have kindly asked to me. We are
very grateful to you for your kindness in will propagate
the cure of our powders ; therefore when you will ask
them I shall send as soon as possible also for more years
of cure. The cost of one years of powders is frs 50 more
frs : 10 for expenses of carriage, which they are same also
for more years of cure ; but it is impossible to sell you
our powders empower. We have had many request from
the best pharmacies and Personages of all contries but
we cannot give this privilege to anybody because we are
forbidden to do so by a article of our statutes.— Believe
us to remain, Sir, most cordially yours truly,
Madame Marie Christine Carobbi,
Pharmacienne du Convent des B6n6dictines, Toscane
Italie.
“Investigatrix,” re the “selfishness” of
women as commented on by a person in authority :
Many women who, judging by results, take “pre-
cautions ” against a large family, are, to Lhose children
they possess, devoted and self-sacrificing mothers. And
there are wheels within wheels. The ordinary husband
does not, theoretically, object to a large family, but he
does object—and at times very unpleasantly—to the cur-
tailed domestic comfort and increased expenditure it
necessitates. And the woman—never very logical where
her feelings are keen—often fails to appreciate this fine
line of distinction, and acts accordingly. The most
gentlemanly husband, with a limited income, invariably
persists, in a resigned and gentlemanly way, in regarding
his wife as the culpable factor in the size of the family.
Also the ultra-fastidiousness of an advanced civilisat ion
has something to answer for—the etiquette of rearing
children isn’t what it used to be. The type of woman, who,
20 years ago, was content to sit down placidly to sew while
the children made dirt-pies in the sun, must now, unless
she would lose caste in the suburban terrace, change their
clothes, curl her own front hair, and put forth the after-
noon tea-set. That these inferences are not wholly incor-
rect is borne out by the fact that in the Real Bush, where
the cost of actual living is small, and manners primitive,
in spite of drought, water-famine and mortgaged home-
steads, the babies still continue to arrive with some
regularity.

water, or bread and milk.
For dinner: Cats’ meat,
or raw beef steak.
For five o’clock tea :
Milk.
For supper : Rice and
fish.
The starvation dietary scale of the concentration
camps, as officially set forth by the British
Government, is about the clearest explanation
yet given of the appalling mortality in these
hunger-holes.

PEARS
Autumn Verses.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
Leaden olouds that fold and fold }
Yellow leaves all sighing !
Oh ! the world seems very old,
For the Autumn’s dying.
Grey the sky is—bleak and grey—
And the gaunt trees swaying,
Seem in undertones to say,—■
Death is past delaying.
Withered bark, all spiritless,
Swings a-moan ere falling ;
Oh, the young gums’ nakedness !
Oh, the ourlews calling 1
I rom the eaves faint twitt’rings oome
As a swoop-hawk passes ;
But the song-birds all are dumb ;
Dead are all the grasses.
Gloom is on the nearer hills,
Not a leaf’s shade ohanges ;
Drought has found the mountain-rills,
Darkness smites the ranges.
Death seems ruling all the land;
Oh, the sere leaves sighing !
Life, the King, at his command,
Dying ! dying ! dying !
MILKY WHITE
A Royal Theatre-Party.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
“ Why, look ! There they come ! ”
No outriders. No display. Two carriages dash
past, down a dark lane at the back of the Adelphi.
A few bystanders press forward. A couple of
police move them back. We are bound for the
theatre. They let us pass. A man’s voice,
woolly with drink, cries: “Come back; I’m as
equal as you ! ”
We wait in the gay, crowded lobby a minute.
These tall women, with heads held high and
shoulders well thrown back, with hair dressed
loosely on top, devoid of curl or crimp, are
Americans. Englishwomen don’t carry them-
selves that way. Grace and deportment are the
special privileges of Parisians and Americans in
London. Beside them, Englishwomen cut poor
figures. Royalty gets in a back-stairs way.
When we enter at last, it is there. The house has
just finished clapping ; the Queen ceases bowing ;
the King also.
Our seats are opposite their box. The play is
“Arizona.” It's charming, tender, simple,
funny, dramatic, and exquisitely acted. It
is our luck to have seen it before—to know it
almost by heart. We are free to turn our
eyes from it and give all our attention to those
four figures in the box nearest the stage. First,
the Queen ; then the King ; then Princess Charles
of Denmark ; finally, Princess Victoria, nearest
the stage. In the adjoining box are three
members of the King’s suite, and one lady-in-
waiting to the Queen —»• very plain and unfashion-
able lady, be it said.
Naturally, necessarily, the Queen first. Is she
not the one picturesque figure of the whole Royal
menage ? And so this, then, is the Queen, this
narrow, slender, small-faced smiling lady in black.
An impression stamps itself vividly on the
brain. She is “made up.” The eye detects in-
stantly something incongruous—something arti-
ficial. After awhile the impression is softened.
The artificial becomes less evident. The first
vision of powder, paint, and false hair yields a
little. She seems to grow younger. But there
always remains in the back of the brain that first
impression—the painted lips, the wig, the utter
absence of youth—of real, genuine youth. How
could it be there, indeed ? But we have been led
to expect it. ‘ ‘ The Queen looks younger than her
daughters,” they say. Not at first glance, not till
the eye accustoms itself to the unnatural conditions
of her face, till it grows blind to enamel and car-
mine, and is no longer struck with the falseness of
her hair—by then we are able to discover that
the Queen is, indeed, in a way, a lovely woman.
Her face is cut like a cameo. As she turns it
upwards or sideways, the contour is always beau-
tiful. The nose is chiselled, the chin is neither
full nor bony. Her mouth has no expression
whatever except a little, masked smile. She bites
her lips continually. She looks gentle, vapid,
expressionless. Very slight about the neck and
shoulder. She sits erect, with a delicate, easy, and
yet pronounced erectness. She is gowned in
black lace, has long transparent sleeves, and is
not in the least decolletee. Her frock is high-
necked. The transparent collar ends in a high
band of black velvet. Her gloves are pale grey
suede, and she wears a diamond necklace and a
little diamond bird in the middle of her fringe,
but far back. The hair, a light nut-brown, not
golden, not auburn. It cleverly avoids the
error of being false in either of these two shades,
commonly consecrated to the wig or the dye. It
is as natural as possible. Only for one’s first im-
pression it is impossible, after a time, to believe it
isn’t genuine. Curled all over the top, and
brought low down on the forehead in a curled
fringe, then rolled and coiled all up the back, the
effect is stiff and devoid of character. That
heavy fringe would rob any face of character.
A sweet, fragile woman, with a hint of endur-
ance and patience about her mouth, a fleeting ex-
pression of wistfulness, of shrinking, in her eyes ; a
graceful woman, incapable of a hard word, or a
harsh thought. It struck me, as I looked, how
sweet she would be tending invalids, how gentle,
how patient.
On her left sits the King. Oh ! a very fat man
indeed is the King, although he is not tall. He
doesn’t wear a crown. He is in evening-dress with
a white “button-hole.” The King has a wider face
than his portraits suggest, and his eyes are opened
wider ; he looks a most kindly, happy, contented,
merry, yet dignified old gentleman —every inch,
“Papa.”
After the first and second acts he rises.
He never says a word to the Queen, or his
daughters. He doesn’t tell them lie is going. He
just rises and quietly passes out of the box, and is
away all that interval and all the next, does not
return till the curtain has gone up. Then he
c unes in, opening the door gently. Entering the
box gently, moving with a cat-like, slow, fat
gentleman's grace. He immediately fixes his
g asses on the prettiest girl on the stage. While
1 1 e 3 is away, the Queen sits in her corner, his vacant
cn lir beside her. Neither of the Princesses move
up to talk to her. They sit and talk in a lifeless
way to each other. They are both white, ansemic-
looking young women, in cloudy black, with
white feather-boas.
Now and then the Queen turns her slight head
a little, and speaks to them. She somehow seems
to be acting as she does so. She looks so lovely,
so wistful, biting her red thin lips, as if to force a
smile that wished, of itself, to wane. There is
something queer about this conversation. After
a time it strikes me that the two white-faced
Princesses soarcely turn their heads to answer
that smiling, doll-like figure in the corner. She
speaks every now and then—a little quick, light
volley of words. Why do they not answer, come
oloser, and talk to her? Because that smiling,
graoef ul woman is stone-deaf. She does not hear
what they say. Her conversation is not intended
to be answered. She talks simply that the
audience will not see her sitting deaf and dumb.
Is it not pathetic ? She will not use an ear-
trumpet. She prefers to throw her little say out
into the air, and smile, and play her part.
Princess Charles is the better-looking of the
royal daughters ; but even she is not pretty. All
three ladies seem remarkably spiritless. There is
no gaiety in that box. All the sweetness of the
Queen, all the affable contentedness of the King,
do not rid the little party of a look of dulness.
Of course, nobody comes in to speak to them.
The orchestra plays "Yankee Doodle.” The
King drums on his shirt-front with his white kid
fingers. Then his hand goes creeping up to his
throat. Just for a second it hovers there. His
expression never changes. If he is suffering he is
a brave man. His'face shows nothing, beyond
the worn look of his eyes. Worry and ennui are
strangers to that face. But the eyes, good-
humored, twinkling eyes, have their worn look all
the same.
Then his hand goes down again. He takes his
opera glasses; he beats time on the front of the
box with them. He hammers out several bars of
that American air, looking round the house all the
time. He is for ever looking round. He looks as
if he wants to see there lots of people that he
knows, as if he likes the act of bowing and smiling,
and seeing smiles from familiar faces shot at him.
As the house is chiefly American there are not
many of his friends there. It is kept a secret
beforehand, a visit from Royalty. The “ Manage-
ment ” lets it out as much as possible. But
London is in a wild state of chasing from one
theatre to another just now, trying to be where
the King is, and generally missing him. There is
no method in his theatre-going. The first play he
visited, as King, was "Mice and Men,” played
by Forbes Robertson and Gertrude Elliott. That
was two weeks ago. Since then he has seen
“ Mademoiselle Mars ” as Mrs. Langtry (the
Queen did not go with him there ; she went later,
however, with the Princess of Wales), “ The
Toreador,” “Kitty Grey,” “Frocks and Frills,”
“Sherlock Holmes,” “The Importance of Being
Earnest,” and “ The Country Girl.”
Here comes someone London is raving over.
She is called “ The Funniest Woman in the
World.” She is American, was a chorus-girl,
Elfie Fay, but attracted the attention of the
house by her funny tricks. Now she drives
crowded houses at the Empire wild with laughter
at her antics as a Sousa Girl. Her facial expres-
sions are too ridiculous for words. Her dancing
is the sheer essence of absurdity and cleverness.
She is one of those very, very rare beings—a
humorous woman. A real American girl, copper
hair parted in the middle, and a bright, cute face,
and a cloak—a wonderful cloak —all lace and fur,
coming to her feet in a long yellow train.
In the lower box sits someone we all know—
Mrs Brown-Potter. She is with a clergyman
and his wife, and a very pretty young girl. She
has grown much stouter since we saw her last in
Sydney. Her face is quite round. I prefer it
with its delicate look. She wears an enormous
black bow in her hair, so big as to look like a
black cap, and keeps on her heavy sable cloak all
the time. At present, she is much talked of in
London. She threw up her part in “ Ulysses ”at
the last moment. Some say Mrs. Tree objected
to the way Calypso embraced Ulysses—Mr. Tree.
The other story is that Stephen Phillips inter-
fered too much. That is more probable. He is a
great poet, this dark, clean-shaven man with a
face like a bushman’s—and there is no great
actress in England at present except, maybe,
Mrs. Kendall.
The play draws near the end. The Queen
manoeuvres a grey feathery wrap round her
shoulders. The King keeps his glasses on pretty
little Olive May, the ingenue actress. He did not.
go out before the last act, but sat in the box, look-
ing just a little bored, just as any other stout old
amiable gentleman might look before the last act,
tired of sitting among his family on the edge of
his chair. The end of the play is in view. The
audience is not strictly conventional. Several
people rise and hurry away. The King does not
kill them with a glance ; he doesn’t even notice.
He watches the stage to the end, then the royal
party rise all together. There is no more bowing,
no more clapping. Quietly the door is opened,
the King goes out alongside the Queen, the white-
faced Princesses with the heavy sheepish eyes
follow. There is no “God Save the King ” from
the orchestra, and in a minute they are gone.
Will they laugh and talk when they get away
into their carriage? Let us hope so. For the
King’s sake, and especially for the Queen’s —the
deaf, sweet, plaintive, painted Queen.
LOUISE MACK.
On 31st January last there were in bond
in Scotland nearly 110,000,000 gallons of proof
whisky!—a quantity about equal to a week’s
water-supply for such a city as Sydney. Apropos,
along the Broomielaw (Glasgow) there is a public-
house to every 18 voters!
Captain Grosstnann, inventor of the water-
walking shoe, recently tramped the surface of the
Danube from Linz to Vienna—over 200 miles as
the river goes—towing his wife in a small boat.
Wearing these water-shoes—which are 15ft. long
—the in ventor lias already saved 21 persons from
drowning. Religious myth will soon not have a
"miracle” left that science can’t parallel in hard
fact.
One Thaise, a desperate robber and mur-
derer, was guillotined, the other day, in New
Caledonia:—
The condemned displayed the utmost calmness and in-
difference, refusing the services of a minister of religion.
He slept well the night before his execution, and in the
morning, stating he was hungry, was supplied with a
breakfast of sardines, chicken, and wine and rum, making
a hearty meal. Whilst the barber was, as usual, shaving
the back of his neck, Thaise talked in a familiar strain,
and, addressing the executioner, said, “ It is not because
your business is to cut off heads that you should not
drink a glass of wine with me,” and thereupon they
drank together. On the scaffold he admitted killing the
man, but said he had not intended doing so. An im-
proved guillotine was used on the occasion. After the
knife ha,d done its work the executioner, before throwing
the head into the basket, seized it with both hands by the
ears, and asked, “Thaise, do you hear me?” Two or
three movements of the jaws followed, from which it was
inferred that what had been said had been heard.
How very French it really all is !
